The Name and the Named: Definition and Distinction
The author defines 'ism' and clarifies the distinction between name and named.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological definition of 'ism' and how it relates to 'musamma', including the resolution of the apparent disagreement about their identity.

Core Idea
The name is not the named unless by 'name' we mean the referent.

Before diving into theological arguments, we must clarify what we mean by 'name' ('ism') and 'named' ('musamma'). This might seem like a dry terminological issue, but it has real consequences for how we discuss the attributes of Allah. The author presents the correct definition and shows how two seemingly conflicting views can be reconciled.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice that the author addresses derivation, gives a definition, and then resolves a dispute.
2
Identify the two definitions of 'ism'
The author gives a theological definition and mentions a grammatical one. Find both and note the difference. The theological definition is 'that which indicates a named thing'.
The grammatical definition is a 'word that indicates a meaning in itself'.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The author says that by default, the name is different from the named, but if 'name' means the referent (the thing itself), then they are identical. This reconciles the two opinions. Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter?
This distinction avoids confusion when discussing whether Allah's names are His essence or not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاشتقاق عندهم من الأفعال، فقولُ بعض العلماء : ( وعند الكوفيين من الوسم بمعنى العلامة) فيه تسمح، ومعناه: ما دل على مسمى، وأما قولهم: (كلمةٌ دَلَّتْ على معنى في نفسها .... إلى آخره، فهو اصطلاح نحوي، وعُلمَ من التعريف المذكور أن الاسم غير المسمَّى ، وهو التحقيق؛ نعم إن أريد به المدلول، كان عين المسمى، وبهذا يُجمع بين القولين.
Derivation according to them is from verbs, so the saying of some scholars: 'And according to the Kufans from wasm meaning a mark' is lenient, and its meaning: that which indicates a named thing. As for their saying: 'A word that indicates a meaning in itself... etc.,' it is a grammatical convention, and it is known from the mentioned definition that the name is other than the named, and that is the verification. Yes, if what is meant by it is the referent, then it is identical to the named, and by this the two sayings are reconciled.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage defines 'ism' (name) and clarifies its relation to 'musamma' (named). The author first addresses the derivation of the word 'ism': according to grammarians it is derived from verbs, contrary to a view (attributed to some Kufans) that it comes from 'wasm' meaning a mark, which the author considers a lenient opinion. The definition of 'ism' in this context is 'that which indicates a named thing' (ما دل على مسمى). The author then distinguishes this from the grammatical definition (a word that indicates a meaning in itself), which is a technical term of grammar. From the theological definition, it is clear that the name is not the same as the named entity—this is the correct position. However, if by 'name' one intends the referent (المدلول) rather than the word, then the name is the same as the named. This reconciles the two apparently conflicting opinions about whether the name is the same as the named.

Key Terms
الاسم
المسمى
المدلول
الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name is always identical to the named.
Correction: The name (the word) is not the same as the named (the entity). They are identical only if by 'name' we mean the referent itself.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what reconciles the two opinions about whether the name is the same as the named?
A) The name is always the same as the named.
B) When 'name' means the referent, it is the same as the named.
C) The name is never the same as the named.
D) It depends on the grammatical school.

Key Takeaway
The name (word) is distinct from the named (entity), unless by 'name' we mean the referent itself, in which case they are identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the name 'Allah' in your prayers, do you ever feel the weight that the word itself is not the same as the One it points to? How does this awareness change the way you connect your tongue to your heart?
?
If the name is a mere indicator, then every time you say 'Bismillah', are you content with just the sound, or are you actively directing your mind to the reality behind the word? What will you do today to make your utterance a true pointer?
?
The author reconciles two views by clarifying intention. In a disagreement you have with a fellow Muslim, could it be that you are arguing at the level of words while meaning the same thing? Will you commit to seeking the intended meaning before insisting on your own wording?

The Definition of Hamd and Its Four Types
Author defines hamd linguistically and classifies its four divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic definition of hamd (praise) and its four divisions, and why it includes both eternal and temporal praise.

Core Idea
Hamd is verbal praise for voluntary good, covering both eternal and temporal acts.

Before delving into the theological content of the Jawharah, the commentator establishes the meaning of the opening phrase 'Al-hamdu lillah'. He provides a precise linguistic definition and shows how it encompasses all four types of praise. This lesson unpacks that definition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Note the structure: a quote from al-Nawawi, an explanation of the conjunction, then the definition and examples.
Pay attention to the technical terms like 'thana'' and 'natha''.
2
Understand the definition and examples
Focus on the linguistic definition: 'verbal praise for a voluntary good in the manner of veneration and magnification.' Note the two examples distinguishing between praise in response to a favor and not.
The definition intentionally includes both scenarios.
3
Identify the four types of hamd
The passage concludes by listing four types: 1) Allah's praise of Himself, 2) Allah's praise of His prophets/saints (both eternal), 3) our praise of Allah, 4) our praise of each other (both temporal). Reflect on how the definition covers all.
This classification shows the comprehensiveness of the term.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (الحَمْدُ اللهِ ... إلى آخره قال النووي رحمه الله تعالى : (يستحبُّ الحمدُ في ابتداء الكتب المصنفة، وكذا في ابتداء دروس المدرسين، وقراءة الطالبين بين يدي المعلمين، سواءٌ قرأ حديثاً أو فقهاً أو غيرَهُما ، وأحسنُ العبارات في ذلك : الحمد لله رب العالمين) انتهى (١). وإنما لم يأتِ بحرف العطف إشارة إلى أن كلا من البسملة والحمدلة محصل للمقصود في الابتداء، أو لاحتمال أن تكون إحداهما خبرية والأخرى إنشائية، والصحيحُ أنه لا يجوز عطفُ الإنشاء على الإخبار وعكسه. والحمدُ لغةٌ : الثناء بالكلام على الجميل الاختياري على جهة التبجيل والتعظيم، سواء كان في مقابلة نعمة أم لا، فمثال الأول ما إذا أكرمك زيد، فقلت: زيد كريم؛ فإنه في مقابلة نعمة؛ ومثال الثاني ما إذا وجدت زيداً يصلي صلاةً تامَّةٌ، فقلت: زيد رجلٌ صالح ؛ فإنه ليس في مقابلة نعمة . والثناء بتقديم المثلثة على النون : هو الإتيان بما يدلُّ على التعظيم، وقيل: هو الذكرُ بخير، وضدُّهُ : النثاء بتقديم النون على المثلثة. وإنما عبرنا بالكلام كما عبّرَ به بعضُ المحققين ليشمل التعريف حينئذ الحَمْدَ القديم؛ وهو حمد الله نفسه بنفسه، وحمده لأنبيائه وأوليائه وأصفيائه، والحمد الحادث ؛ وهو حمدنا الله تعالى، وحمد بعضنا البعض، فدخلَتْ أقسام الحمد الأربعة؛
His saying: 'Praise be to Allah ... etc.' Al-Nawawi, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'Praise is recommended at the beginning of authored books, and likewise at the beginning of teachers' lessons and students' recitations before teachers, whether one reads hadith, fiqh, or other than them. The best expressions for that are: al-hamdu lillahi rabbi al-alamin.' End (1). And he did not bring a conjunction to indicate that each of the basmalah and hamdalah achieves the intended purpose in the beginning, or because one of them might be declarative and the other performative, and the correct view is that it is not permissible to conjoin a performative to a declarative or vice versa. And hamd linguistically: verbal praise for a voluntary good in the manner of veneration and magnification, whether in response to a favor or not. An example of the first: if Zayd honors you and you say 'Zayd is generous' – it is in response to a favor. An example of the second: if you find Zayd performing a perfect prayer and you say 'Zayd is a righteous man' – it is not in response to a favor. And al-thana' (with tha' before nun) is: bringing what indicates magnification; and it is said: it is mentioning with good; its opposite is al-natha' (with nun before tha'). And we specified 'with speech' as some investigators did, so that the definition then includes the eternal hamd – which is Allah's praise of Himself, and His praise of His prophets, saints, and chosen ones – and the temporal hamd – which is our praise of Allah, and our praise of one another. Thus the four divisions of hamd are included.
Source pages p. 37

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'hamd' (praise). It begins by citing al-Nawawi's recommendation to begin books and lessons with praise, then explains why the basmalah and hamdalah are not joined by a conjunction: each independently suffices for the beginning, and they differ in grammatical mode (declarative vs. performative). The linguistic definition is then given: hamd is verbal praise (thana' bil-kalam) for a voluntary good act, expressed with veneration and magnification, whether or not it is in response to a favor. Two examples illustrate this. The author then clarifies the word 'thana'' (praise) and its opposite 'natha'' (blame). Finally, by including the word 'kalam' (speech) in the definition, the definition covers all four types of hamd: (1) eternal praise – Allah's praise of Himself, (2) eternal praise – Allah's praise of His prophets etc., (3) temporal praise – our praise of Allah, (4) temporal praise – our praise of each other. These four are the complete classification.

Key Terms
حمد
ثناء
جميل اختياري
حمد قديم
حمد حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think hamd (praise) is only said in response to a favor or blessing.
Correction: The definition explicitly includes praise not in response to a favor, as shown by the example of praising someone for their good character or actions without prior benefit.

Checkpoint
How many types of hamd are mentioned in the passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
Hamd is verbal praise for voluntary good, encompassing both eternal praise from Allah and temporal praise from creatures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise others, do you often only do so when they do something for you? The lesson teaches that hamd can be for any voluntary good. Reflect on a time you praised someone purely for their virtue, not because of a favor. How did it feel? Will you make an effort to praise more freely, especially in your relationships with family and friends?
?
Allah praises Himself eternally. How does this truth affect your understanding of your own need to praise Allah? Do you ever feel that your praise is insufficient or that you must only praise Allah when He grants you a specific favor? How can you shift to praising Him constantly for His inherent perfection?
?
The definition of hamd requires verbal expression. How often do you actually utter words of praise to Allah beyond the obligatory prayers? Consider setting a daily habit of verbalizing hamd for at least three things — what would you say today?

The Four Types of Hamd and the Inclusion of 'Bil-Lisan'
The author enumerates the four types of hamd and discusses the scope of the definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four categories of hamd (praise) based on the nature of the praiser and the praised, and how a potential objection regarding the definition's inclusion of eternal praise is resolved through figurative interpretation.

Core Idea
Praise has four types; the definition of hamd must include eternal praise.

This passage from the Jawharat al-Tawhid commentary classifies hamd into four types and addresses a subtle definitional issue. Some scholars restrict hamd to verbal praise, but the author shows how that would exclude divine praise. He resolves it by invoking a well-known figurative usage. Let's examine his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the classification and the critique that follows.
The passage has two main parts: an enumeration and a discussion.
2
Identify the four types of hamd
List the four combinations based on whether the praiser and the praised are eternal (qadim) or contingent (hadith). Understand each type.
These are all logically possible categories.
3
Understand the critique of 'bil-lisan'
The author objects to defining hamd as 'by the tongue' because it would exclude eternal praise. Then see how he solves this by interpreting 'lisan' as a metaphor for speech.
The resolution uses majaz mursal (synecdoche).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي : حمد قديم لقديم، وحمد قديم لحادث، وحمد حادث لقديم، وحمد حادث لحادث ; وأما تعبير بعضهم باللسان ، فيلزم عليه ألا يكون التعريف شاملاً للقديم، إلا أن يُراد باللسان الكلام على سبيل المجاز المرسل؛ من إطلاق السبب وهو وإرادة المسبَّبِ وهو الكلام ، ولا يرد أن التعاريف تُصانُ عن المجاز؛ لأن محل ذلك ما لم يكن المجاز مشهوراً كما هنا .
And they are: eternal praise for the eternal, eternal praise for the contingent, contingent praise for the eternal, and contingent praise for the contingent. As for the expression of some by 'tongue', then it necessitates that the definition does not encompass the eternal, unless by 'tongue' is meant speech by way of rhetorical synecdoche (majaz mursal): the mention of the cause intending the effect, which is speech. And it is not objected that definitions are protected from figurative language, because the place of that is when the figurative usage is not well-known, as it is here.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a classification and a methodological discussion. The author first lists the four possible types of hamd based on whether the praiser and the praised are eternal (qadim) or contingent (hadith). Then he critiques a definition that restricts hamd to 'by the tongue' (bil-lisan), arguing it would exclude the eternal (e.g., Allah's praise of Himself or of prophets). He resolves this by noting that 'lisan' can be interpreted as a metaphor for 'speech' (kalam) via majaz mursal, where the cause (the tongue) stands for the effect (speech). He further defends the use of figurative language in definitions, stating that the objection against figurative usage applies only when the figure is not well-known, and here it is common.

Key Terms
حمد (hamd)
قديم (qadim)
حادث (hadith)
لسان (lisan)
مجاز مرسل (majaz mursal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamd is only the verbal praise uttered by the tongue.
Correction: Hamd includes eternal praise (e.g., Allah's praise of Himself) which is not verbal, so the definition must be broad enough to include it.

Checkpoint
According to the text, why is it not a problem that the definition of hamd uses a figurative expression (majaz mursal)?
A) Because definitions are always figurative.
B) Because this figurative expression is well-known.
C) Because the definition is only for contingent beings.
D) Because the tongue is not a cause.

Key Takeaway
Hamd includes four types based on the eternal/contingent status of the praiser and praised, and the definition must accommodate all, including divine praise, which is possible through figurative interpretation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Alhamdulillah', do you limit your praise to mere words, or do you recognize that your entire existence, actions, and even your inner state are forms of praise? This lesson teaches that hamd extends beyond the tongue. How will you consciously embody praise through your daily actions and thoughts starting today?
?
The author defends using well-known figurative language in definitions. When you read the Quran's descriptions of Allah (e.g., 'Hand' or 'Throne'), do you reflexively take them literally, or do you pause to consider deeper, figurative interpretations understood by the early Muslims? How will you train yourself to approach such verses with humility and proper scholarship?

The Definition of Hamd and Its Distinction from Madh
Definition of hamd and distinction from madh

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of hamd as praise for a voluntary good act, how it differs from madh, and why the voluntary nature is attached to the cause of praise, not the attribute used.

Core Idea
Hamd is praise for voluntary good; madh is for involuntary qualities.

The text begins by clarifying the phrase 'upon the voluntary good' in the definition of hamd. It explains that the goodness of the act is based on the praiser's belief, even if the act is not legally good. Then it distinguishes hamd from madh by the voluntary nature of the praised attribute. Finally, it addresses a nuance: the voluntary condition applies to the cause of praise, not the attribute with which one praises.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without interruption, then read the English translation. Notice the key terms: حمد, مدح, اختياري, اضطراري.
The passage is dense; read slowly to capture the distinctions.
2
Identify the core definition
Find where the definition of hamd is given: praise for voluntary good. Note the exception: even if the good is only in the praiser's belief, not legally good.
This shows hamd is about subjective good actions.
3
Contrast with madh
Locate the contrast: praise for involuntary qualities is madh, not hamd. The example of praising a pearl illustrates this.
Madh can be for any good quality, voluntary or not.
4
Understand the restriction
Analyze the author's final point: 'voluntary' is a restriction on the mahmud 'alayhi (what is praised for), not on the mahmud bihi (what is praised with). Use the Zaid example to see how hamd can use involuntary attributes.
This resolves a potential confusion about the scope of hamd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا على الجميل (الاختياري) أي: لأجل الجميل الاختياري، ولو كان جميلاً في اعتقاد المحمود بزعم الحامد، وإن لم يكن جميلاً شرعاً؛ كنهب الأموال، وخرج بقيد (الاختياري) الاضطراري؛ فإن الثناء عليه يسمى مدحاً لا حمداً، تقول : مدحت اللؤلؤةً على حسنها، دون حمدتُها، وقال الزمخشري: الحمدُ والمدح أخوان بمعنى: أنهما مترادفان. والاختياري إنما هو قيد في المحمود عليه لا في المحمود به، فقد يكون المحمود عليه اختياريًّا، والمحمود به اضطراريًا، كما إذا أكرمك زيد ; فقلت : زيد حسن
And our statement 'upon the voluntary good' means: for the sake of the voluntary good, even if it is good in the belief of the praised according to the claim of the praiser, even if it is not good legally, like plundering wealth. And by the restriction 'voluntary' exits the involuntary; for praise of it is called madh, not hamd. You say: 'I praised (madhtu) the pearl for its beauty' but not 'hamadtuha'. And al-Zamakhshari said: 'al-hamd and al-madh are brothers' meaning they are synonymous. And the voluntary is only a restriction in what is praised for (mahmud 'alayhi), not in what is praised with (mahmud bihi). So it can be that what is praised for is voluntary, while what is praised with is involuntary, as when Zaid honors you; so you say: 'Zaid is good'.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage is a definition and clarification. The author explains the phrase 'upon the voluntary good' in the definition of hamd. He emphasizes that the goodness of the action is according to the praiser's belief, even if it is not legally good (e.g., plundering). The restriction 'voluntary' excludes praise of involuntary qualities, which is called madh, not hamd. He gives the example: one says 'madhtu al-lu'lu'ata' (I praised the pearl) for its beauty, but not 'hamadtuha'. He then cites al-Zamakhshari who claims hamd and madh are synonymous, but the author's own position distinguishes them. The key point is that 'voluntary' is a restriction on the mahmud 'alayhi (what is praised for), not on the mahmud bihi (what is praised with). This means the cause of praise must be a voluntary act, but the attribute used to praise can be involuntary. Example: if Zaid does a voluntary favor (honoring you), and you say 'Zaid is good' (an involuntary attribute), it is still hamd because the cause is voluntary.

Key Terms
حمد (hamd)
مدح (madh)
اختياري (ikhtiyari)
اضطراري (idrari)
محمود عليه (mahmud 'alayhi)
محمود به (mamdud bihi)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamd can only be for acts that are legally good. For example, one might think plundering wealth cannot be praised with hamd.
Correction: The author clarifies that hamd is based on the praiser's belief, so even legally invalid acts (like plundering) can be praised with hamd if the praiser considers them good.

Checkpoint
Why is the statement 'Zaid is good' (Zaid Hasan) considered hamd when said after Zaid honored you, even though 'good' describes an involuntary quality?
A) Because the praise is for the voluntary act of honoring, and 'good' is just the instrument.
B) Because 'good' is a voluntary attribute.
C) Because Zaid is good by nature.
D) Because the praiser uses madh not hamd.

Key Takeaway
Hamd is praise directed at a voluntary good act, even if the attribute used is involuntary, while madh is praise for involuntary qualities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah in your daily life—whether in prayer or in gratitude—are you praising Him for His voluntary acts of mercy and guidance, or are you merely appreciating His inherent majesty? How can you ensure your hamd is directed at what He has chosen to do for you?
?
Recall a time you praised a person for a trait they were born with, like intelligence or beauty. According to this lesson, was that hamd or madh? Does that distinction change how you view the sincerity of your praise?
?
The author notes that hamd can be for what the praiser believes is good, even if it is not legally good. Have you ever praised someone for an action you thought was good but later realized was wrong? How does this lesson call you to examine the foundation of your praise before you offer it?

The Qualifier 'Upon Glorification and Magnification'
The author explains the restriction 'upon glorification and magnification' excludes mockery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the qualifier 'upon glorification and magnification' is necessary in the definition of hamd and how it excludes mockery or derisive praise.

Core Idea
Genuine praise requires sincere glorification, not just the form.

In defining hamd, the author adds a crucial qualifier: praise must be 'upon the manner of glorification and magnification.' This prevents mere verbal praise from being considered hamd if it is actually intended as mockery. The angels' sarcastic remark to Abu Jahl in the Quran illustrates this exclusion perfectly.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author explains the two key terms (tabjīl and ta‘ẓīm) and their role in the definition.
The author states that 'tabjīl' and 'ta‘ẓīm' are synonyms, with 'ta‘ẓīm' added for clarification.
2
Identify the qualifier's function
Understand that the phrase 'upon the manner of glorification and magnification' is an explanatory annexation (iḍāfah li’l-bayān). It specifies that the praise must be accompanied by genuine reverence.
3
Examine the excluded case
Look at the example of the angels' statement to Abu Jahl. The author shows that even though the words 'You are the mighty, the noble' appear to be praise, they are actually mockery. This type of statement is excluded from hamd.
The author quotes al-Khazin's tafsir to confirm the sarcastic intent.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this qualifier is necessary. Without it, any statement that merely has the form of praise could be considered hamd, but true hamd requires sincere glorification of Allah.
The author concludes that the qualifier is clarifying, not adding a new condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولنا : ( على جهة التبجيل والتعظيم ) أي : على جهة هي التبجيل والتعظيم، فالإضافة للبيان، وعطف التعظيم على التبجيل للتفسير; وخرج بذلك ما إذا كان على جهة الاستهزاء والسخرية، كما في قول الملائكة لأبي جهل : ذُقْ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ الْعَزِيزُ الْكَرِيمُ أي: بزعمك عند قومك، وذلك أن أبا جهل - لعنه الله - كان يقول: أنا أعزُّ البوادي وأكرمهم، وعبارة الخازن ما نصه: (ذُقْ؛ أي: هذا العذاب، إنك أنت العزيز الكريم؛ أي: عند قومك بزعمك؛ وذلك أن أبا جهل - لعنه الله - كان يقول : أنا أعزُّ البوادي وأكرمُهم، فتقول خزنة النار له ذلك على طريق الاستخفاف والتوبيخ). وفي الحقيقة هذا خارج من أوَّلِ الأمر ; لأنه ليس ثناء إلا بحسب الصورة، فهذا القيد عند التحقيق للإيضاح.
And our statement 'upon the manner of glorification and magnfication' means: upon the manner that is glorification and magnfication, so the annexation is for clarification, and the conjunction of 'magnification' to 'glorification' is for interpretation. And by that is excluded what is in the manner of mockery and ridicule, as in the statement of the angels to Abu Jahl: 'Taste! Indeed, you are the mighty, the noble' — meaning according to your claim among your people, because Abu Jahl (may Allah curse him) used to say: 'I am the mightiest of the deserts and the most generous of them.' And the expression of al-Khazin is its text: 'Taste, i.e., this punishment; indeed you are the mighty, the noble; i.e., among your people according to your claim. And that is because Abu Jahl used to say: I am the mightiest of the deserts and the most generous of them, so the keepers of Hellfire say that to him by way of belittlement and reproach.' And in reality, this is excluded from the beginning, because it is not praise except in form, so this restriction is, upon investigation, for clarification.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a qualifier in the definition. The author interprets the phrase 'upon the manner of glorification and magnfication' as an explanatory annexation; the two words are synonyms for emphasis. This qualifier excludes any statement that takes the form of praise but is actually mockery or ridicule, such as the angels' words to Abu Jahl in Hell (Quran 44:49). The author quotes al-Khazin's tafsir to show that the words are said sarcastically. He notes that such statements are not truly praise, so the qualifier is clarifying rather than adding a new condition; it merely makes explicit what is already implicit in the concept of hamd.

Key Terms
تبجيل (tabjil)
تعظيم (ta'zim)
استهزاء (istihza')
سخرية (sukhriya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any statement praising in form is considered hamd, regardless of intent.
Correction: The author shows that hamd requires genuine glorification and magnification; mockery in the form of praise is excluded.

Checkpoint
Why is the angels' statement to Abu Jahl not considered hamd?
A) Because it is not in the form of praise.
B) Because it is said with the intention of mockery.
C) Because it is a divine statement.
D) Because it is said by angels.

Key Takeaway
True hamd must be offered with sincere glorification and magnification, not merely in the form of praise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The angels' statement was only praise in form, not reality. Are there areas of your worship—prayer, charity, or even your daily speech—that are only 'form'? What would change if you infused them with true tabjīl and ta‘ẓīm?

The Comprehensive Definition of Hamd
Definition of hamd with its three dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of hamd as an act of glorification that encompasses speech, belief, and action.

Core Idea
Hamd is not just words—it includes heart and limbs.

This lesson unpacks the precise technical meaning of hamd as defined in classical Islamic theology. The passage clarifies that hamd is a comprehensive act covering all forms of expressing gratitude and glorification to the Benefactor, whether by tongue, heart, or actions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author specifies 'terminologically' to distinguish from the linguistic meaning.
The definition begins with 'وأما الحمد اصطلاحاً'.
2
Identify the three dimensions
The passage lists three ways hamd can be expressed: speech, belief, and action. Identify each and understand that none is excluded.
3
Reflect on inclusiveness
Consider why the author includes belief and action. This shows that hamd is not merely a verbal act but a comprehensive state of gratitude.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that hamd is only verbal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحمد اصطلاحاً فهو فعل ينبئ عن تعظيم المنعم من حيث كونه منعماً على الحامد أو غيره؛ سواء كان ذلك قولاً باللسان أو اعتقاداً بالجنان، أو عملاً بالأركان.
As for hamd terminologically, it is an act that indicates glorification of the benefactor (al-mun'im) in terms of being a benefactor upon the one who praises or others, whether that is speech by the tongue, belief in the heart, or action by the limbs.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a definition. The author provides the technical (istilahi) definition of hamd: it is an act (fi'l) that conveys glorification (ta'zim) of the benefactor (al-mun'im) specifically because he is a benefactor, whether the beneficiary is the one praising or someone else. The act can be verbal (by the tongue), cognitive (belief in the heart), or physical (actions of the limbs). This definition is comprehensive, covering all forms of expressing gratitude and glorification to Allah for His favors.

Key Terms

فعل (fi'l)
تعظيم (ta'zim)
منعم (mun'im)
قول (qawl)
اعتقاد (i'tiqad)
عمل ('amal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamd is only verbal praise.
Correction: The definition includes belief and action, making hamd a comprehensive act.

Checkpoint

A) Speech
B) Belief
C) Action
D) Silence

Key Takeaway
Hamd is a comprehensive act that includes speech, belief, and action, all directed to glorify the Benefactor.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Consider a difficulty someone else is going through. Does your hamd to Allah include gratitude for His blessings upon them, even when you are not the direct recipient? How can you train yourself to praise Allah for His kindness to others as well?

The Condition of Optionality and Praising God's Essence
The author answers an objection about whether praising God's essence counts as hamd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the condition of optionality in hamd applies to praising God's essence and attributes, and how scholars include them under juridical optionality.

Core Idea
Praising God is still hamd because His essence is the source of optional acts.

The author has defined hamd as praise for an optional act of beneficence. But what about praising God’s essence or attributes, which are not optional? This lesson examines the objection and the scholarly response that expands the meaning of 'optional' to include what is the source of optional acts or inseparable from it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully, focusing on the objection that praising God's essence would not be called hamd because it is not optional.
The objection is introduced by 'فإن قلت'.
2
Understand the answer: juridical optionality
Notice that the author responds by expanding 'optional' to include juridically what is the source of optional acts (like divine essence and attributes of influence) or inseparable from that source (like attributes not of influence).
Attributes of influence include will and power; attributes not of influence include life, knowledge, speech, hearing, sight.
3
Reflect on the doctrinal importance
Consider why this distinction matters: it ensures that all genuine praise of God, including His essence and attributes, is considered hamd, not merely madh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذاتاً واعتباراً ; كما إذا أكرمك زيد ; فقلت زيد عالم فإن المحمود عليه هو الكرم والمحمود به هو العلم . فإن قلت : التقييد بالاختياري يخرجُ الحمد على ذاته تعالى وصفاته، فظاهره أنه لا يسمَّى حمداً، والتزمه بعضُهم وقال يسمّى مدحاً . قلتُ: أجيب عن ذلك بأن المراد ما يشمل الاختياري حقيقة وهو ظاهر، أو حكماً والمراد به ما كان منشأ للأفعال الاختيارية (۲) كالذات وصفاتِ التأثير (۳) أو ملازماً للمنشأ; كصفات غير التأثير (٤) وقولنا : ( على جهة التبجيل والتعظيم أي : على جهة هي التبجيل والتعظيم، فالإضافة للبيان )، وعطف التعظيم على التبجيل للتفسير ; وخرج بذلك ما إذا كان على جهة الاستهزاء والسخرية، كما في قول الملائكة لأبي جهل : ﴿ذُقْ إِنَّكَ أَنتَ العزيزُ الكَرِيمُ﴾ [الدخان: ٤٩] أي: بزعمك عند قومك، وذلك أن أبا جهل - لعنه الله - كان يقول: أنا أعزُّ البوادي وأكرمهم ، وعبارة الخازن ما نصه: (ذُقْ; أي: هذا العذاب، إنك أنت العزيز الكريم; أي: عند قومك بزعمك; وذلك أن أبا جهل - لعنه الله - كان يقول : أنا أعزُّ البوادي وأكرمُهم ، فتقول خزنة النار له ذلك على طريق الاستخفاف والتوبيخ)) . وفي الحقيقة هذا خارج من أوَّلِ الأمر ; لأنه ليس ثناء إلا بحسب الصورة، فهذا القيد عند التحقيق للإيضاح
Essence and consideration; as when Zayd honors you, so you say 'Zayd is generous', then the praised-for is generosity, and the praised-by is knowledge. If you say: 'The restriction to the optional excludes praise of His essence and attributes, so apparently it is not called hamd, and some adhered to this and said it is called madh.' I say: 'It is answered that what is meant includes the optional either literally—which is apparent—or juridically, meaning that which is the source of optional acts (2), like the essence and attributes of influence (3), or that which is inseparable from the source, like attributes not of influence (4). And our saying: 'on the basis of veneration and glorification' means: on a basis that is veneration and glorification, so the annexation is explanatory; and the conjunction of glorification to veneration is for interpretation; and by that is excluded what is on the basis of mockery and ridicule, as in the saying of the angels to Abu Jahl: 'Taste! Indeed, you are the mighty, the generous' (Quran 44:49), meaning: in your claim among your people, because Abu Jahl—may Allah curse him—used to say: I am the mightiest of the Bedouins and the most generous of them. And the expression of al-Khazin is: 'Taste, that is, this punishment; indeed you are the mighty, the generous, that is, among your people in your claim; because Abu Jahl used to say: I am the mightiest and most generous, so the keepers of Hell say that to him by way of belittling and reprimand.' And in reality this is excluded from the beginning, because it is not praise except in form; so this restriction in investigation is for clarification.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the condition of optionality (اختياري) in the definition of hamd (praise). The author first gives an example: if Zayd honors you, you say 'Zayd is generous' — the praised-for (المحمود عليه) is the generosity (the act), and the praised-by (المحمود به) is the knowledge (the attribute). Then he raises an objection: if hamd requires that the praised act be optional, then praising God's essence and attributes (which are not optional) would not be called hamd. Some scholars indeed said it is called madh (commendation) instead. The author responds by saying that the term 'optional' includes either literal optionality (which is apparent) or juridical optionality, meaning that which is the source of optional acts (like the divine essence and attributes of influence such as will and power), or that which is inseparable from the source (like attributes not of influence such as life, knowledge, speech, hearing, sight). Then he explains the phrase 'on the basis of veneration and glorification' (على جهة التبجيل والتعظيم), noting that it excludes praise given mockingly, as in the angels' address to Abu Jahl in the Quran. He cites al-Khazin's explanation that this is said by way of belittling and reprimand, not genuine praise. Finally, he concludes that such mockery is excluded from the start and the restriction is for clarification.

Key Terms
حمْد
مدح
اختياري
صفات التأثير
صفات غير التأثير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Praising God's essence and attributes is not hamd but only madh because it is not optional.
Correction: The author shows that under juridical optionality, praising God's essence and attributes is included in hamd because they are the source of optional acts or inseparable from that source.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does 'optional' include when praising God's essence?
A) Only literal optional acts
B) Only attributes not of influence
C) The source of optional acts or inseparable from it
D) Only acts of worship

Key Takeaway
Praising God's essence and attributes qualifies as hamd because they are the source of optional acts or inseparable from that source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise Allah for His attribute 'Al-Khāliq' (The Creator), do you recognize that you are praising Him for what is the source of His optional act of creation? How does this awareness deepen your hamd beyond just thanking for favors? Commit to including praise of His essence in your daily adhkār, not just for what He gives.
?
Have you ever thought that praising Allah for His essence (like His being 'Al-Ḥayy') is somehow less meaningful than praising Him for specific blessings? This lesson shows that such praise is still hamd. Will you now intentionally praise Allah for qualities like His life, knowledge, and power, knowing they are the fountainhead of all optional acts? Pause and formulate one such praise right now.
?
The distinction between hamd and madh is subtle but significant. In your own life, do you ever praise people for inherent qualities (like intelligence) versus voluntary actions (like generosity)? According to this lesson, which type of praise is deeper? Ask yourself: Am I failing to praise Allah for His essence because I don't see it as 'optional'? Resolve to correct that today by reciting 'Al-ḥamdu lillāh' with full awareness of its comprehensive meaning.

The Exclusion of Mockery from Hamd
The author clarifies that hamd must be based on veneration, excluding mockery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why praise given mockingly is not considered hamd, as illustrated by the angels' address to Abu Jahl.

Core Idea
Praise is only hamd when based on genuine veneration; mockery is excluded.

The definition of hamd includes the phrase 'on the basis of veneration and glorification.' This lesson explores why that phrase is necessary by examining the Quranic example of the angels mocking Abu Jahl. Genuine praise must be rooted in respect, not ridicule.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the angels' statement
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the explanation of the phrase 'on the basis of veneration and glorification' and the Quranic example.
The example is from Quran 44:49.
2
Understand the distinction between form and substance
Notice that the angels' words appear to be praise but are actually mockery. The author says it is not praise except in form.
3
Recognize the purpose of the restriction
Understand that the restriction clarifies that hamd must be genuine veneration; otherwise, it is excluded from the beginning.
The author concludes that this restriction is for clarification.

The Terminological Definition of Hamd (Praise)
The author defines hamd terminologically as an act indicating veneration of the Benefactor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the comprehensive terminological definition of hamd (praise) as an act that includes speech, belief, and action, and why belief is considered an act in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Hamd is not just words—it is any act that shows veneration of the Benefactor, including belief and action.

In this passage, the author specifies what hamd (praise) means in Islamic terminology. While many might think praise is simply speaking well of someone, the author expands it to include internal belief and physical actions. This lesson will clarify the definition and its three forms, and address a common misunderstanding about the nature of belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author defines hamd as an 'act' that indicates 'veneration of the Benefactor' and then specifies three forms.
The key is that hamd is an act, not just a statement.
2
Identify the three forms of hamd
The author lists three forms: speech with the tongue, belief with the heart, and action with the limbs. Find these in the text and understand how each qualifies as hamd.
Belief is explicitly included, which may be surprising.
3
Understand why belief is an act
The author explains that belief is an act because it is a determination (التصميم) of the heart. Then he dismisses a subtle philosophical discussion about belief being a 'how' or a form in the soul.
The author prioritizes the practical definition over theoretical nuances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحمد :اصطلاحاً فهو فعل ينبئ عن تعظيم المنعِم من حيث كونه منعِماً على الحامد أو غيره ; سواء كان ذلك قولاً باللسان أو اعتقاداً بالجنان، أو عملاً بالأركان التي هي الأعضاء (١) ، كما قال القائل: [من الطويل] أَفَادَتْكُمُ النَّعْمَاءُ مِنِّي ثَلَاثَةٌ يَدِي وَلِسَانِي وَالضَّمِيرَ المُحَجَّبَا (٢) وإنما كان الاعتقاد فعلاً لأنه التصميم بالقلب، وأما قولهم: التحقيق أنه كيف; أي الصورة الحاصلة في النفس فهو تدقيق كلامي لا ينظر إليه هنا .
And as for hamd terminologically: it is an act that indicates veneration of the Benefactor insofar as He is a Benefactor to the praiser or to others, whether that be by speech with the tongue, or belief with the heart, or action with the limbs which are the organs (1), as the poet said: [from the tawil meter] 'The favors from me have benefited you three: my hand, my tongue, and the veiled conscience' (2). And belief is an act because it is determination with the heart. As for their saying: 'the verification is that it is a how, i.e., a form occurring in the soul' — that is a kalam subtlety not considered here.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage provides the terminological definition of hamd (praise). The author defines hamd as 'an act that indicates veneration of the Benefactor insofar as He is a Benefactor to the praiser or to others.' This act can be of three types: (1) speech with the tongue (قول باللسان), (2) belief with the heart (اعتقاد بالجنان), or (3) action with the limbs (عمل بالأركان). To support that action includes belief, he cites a line of poetry about benefiting by hand, tongue, and conscience. He explains that belief is considered an act because it is a determination (تصميم) of the heart. He then dismisses a philosophical subtlety about whether belief is a 'how' or form in the soul, saying it is a kalam subtlety not relevant here.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hamd is only verbal praise.
Correction: Hamd includes speech, belief, and action. The author explicitly includes actions of the heart and limbs.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The author dismisses a subtle debate about the nature of belief as a 'how' as not relevant here. Are you sometimes distracted by theological arguments that prevent you from actually practicing hamd in your life? What step can you take to move from discussion to action?

Belief as Praise and the Definition of Shukr
Answers objection about belief not informing of veneration, then defines shukr linguistically and terminologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that belief cannot inform of veneration and how he defines shukr linguistically and terminologically.

Core Idea
Belief informs veneration directly or through speech, and shukr means using blessings for their purpose.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if belief is internal, how can it be a form of praise? The author offers two compelling answers and then defines shukr, showing its relationship to hamd. Understanding this clarifies the role of inner faith in worship.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the objection that belief does not inform about veneration. Then see the author's two-part answer.
The answer has two distinct responses; try to identify both.
2
Understand the two forms of praise
The author explains that when belief is inferred from speech, there are two praises: one by speech and one by the belief from which it is taken. Reflect on how this shows belief is a valid form of veneration.
3
Learn the definition of shukr
The author gives the linguistic definition of shukr (same as hamd) and the terminological definition (directing blessings to their purpose). Compare shukr to hamd and think about how this definition applies to daily life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الاعتقاد لا ينبئ عن تعظيم المنعم أجيب بأنه ينبئ لو اطلع عليه، أو أنه يُستدل عليه بالقول، ويتحقق حينئذ حمدان; أحدهما بالقول، والآخرُ بالاعتقاد المأخوذ منه . والشكر لغة : هو الحمدُ اصطلاحاً ، لكن بإبدال الحامد بالشاكر. واصطلاحاً صرفُ العبدِ جميع ما أنعم الله عليه به فيما خُلقَ لأجله (٥) .
If it is said: 'Belief does not inform about veneration of the Benefactor.' It is answered that it informs if one is aware of it, or it is inferred from speech, and then there are two praises: one by speech and the other by the belief taken from it. And shukr linguistically: it is hamd terminologically, but with substitution of the praiser by the thank-giver. And terminologically: it is the servant's directing all that Allah has bestowed upon him to that for which it was created (5).
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection and then defines shukr. The objection states: belief (اعتقاد) does not inform others about veneration of the Benefactor, so it cannot be a valid form of hamd since hamd is supposed to indicate veneration. The author gives two answers: (1) belief does inform if someone becomes aware of it (e.g., by revelation or knowledge of the heart), and (2) belief is inferred from speech, so there are actually two praises: one by speech and one by the belief that is taken from it. Then he defines shukr (gratitude/thanks). Linguistically, shukr is the same as hamd terminologically, but with the substitution of 'praiser' by 'thank-giver'. Terminologically, shukr is the servant's directing all that Allah has bestowed upon him to the purpose for which it was created. This emphasizes using blessings in their intended way.

Key Terms
اعتقاد
حمْد
شكر
نبأ
تعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Shukr is merely verbal thanks or feeling gratitude in the heart, and belief cannot be considered a form of praise because it is hidden.
Correction: The author shows that belief can inform others either directly or through inference, making it a valid form of praise. Moreover, shukr terminologically involves actively using blessings for their intended purpose, not just verbal expression.

Checkpoint
How does the author answer the objection that belief does not inform about veneration?
A) Belief informs only through action
B) Belief informs when known or is inferred from speech
C) Belief is not actually required
D) Belief is less important than speech

Key Takeaway
Belief can serve as a form of praise through awareness or inference, and true gratitude (shukr) means using every blessing for its intended purpose.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'alhamdulillah' for your eyesight, do you also actually use your eyes to look at what Allah created for you to see, or do you let them wander toward what He forbade? What needs to change today?
?
Your beliefs are a form of praise to Allah—is your heart really magnifying Him, or is it filled with distractions and doubts? Take a moment to examine what you truly believe about Allah right now, and ask Him to purify your conviction.
?
Shukr means using every blessing for its purpose. Look at your wealth, your time, your health: are you spending them on what Allah created them for, or are you consuming them carelessly? Pick one blessing and commit to redirecting it starting today.

The Act of Belief and the Objection about Glorification
Author defines belief as an act and answers an objection about its hidden nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why belief (i'tiqad) is considered an act of the heart and how it can convey glorification despite being internal.

Core Idea
Belief is a heart's determination that can be known and praised even when hidden.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if belief is an internal state, how can it be a basis for praising Allah? The author first defines belief as a heart's act of determination, then answers the objection by showing that belief can be directly known or inferred from speech. This clarifies that internal acts are still praiseworthy.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of belief
Notice the author states belief is an act because it is determination (tasmeem) of the heart. He dismisses a more technical definition as irrelevant for this context.
2
Identify the objection
The objection claims belief does not inform others of glorification, so how can it be a basis for praise? See the logic.
3
Understand the two-part answer
The answer provides two ways belief informs: either by direct insight or by inference from speech. This results in two praises.
4
Reflect on the practical implication
Connect that internal acts of the heart are still valid for reward and praise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما كان الاعتقاد فعلاً لأنه التصميم بالقلب، وأما قولهم: التحقيق أنه كيف؛ أي الصورة الحاصلة في النفس فهو تدقيق كلامي لا ينظر إليه هنا . فإن قيل : الاعتقاد لا ينبئ عن تعظيم المنعم أجيب بأنه ينبئ لو اطلع عليه، أو أنه يُستدل عليه بالقول، ويتحقق حينئذ حمدان؛ أحدهما بالقول، والآخرُ بالاعتقاد المأخوذ منه .
And belief is only an act because it is the determination of the heart. Then if it is said: Belief does not inform of the glorification of the Benefactor, it is answered: it informs if one is aware of it, or it is inferred from speech, and then two praises are realized: one by speech, and the other by the belief taken from it.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The author first defines i'tiqad (belief) as an act of the heart, specifically the determination (tasmeem) of the heart. He dismisses a detailed technical definition as irrelevant. Then an objection is raised: belief is internal and does not inform others of glorification, so how can it be a basis for praise? The answer has two parts: either the belief informs if someone has insight into the heart, or it is inferred from outward speech, and thus two praises occur—one by speech, one by the belief that is inferred.

The Definition of Shukr (Thanks) Linguistically and Terminologically
Author defines shukr linguistically and terminologically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and terminological definitions of shukr (thanks) and how it differs from mere verbal praise.

Core Idea
Shukr is not just saying thanks – it means using Allah’s blessings for their intended purpose.

In this brief passage, the author clarifies what true thankfulness to Allah means. He first gives the linguistic definition, equating shukr to praise, and then the terminological definition, which goes beyond words to the proper use of blessings. This foundational concept is essential for understanding human responsibility toward Allah's gifts.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the conciseness of the original.
2
Compare linguistic and terminological definitions
Identify the two definitions: linguistic (shukr as hamd with agent substitution) and terminological (using blessings as intended). Note the shift from words to actions.
3
Note the key difference in agent
Linguistically, shukr is like praise but with 'thanks-giver' instead of 'praiser'. Terminologically, it focuses on the use of blessings, not just utterance.
4
Reflect on practical implications
Consider how this definition affects your daily gratitude: Are you using your blessings in the way Allah intended? How can you improve?
This connects the lesson to personal practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والشكر لغة : هو الحمدُ اصطلاحاً ، لكن بإبدال الحامد بالشاكر. واصطلاحاً صرفُ العبدِ جميع ما أنعم الله عليه به فيما خُلقَ لأجله .
And shukr (thanks) linguistically is hamd (praise) terminologically, but by replacing the one who praises (al-hamid) with the thanks-giver (al-shakir). And terminologically it is the slave's expenditure of all that Allah has bestowed upon him in that for which it was created.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage defines shukr. Linguistically, shukr is equated to hamd (praise) but the agent is different: the one who praises becomes the one who gives thanks. Terminologically, shukr means using all blessings from Allah in the way they were intended to be used, i.e., in obedience and proper purpose.

Grammar of Invocation upon the Prophet
Commentary explains the grammar of the poem's invocation on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical construction of the phrase 'upon a prophet' in the poem and why the preposition 'ala' is used instead of 'lam'.

Core Idea
The grammar of supplication shapes our understanding of prophethood in this verse.

This lesson unpacks the opening line of a section on prophets, explaining the precise grammar used to invoke blessings upon them. The poem says 'Upon a prophet who came with tawhid', and the commentary clarifies the grammatical function of each word. By examining this construction, we avoid confusion about the status of prophets and learn a subtle rule about fixed expressions in Arabic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation. Notice that the passage begins by identifying the phrase as a prepositional phrase serving as a predicate.
The passage is a commentary on a line from the poem.
2
Identify the grammatical function
The author states that 'upon a prophet' is a preposition and its object that relate back to a deleted predicate, meaning the full phrase is 'the prayers and peace are upon a prophet'. This is a common construction in Arabic.
3
Understand the tanazu' issue
The author explains that this is not a case of tanazu' (mutual attraction) because some grammarians forbid it in fixed expressions. This is a technical point about Arabic syntax.
Tanazu' occurs when two operators act on the same noun.
4
Note the use of 'ala'
Normally, supplication for good uses 'lam' (for), and for evil uses 'ala' (upon). Here, 'ala' is used despite it being a good supplication. The commentary says this is because of the prohibition of tanazu' in fixed expressions, though the reasoning is cut off.
This shows how grammatical rules affect the choice of prepositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تعريف النبي والرسول وبيانُ أعدادهم عليهم أفضل الصلاة والسلام] ٢ - عَلَى نَبِيِّ جَاءَ بِالتَّوْحِيدِ وَقَدْ خَلَا الدِّينُ عَنِ التَّوْحِيدِ قوله : ( عَلَى نَبِيٍّ ) أي : كائنان على نبي، فالجار والمجرور متعلق بمحذوف خبر المبتدأ، وليس من باب التنازع ؛ لأن بعضهم منعه في الجوامد، وإنما عدى الدعاء ب (على) مع أن الدعاء إن كان بخير تعدَّى باللام وإن كان بشر تعدى بـ (على)، لأن
[Definition of the Prophet and the Messenger and statement of their numbers, may the best prayers and peace be upon them] 2. 'Upon a prophet who came with tawhid, while religion was devoid of tawhid.' His saying: 'Upon a prophet' i.e., (the prayers and peace) are upon a prophet. The preposition and its object are related to a deleted predicate of the subject, and it is not a case of mutual attraction because some have prohibited it in fixed expressions. The supplication was made with 'upon' even though supplication for good is normally with 'lam' and for evil with 'ala', because...
Source pages p. 44

Commentary
This passage begins a new section defining the terms 'Nabi' (prophet) and 'Rasul' (messenger) and stating their numbers. The author comments on the line 'Upon a prophet who came with tawhid...' from the poem. He explains the grammatical function of the phrase 'upon a prophet' as a prepositional phrase serving as a predicate for a deleted subject (i.e., the blessings are upon a prophet). He clarifies that this is not an instance of 'tanazu'' (mutual attraction between two operators) because some grammarians have prohibited tanazu' in fixed expressions. He then notes the usage of the preposition 'ala' (upon) in supplication, even though supplication for good usually takes 'lam' and supplication for evil takes 'ala'. The reason for this usage is left incomplete in the text (it likely continues to explain that the supplication here is considered as an honorific, not literally for good or evil).

Key Terms
النبي
التنازع
على

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the preposition 'ala' (upon) used in the supplication instead of 'lam'?
A) Because the supplication is for evil.
B) Because some grammarians prohibited tanazu' in fixed expressions.
C) Because the object is a prophet.
D) Because the line requires it for rhyme.

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'upon a prophet' is a predicate construction, and the use of 'ala' is a grammatical exception related to the prohibition of tanazu' in fixed expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite blessings upon the Prophet, do you ever reflect on the precise meaning of each word, or do you let familiarity numb you to their weight? How can the grammar of a single preposition shape the sincerity of your invocation? Commit today to learning the meaning of one phrase you often repeat.
?
The source shows that even a fixed expression like 'upon a prophet' is analyzed with care. Are there aspects of your faith that you have taken for granted, assuming you already understand them? What would it cost you to examine them more deeply this week?
?
The use of 'ala' for good is an exception due to a grammatical rule. Think of a time you made an exception to a norm for a higher purpose. How does this mirror the way divine revelation prioritizes meaning over convention, and how can you apply that principle in your own life?

The Definitions of Nabi and Rasul and Their Relationship
Formal definitions of Nabi and Rasul and four opinions on their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the formal definitions of Nabi and Rasul in Islamic theology and the four scholarly views on how they relate logically.

Core Idea
Every Rasul is a Nabi, but not every Nabi is a Rasul – yet scholars differ.

Before studying the proofs of prophethood, it is essential to clarify exactly who qualifies as a prophet (Nabi) and who as a messenger (Rasul). Theologians have laid down precise conditions for each term, and there is significant scholarly debate on whether the two categories overlap entirely, partially, or are identical. This passage from Sanusi presents both the definitions and the four main positions on the relationship.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Notice the conditions for a Nabi: human, male, free, from Adam's children, free from repulsive qualities, receives revelation prescribing a law even if not commanded to convey. Then see how a Rasul adds the command to convey.
2
Identify the four opinions
The text gives four views on the relationship: (1) absolute generality/specificity, (2) messenger more general (including angels), (3) equality, (4) general-special from one aspect.
3
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: why does this distinction matter? It affects which individuals are considered prophets and messengers, and how we understand their roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرَّفُوا النبيَّ بأنه إنسان ذكرٌ حرّ من بني آدم سليم عن منفّرِ طبعاً، أوحي إليه يشرع يُعمل به وإن لم يُؤمر بتبليغه، وأما الرسول فيعرف بما ذكر، لكن مع التقييد بقولنا : وأمر بتبليغه، فبينهما العمومُ والخصوص المطلق; لأن كل رسول نبي ولا عكس وجعل بعضهم الرسول أعم ، قال : لأن الرسلَ تكون من الملائكة، وقال العلامة السعد التفتازاني : هما متساويان، وقيل : بينهما العموم والخصوص الوجهي; لأن النبيَّ فقط من أوحي إليه بشرع يُعمل به واختص به، والرسول فقط
And they defined the prophet as a human male, free, from the children of Adam, naturally free from repulsive qualities, to whom revelation is given prescribing a law to be acted upon, even if he is not commanded to convey it. As for the messenger, he is defined by what was mentioned, but with the restriction of our saying: 'and he is commanded to convey it.' So between them there is absolute generality and specificity; because every messenger is a prophet but not vice versa. And some made the messenger more general, saying because messengers can be from the angels. And the distinguished scholar Sa‘d al-Taftazani said: they are equal. And it was said: between them there is general-special from one aspect (al-‘umum wa al-khusus al-wajhi), because the prophet alone is one to whom revelation is given with a law to be acted upon and he is devoted to it, and the messenger alone is...
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a formal definition section. It begins by defining 'النبي' (prophet) with several conditions: human, male, free, from the children of Adam, naturally free from repulsive qualities, receives revelation prescribing a law to be acted upon, even if not commanded to convey it. Then it defines 'الرسول' (messenger) with the same conditions plus the additional requirement that he is commanded to convey the revelation. The passage then discusses the logical relationship between the two terms. Four views are mentioned: (1) Absolute generality and specificity (العموم والخصوص المطلق): every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger. (2) Some made the messenger more general, arguing that messengers can be from angels as well, thus including angelic messengers. (3) Al-Taftazani said they are equal (متساويان). (4) It was said there is general-special from one aspect (العموم والخصوص الوجهي): a prophet alone receives a law and is devoted to it without conveying, while a messenger alone conveys, but some individuals may share both aspects. The text is cut off at the end, but the opinions are clear. This passage is a classification of the terms and the scholarly differences regarding their relationship.

Key Terms
النبي
الرسول
العموم والخصوص المطلق
العموم والخصوص الوجهي
التساوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'Nabi' and 'Rasul' are interchangeable terms with no distinction.
Correction: The text shows that there are precise conditions and differing opinions on their relationship; they are not simply synonyms.

Checkpoint
According to the majority view mentioned, what is the relationship between a prophet and a messenger?
A) Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger
B) Every prophet is a messenger, but not every messenger is a prophet
C) They are exactly the same
D) They are completely different with no overlap

Key Takeaway
The definitions of Nabi and Rasul differ in the command to convey revelation, and scholars hold four different positions on whether the terms are absolutely/generally related, partially overlapping, or identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The definition of a Nabi includes receiving a law to be acted upon even if not commanded to convey it. Do you treat the truth you learn as merely for personal action, or do you feel a responsibility to convey it to others? What would change if you saw yourself as a messenger of that truth today?
?
Scholars disagree on the exact relationship between Nabi and Rasul. How does this diversity of opinion challenge your own tendency to insist on one view as absolutely correct? In what area of your life can you practice more humility toward differing orthodox positions?
?
The prophet is described as 'naturally free from repulsive qualities'. Look honestly at your own character: what repulsive quality—pride, harshness, laziness—have you tolerated in yourself? What specific step will you take this week to seek purification from it?

Derivation of the Word 'Nabi'
Author enumerates the etymological explanations of 'nabi'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple linguistic derivations of the word 'nabi' and how each reflects an aspect of prophethood.

Core Idea
The word 'nabi' holds layers of meaning: news-bringer and rank-elevated.

The author of the Jawhara explores the etymology of the word 'nabi' (prophet) to reveal the depth of its meaning. Rather than a single definition, he presents multiple possible derivations, each shedding light on a different aspect of prophethood. This lesson unpacks these linguistic roots.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the author's method of presenting multiple possibilities.
The passage includes footnote markers (3) which refer to scholarly notes.
2
Identify the two main derivations
The passage proposes two roots: from 'naba' (news) and from 'nabwah' (elevation). Find where each is mentioned.
3
Examine the 'naba' derivation more closely
Under the 'naba' derivation, the author gives two interpretations: the prophet as informer (mukhbir) or as informed (mukhbar). Notice the vocalization difference (kasra vs. fatha) and the implications.
The text reads 'mukhayyar' but it is likely a typo for 'mukhbir' or 'mukhbar'.
4
Understand the morphological conclusion
The author concludes that the pattern 'fa'eel' can be used for both active and passive meanings, making both derivations linguistically valid.
This shows the richness of Arabic morphology in capturing theological concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنبيه - بالهمز وتركه - مأخوذ من النبأ ؛ وهو الخبر؛ لأنه مخير بكسر الباء، فإنه يخبرنا بالأحكام عن الله تعالى إن كان رسولاً أيضاً، فإنْ كانَ نبيًّا فقط، أخبرنا بأنه نبي ليحترم ، أو هو مخبر بفتحها ؛ لأنَّ جبريل يخبره عن الله ، أو مأخوذ من النَّبوة، وهي الرفعة (۳) ؛ لأنه مرفوع الرتبة، فإنه ما من نبي إلا وهو أفضلُ من أمته، أو رافع رتبةَ مَنْ تبعه، فعلى كلِّ (فَعِيل) صالح لاسم الفاعل واسم المفعول (۳)
And 'al-nabiyy' - with hamz and without it - is taken from 'al-naba' meaning news; because he is one who informs (mukhayyar? ) with kasra on the ya', for he informs us of rulings from Allah Almighty if he is also a messenger, and if he is only a prophet, he informs us that he is a prophet to be respected; or he is one who is informed (mukhbar) with fatha, because Jibril informs him from Allah; or it is taken from 'al-nubuwwa' meaning elevation (3); because he is raised in rank, for there is no prophet except that he is better than his nation, or he raises the rank of those who follow him. So in any case, the pattern 'fa'eel' is suitable for the active participle and the passive participle (3).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a lexicographical explanation of the word 'nabi' (prophet). The author presents multiple possible derivations. First, 'nabi' can be derived from 'naba' (news), with two possible vocalizations: if it is read as 'mukhbir' (informing) with kasra, the prophet is the one who informs people of divine rulings, especially if he is also a messenger; if he is only a prophet, he informs that he is a prophet so that he may be respected. Alternatively, it could be read as 'mukhbar' (informed) with fatha, because Gabriel informs the prophet from Allah. Second, 'nabi' may be derived from 'nabwah' (height, elevation), because the prophet is elevated in rank—every prophet is superior to his nation, or he elevates the ranks of his followers. The author concludes that the morphological pattern 'fa'eel' in Arabic is valid for both the active and passive meanings, so both derivations are plausible. The footnote (3) likely references a scholarly discussion on the pattern.

النبأ
النباوة
الرفعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The word 'nabi' is derived only from 'naba' meaning news.
Correction: The author shows it can also be derived from 'nabwah' meaning elevation, and both are valid.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The word 'nabi' in Arabic carries dual meanings of conveying news (from Allah) and elevated rank, both captured by the morphological pattern 'fa'eel'.

?

?
When you think of the Prophet, do you see him primarily as a news-bringer or as one of elevated rank? How does your answer affect the way you obey his commands and honor his status?

Definitions of Nabi and Rasul and Their Relationship
Author defines nabi and rasul and presents views on the relationship between them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of nabi and rasul in Islamic theology and the different scholarly views on the relationship between these two terms.

Core Idea
Every messenger is a prophet, but not every prophet is a messenger.

This passage from the Jawharah commentary establishes precise definitions for two central terms in Islamic prophethood: nabi (prophet) and rasul (messenger). After defining each, the author presents multiple scholarly opinions on whether they are identical, overlapping, or one is more general. Understanding these definitions is essential for deeper study of prophethood and revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions carefully
Pay attention to each condition in the definition of nabi and note the additional condition for rasul. Write down the key elements: human, male, free, etc.
The definition is very specific and must be memorized.
2
Identify the four views on relationship
The passage presents absolute generality, equality, mutual generality, and one view making rasul more general. List each view and its supporters.
The view of Taftazani (equality) is a minority opinion.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how each view affects our understanding of the roles of prophets and messengers. Which view is most widely accepted and why?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعرَّفُوا النبيَّ بأنه إنسان ذكرٌ حرّ من بني آدم سليم عن منفّرِ طبعاً، أوحي إليه يشرع يُعمل به وإن لم يُؤمر بتبليغه، وأما الرسول فيعرف بما ذكر، لكن مع التقييد بقولنا : وأمر بتبليغه (٤) ، فبينهما العمومُ والخصوص المطلق؛ لأن كل رسول نبي ولا عكس وجعل بعضهم الرسول أعم ، قال : لأن الرسلَ تكون من الملائكة، وقال العلامة السعد التفتازاني : هما متساويان)(٥) ، وقيل : بينهما العموم والخصوص الوجهي؛ لأن النبيَّ فقط من أوحي إليه بشرع يُعمل به واختص به، والرسول فقط من أُوحي إليه بشرع يعملُ به ويبلغه لغيره ولم يختص بشيء منه. فإن اختص بالبعض وبلغَ البعض فهو نبي ورسول (۱) .
And they defined al-nabi as: a human, male, free, from the children of Adam, free from natural repulsiveness, inspired to him a law to act upon even if not commanded to convey it. As for al-rasul, he is defined by what was mentioned, but with the restriction by our saying: and commanded to convey it (4). So between them is absolute generality and particularity, because every rasul is a nabi and not the reverse. And some made al-rasul more general; they said because messengers can be from angels. And the scholar Sa'd al-Taftazani said: they are equal (5). And it was said: between them is mutual generality and particularity; because the nabi solely is one inspired with a law to act upon and he keeps it to himself, and the rasul solely is one inspired with a law to act upon and convey to others and he does not keep anything of it to himself. If he keeps some and conveys some, then he is a nabi and a rasul (1).
Source pages p. 45

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section that first provides the technical definition of a prophet (nabi) and a messenger (rasul) in Islamic theology, and then explains the logical relationship between the two terms. The definition of nabi includes conditions: he must be a human male, free, from the children of Adam, free from any naturally repulsive qualities, and he receives revelation (wahy) of a law (shar') that he is to act upon, even if he is not commanded to convey it to others. The definition of rasul is the same except that he is additionally commanded to convey the revelation. Then the author presents three scholarly views on the relationship: (1) Absolute generality and particularity: every rasul is a nabi, but not every nabi is a rasul. This is the mainstream view. (2) Some scholars consider 'rasul' more general because messengers can be from angels (though the definition given restricts to humans, so this may be a conceptual generalization). (3) Al-Taftazani held that the two terms are coextensive (equal). (4) A third opinion holds that there is mutual generality and particularity: a nabi is one who receives law for himself only, a rasul is one who receives law to convey, and if someone does both he is both nabi and rasul. The footnote (1) refers to the Quranic example of Ismail being both.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume 'nabi' and 'rasul' are synonymous and interchangeable terms.
Correction: The dominant view distinguishes them: every rasul is a nabi, but not every nabi is a rasul; the key distinction is the command to convey revelation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The distinction between nabi and rasul lies in the command to convey the revelation: a rasul must deliver it, while a nabi may not be required to do so.
