The Definition of Prophet and Messenger and Their Exclusivity to Human Males
Defines prophet and messenger and excludes animals and women.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of a prophet and messenger and why prophethood is limited to human males, excluding animals and women.

Core Idea
Prophethood is a male human prerogative; no female or animal can be a prophet.

This lesson establishes the foundational definition of a prophet and messenger according to Sunni theology. It then clarifies two crucial exclusions: non-human animals cannot be prophets, and women cannot be prophets. The author also addresses a controversial claim about female prophets, showing it to be a weak opinion.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Definition
Read the definition of a prophet and messenger: a human male to whom a law is revealed to act upon and convey. Note the condition of not being specialized in only part of the revelation.
The definition includes both prophet and messenger; the distinction is in specialization.
2
Examine the Exclusion of Animals
The passage states that animals are excluded from prophethood. Anyone who claims every animal group has a messenger is committing disbelief. The Quranic verse (35:24) is about human nations, not animals.
3
Examine the Exclusion of Women
The passage explains that women are excluded because the word 'human' (insān) in Arabic is masculine. Some linguists use 'insānah' but the author dismisses claims of female prophets as weak.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من أُوحي إليه بشرع يعملُ به ويبلغه لغيره ولم يختص بشيء منه. فإن اختص بالبعض وبلغَ البعض فهو نبي ورسول. وخرج بالإنسان بقية الحيوانات، وكفر من قال: في كل أمة نذير بمعنى أنه في كل جماعة من الحيوانات رسول، وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿وَإِن مِنْ أُمَّةٍ إِلَّا خَلَا فِيهَا نذِيرٌ﴾ [فاطر: ٢٤]، فهو في أمم البشر الماضية. وخرج بالذكر الأنثى، بناءً على أنه يقال لها : إنسان وقال بعضهم : يقال لها إنسانة، كما قال القائل: إِنْسَانَةٌ فَيَّانَةٌ بَدْرُ الدُّجَى مِنْهَا خَجِلْ وعليه فتكون الأنثى خرجت بالإنسان والقول بنبوة مريم وآسية امرأة فرعون وحواء وأم موسى - واسمها يوحانذ بالذال المعجمة - وهاجر وسارة، فهو مرجوح.
He to whom a law is revealed to act upon and convey to others, and he is not specialized in any part of it. If he is specialized in some and conveys some, then he is a prophet and a messenger. And by 'human' the rest of the animals are excluded, and he disbelieves who says: 'In every nation there is a warner' meaning that in every group of animals there is a messenger. As for His saying, the Most High: 'And there is not a nation but a warner has passed among them' [Fatir: 24], it is about past human nations. And by 'male' the female is excluded, based on the view that she is called 'insān' (human). And some said: she is called 'insānah' (female human), as the poet said: 'An insānah, young, the full moon of the night is shy of her.' Accordingly, the female is excluded by 'human'. And the claim of prophethood for Mary, Asiyah wife of Pharaoh, Eve, the mother of Moses—her name is Yūḥānidh with a dotted dhāl—Hagar, and Sarah, it is weak.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines a prophet and a messenger and then clarifies exclusions. It begins by defining the one who receives revelation (ūḥiya ilayhi) with a law to act upon and convey, without being specialized in only part of it—that person is a prophet and a messenger. If he is specialized in some parts and conveys others, he is both prophet and messenger. The author then explains the exclusions from the general term 'human' (insān). First, all animals (the rest of the living creatures) are excluded; the author even declares as disbelief the claim that every animal group has a messenger, citing Q. 35:24 which refers only to past human nations. Second, females are excluded from prophethood, based on the premise that the word 'insān' in Arabic is masculine and not applied to women. Some linguists allow 'insānah' for women, but the author dismisses the notion of female prophets—mentioning Mary, Asiyah, Eve, Moses' mother, Hagar, and Sarah—as a weak (marjūḥ) opinion. The passage thus establishes that prophets are male humans.

Defining Tawhid and Ilm al-Kalam
Definition of ilm al-kalam with a critical note on the relation between science and beliefs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of ilm al-kalam as a science for proving religious beliefs and the logical tension raised by al-Ajhuri regarding the relationship between the science and the beliefs.

Core Idea
Kalam is a tool for proving beliefs, not the beliefs themselves—but that raises a puzzle.

In this passage, the author sets out the foundational definitions of tawhid and ilm al-kalam. He distinguishes the linguistic meaning of tawhid from its technical sense as the compiled discipline. Then he gives a formal definition of ilm al-kalam as a science that proves religious beliefs from certain proofs. However, a later commentator, al-Ajhuri, points out a logical problem: if kalam is separate from the beliefs, how can it prove them? This note alerts us to a deeper debate about the nature of theological knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the rhetorical opening
The author begins by explaining the phrase 'by tawhid' as referring to its seeking, and he notes that this is an example of barāʿat al-istihlāl, where the opening hints at the subject. Recognize this as a stylistic feature.
2
Distinguish linguistic vs technical meanings
Tawhid is defined linguistically as knowledge that something is one. Technically (sharʿan), it refers to the compiled discipline that will be explained. Note that the technical meaning aligns with ilm al-kalam.
3
Analyze the definition of ilm al-kalam and its critique
Read the formal definition: 'a science by which one is able to prove religious beliefs, acquired from its certain proofs.' Then consider al-Ajhuri's objection: if the science is distinct from the beliefs, it cannot prove them; if identical, it cannot be acquired from their proofs. Reflect on why this matters.
The critique is not meant to reject the definition but to highlight a subtle logical issue that scholars debated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِالتَّوْحِيدِ) أي : بطلبه، وفيه براعةُ استهلال؛ وهي أن يأتي المتكلم في طالعة كلامه بما يشعر بمقصوده. والتوحيد لغةٌ : العلم بأن الشيء واحد. وشرعاً بمعنى الفن المدوَّن (٥) فيما سيأتي : وهو علم يُقتدر به على إثباتِ العقائد الدينية مكتسب (٦) من أدلتها اليقينية (٧) . (۷) قوله : (وهو) علم (...) هذا يقتضي أن العلم غير العقائد، وأنه مكتسب من أدلة العقائد، والظاهر أن العلم هو نفس العقائد، فلا معنى لكونه يقتدر به على إثباتها، ولا معنى لاكتسابه من أدلتها مع المغايرة بينه وبينها فيلزم من اكتسابه من أدلتها .. أن يكون هو عين العقائد). انتهى أجهوري.
His saying: 'by al-tawhid' means by its seeking, and it contains a rhetorical opening (barāʿat al-istihlāl); which is that the speaker begins his speech with something that indicates his intended meaning. Al-tawhid linguistically is knowledge that something is one. And legally/technically in the sense of the compiled discipline (al-fann al-mudawwan) as will come: it is a science by which one is able to prove religious beliefs, acquired from its certain proofs (7). (7) His saying: 'It is a science...' This necessitates that the science is other than the beliefs, and that it is acquired from the proofs of the beliefs, yet apparently the science is the beliefs themselves, so there is no meaning to it being able to prove them, nor meaning to its being acquired from their proofs given the difference between it and them, so necessarily from its being acquired from their proofs... it would be identical to the beliefs). End of Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage provides definitions for two key terms: tawhid and ilm al-kalam. The author begins by explaining the phrase 'by tawhid' from the poem: it means 'by seeking it' (tawhid). He notes that this phrase constitutes a rhetorical device called barāʿat al-istihlāl, where the beginning of the speech hints at its subject. Then he defines tawhid linguistically: it is knowledge that something is one. Technically (sharʿan), tawhid refers to the compiled discipline (al-fann al-mudawwan) that will be explained later. That discipline is then defined: 'It is a science by which one is able to prove religious beliefs, acquired from its certain proofs.' This is the definition of ilm al-kalam. The author then includes a critical note from al-Ajhuri (in parentheses) that raises a potential circularity: if the science (al-kalam) is distinct from the beliefs, it cannot be used to prove them because it would be separate; but if it is identical to the beliefs, then it cannot be acquired from their proofs. Al-Ajhuri suggests that the definition implies the science is essentially the same as the beliefs, which would render the definition problematic. This note shows that the definition was debated. The passage thus establishes the technical meaning of tawhid as synonymous with ilm al-kalam and provides a formal definition, while also flagging a subtle logical issue.

Key Terms
توحيد
براعة استهلال
فن مدون
علم يقتدر به على إثبات العقائد الدينية
أدلة يقينية
العقائد الدينية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that ilm al-kalam is simply the set of religious beliefs themselves, rather than a tool for proving them.
Correction: The passage defines ilm al-kalam as a 'science by which one is able to prove religious beliefs,' indicating it is a method or discipline distinct from the beliefs it proves. Al-Ajhuri's critique further explores the implications of this distinction.

Checkpoint

A) The study of Allah's unity through speculative reasoning.
B) A science by which one is able to prove religious beliefs, acquired from certain proofs.
C) The knowledge of the Prophet's age at prophethood.
D) The linguistic meaning of tawhid.

Key Takeaway
Ilm al-kalam is a science for proving religious beliefs from certain proofs, but it is distinct from the beliefs themselves—a point that sparked debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about your faith, do you treat ilm al-kalam as a mere tool to confirm what you already believe, or do you genuinely allow it to challenge your assumptions? Honestly, which one are you more comfortable with?
?
The critical note from Ajhuri reveals a tension even among scholars about the nature of this science. Are you willing to sit with uncertainty and logical difficulties in your own understanding of Allah, or do you prefer neat answers that avoid complexity? What specific belief are you holding onto without a proper proof that you could re-examine today?

Two Definitions of Tawhid
Linguistic and technical definitions of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two definitions of tawhid: its simple linguistic meaning and its technical meaning as the science of kalam.

Core Idea
Tawhid means both 'oneness' and the science that proves religious beliefs.

This passage from the commentary on the poem introduces the key term 'tawhid'. The author first notes the rhetorical device of a good opening, then gives the linguistic and technical definitions. Understanding this distinction is essential before studying the rest of the poem.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how the author begins by commenting on the word 'bi-t-tawhīd' and then defines tawhid in two ways.
Pay attention to the phrase 'barā‘at istihlāl'.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the linguistic definition and the technical definition in the text. Notice the keywords 'lughatan' and 'shar‘an' that mark them.
3
Understand 'barā‘at istihlāl'
The author says this is a good opening. Reflect on why starting with the definition of the subject matter is rhetorically effective.
This device signals the speaker's intention from the outset.
4
Connect to 'ilm al-kalam
The technical definition describes a science that establishes religious beliefs from certain proofs. This is 'ilm al-kalam. Consider why it is important to have such a science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِالتَّوْحِيدِ) أي : بطلبه، وفيه براعةُ استهلال؛ وهي أن يأتي المتكلم في طالعة كلامه بما يشعر بمقصوده. والتوحيد لغةٌ : العلم بأن الشيء واحد. وشرعاً بمعنى الفن المدوَّن (٥) فيما سيأتي : وهو علم يُقتدر به على إثباتِ العقائد الدينية مكتسب (٦) من أدلتها اليقينية (٧) .
His statement: 'bi-t-tawhīd' means by seeking it, and in it there is a good opening; that is, the speaker brings in the beginning of his speech what indicates his intention. And tawhīd linguistically is knowledge that a thing is one. And according to the revealed law (shar‘an), meaning the codified discipline (al-fann al-mudawwan) that will come later: it is a science by which one is enabled to establish religious beliefs, acquired from their certain proofs.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the word 'bi-t-tawhīd' in the poem. The author first explains that it means 'by seeking it' and notes that this is a good opening (barā‘at istihlāl), where the speaker hints at his purpose from the start. Then he gives two definitions of tawhīd. The first is the linguistic definition: knowledge that a thing is one. The second is the legal/technical definition in the sense of the codified discipline that will be discussed later: it is a science that enables one to establish religious beliefs from certain proofs. This science is later identified as 'ilm al-kalam. The footnotes (5-7) refer to scholarly comments, but the core point is the distinction between the simple linguistic meaning and the technical meaning as a science.

The Legal Definition of Tawhid and Its Doctrinal Implications
Two definitions of tawhid shar'i and their doctrinal implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the legal definition of tawhid as singling out God with worship while affirming His absolute oneness in essence, attributes, and actions, and how this refutes both anthropomorphism and the stripping of attributes.

Core Idea
Tawhid is not just saying 'God is one' but affirming His unicity in all aspects while denying all forms of resemblance and division.

This lesson presents the core definition of tawhid from a legal (shar'i) perspective, distinguishing it from the mere verbal profession of oneness. The passage provides two complementary definitions that together safeguard the believer from two dangerous extremes: likening God to creation (tashbih) and stripping Him of His attributes (ta'til). Understanding these definitions is essential for a correct Islamic creed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overanalyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice that the passage contains two definitions, the second introduced by 'wa qeel'.
2
Identify the two definitions
The first definition is 'singling out the Worshipped One with worship along with belief in His oneness in essence, attributes, and actions.' The second is 'affirmation of an essence that does not resemble essences nor is stripped of attributes.'
The second definition explicitly refutes the Mu'tazilites.
3
Analyze the negations in the first definition
Note the negations: no essence resembles God's, indivisible in reality, mind, or supposition; no attributes resemble His; no multiplicity like two powers; no sharing in actions (creation belongs to God alone; others' acts are kash).
These negations protect tawhid from shirk and tashbih.
4
Understand the second definition and its target
The second definition affirms an essence that is neither like other essences (against tashbih) nor stripped of attributes (against ta'til). It directly opposes the Mu'tazilites who deny existential attributes.
This definition balances transcendence and affirmation of attributes.
5
Reflect on the purpose of these definitions
Ask yourself why the author gives two definitions. The first focuses on worship and belief, the second on the nature of God. Together they provide a complete picture of tawhid.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد به هنا الشرعي (١) ، لا بمعنى الفن المدوّن فيما سيأتي، وهو (٢) : إفراد المعبودِ بالعبادة مع اعتقادِ وحدته والتصديق بها ذاتاً وصفات وأفعالاً ؛ فليس هناك ذات تشبه ذاته تعالى ولا تقبل ذاته الانقسام لا فعلاً ولا وهماً ولا فرضاً مطابقاً للواقع (۳) ، ولا تشبه صفاته الصفاتِ، ولا تعدُّدَ فيها من جنس واحد؛ بأن يكون له تعالى قدرتان مثلاً ، ولا يدخل أفعاله الاشتراك ؛ إذ لا فعل لغيره سبحانه وتعالى خلقاً وإن نُسب إلى غيره كسباً (٤) وقيل (٥) : هو إثباتُ ذاتٍ غير مشبِهَةٍ للذواتِ ولا معطلة عن الصفات، خلافاً للمعتزلة المعطلين للذات عن الصفات الوجودية (٦) .
And what is intended by it here is the legal (shar'i) [definition], not in the sense of the codified discipline as will come later. And it is: singling out the Worshipped One with worship, along with belief in His oneness and affirming it in essence, attributes, and actions. So there is no essence that resembles His essence, may He be exalted, nor does His essence accept division—neither actually, nor mentally, nor by a supposition that corresponds to reality—nor do His attributes resemble attributes, nor is there multiplicity among them of one kind; such that He, may He be exalted, should have two powers, for example. Nor does sharing enter into His actions, for there is no action for other than Him, glorified and exalted, in terms of creation, even if it is attributed to others as acquisition (kash). And it is said: it is the affirmation of an essence that does not resemble essences nor is stripped of attributes, contrary to the Mu'tazilites who strip the essence of existential attributes.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines the legal meaning of tawhid as distinct from the discipline of kalam. The author gives two definitions: first, the standard definition from the poem (singling out God with worship while affirming oneness in essence, attributes, and actions). Then he expands: God's essence has no likeness, is indivisible (physically, mentally, or by a real supposition), His attributes are not like others, there is no multiplicity within one kind (e.g., two powers), and His actions are exclusively His creation; others' acts are only 'acquisition' (kasb). The second definition (from Sheikh Abd al-Salam) emphasizes affirming an essence that is neither anthropomorphic (tashbih) nor stripped of attributes (ta'til), contrary to the Mu'tazila who deny existential attributes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tawhid is merely saying 'God is one' or 'There is no god but Allah' without understanding the implications for God's essence, attributes, and actions.
Correction: True tawhid requires not only linguistic profession but also affirming His oneness in essence (no parts, no resemblance), attributes (no likeness, no multiplicity), and actions (creation is solely His). It also negates both anthropomorphism and the stripping of attributes.

Checkpoint

C) God's essence is composed of parts.

Key Takeaway
Legal tawhid requires worshiping God alone while affirming His absolute uniqueness and transcendence in essence, attributes, and actions, and rejecting both anthropomorphism and the stripping of attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim to worship God alone, but do you also affirm that His essence is utterly unlike anything you can imagine? When you think of God, do you unconsciously picture something familiar? What will you do today to purify your concept of God from any trace of resemblance?
?
The Mu'tazilites denied God's attributes out of a desire to protect His oneness. Where have you been tempted to deny or downplay certain divine attributes (like His anger, His mercy in judgment) because they made you uncomfortable? How will you submit to the full revelation of His attributes this week?
?

In your heart, did you truly attribute that success to God alone, or did you see yourself as the real creator? What would it look like to live with the constant awareness that every good action is from Him?

Why the Poet Specified Tawhid
The author answers an objection about why the poet specified tawhid rather than other duties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reason why the poet of Jawharat al-Tawhid focused exclusively on tawhid despite mentioning other duties.

Core Idea
Tawhid is the noblest act of worship, so it deserves special emphasis.

The poem Jawharat al-Tawhid is devoted to the subject of tawhid, but it also references other religious duties. This might raise a question: why single out tawhid when other obligations are equally important? The author anticipates this objection and provides a clear answer grounded in a prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first the objection (فإن قيل), then the answer (أجيب).
2
Identify the core argument
The answer states that tawhid is the noblest act of worship (أشرف العبادات). Reflect on what 'noblest' means in terms of priority.
The word 'أشرف' implies superior honor and rank.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
The author quotes a hadith from Abu Sa'id stating that nothing better than tawhid and prayer was prescribed. Consider why prayer is mentioned and how it supports the nobility of tawhid.
The hadith also implies that if anything were better, it would have been prescribed for angels.
4
Understand the justification
Connect the hadith to the poet's choice: since tawhid is the best of deeds, it merits exclusive focus in a poem dedicated to theological foundations.
The poet's choice is not arbitrary but based on religious precedence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قد جاء بغير التوحيد، فلِمَ اقتصر الناظم على التوحيد؟ أجيب بأنه خصَّهُ لأنه أشرفُ العبادات، ويليه الصلاة؛ كما في حديث أبي سعيد: إن الله تعالى لم يفرض شيئاً أفضل من التوحيد والصلاة، ولو كان شيء أفضل منه، لافترضَهُ على ملائكته؛ منهم رائع ومنهم ساجد.
For it is said: He came with other than tawhid, so why did the poet confine himself to tawhid? The answer is that he specified it because it is the noblest of acts of worship, followed by prayer; as in the hadith of Abu Sa'id: 'Indeed, God Almighty did not prescribe anything more excellent than tawhid and prayer, and if anything were more excellent than it, He would have prescribed it for His angels; among them are those who bow and those who prostrate.'
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage addresses a potential objection: since the poem (Jawharat al-Tawhid) is about tawhid but also mentions other matters, why focus only on tawhid? The answer is that tawhid is the noblest act of worship, followed by prayer, as supported by a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (or similar) stating that God prescribed nothing better than tawhid and prayer; if any act were better, He would have obligated it upon the angels. Thus, the poet's emphasis is justified.

Correction: The poet's focus is justified because tawhid is the noblest act of worship, as confirmed by a prophetic hadith.

Definition and Subject Matter of Ilm al-Kalam
The author defines ilm al-kalam and enumerates its subject matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of ilm al-kalam and its four subject areas as taught in the classical tradition.

Core Idea
Ilm al-kalam is not just about God; it also covers prophethood, the world as evidence, and revealed beliefs.

In this foundational passage, the author establishes both what ilm al-kalam means and what it studies. He first reminds us of the earlier definition, then lays out the four domains that constitute its subject. This gives us a roadmap for everything that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Notice that the author says the definition is 'what preceded' – he refers to the poem's earlier lines. This reminds us that kalam is an ongoing conversation.
2
Identify the four subjects
List the four components: God's essence, prophets, contingent beings, and sam'iyyat.
3
Understand the three judgments for God and prophets
For God and prophets, kalam considers what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for them.
These are the three rational judgments.
4
See the role of the contingent
The contingent (possible things) are studied as evidence leading to the existence of the Creator.
This is the cosmological argument.
5
Define sam'iyyat
These are matters from revelation that must be believed, like Paradise and Hell.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فحد هذا الفنّ لغة وشرعاً ما تقدَّم. وموضوعُهُ : ذاتُ اللهِ من حيث ما يجب له وما يستحيل وما يجوز، وذاتُ الرُّسل كذلك، والممكنُ من حيث إنه يُتوصل به إلى وجود صانعه، والسمعيات من حيث اعتقادها.
The definition of this art, linguistically and legally, is what preceded. And its subject: the essence of God in terms of what is necessary for Him, what is impossible, and what is permissible; and the essence of the messengers likewise; and the contingent thing in terms of its being a means to arrive at the existence of its Maker; and the sam'iyyat (audible matters) in terms of belief in them.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines ilm al-kalam and specifies its subject matter. The author begins by stating that the definition of this discipline, both in language (lughatan) and in revealed law (shar'an), is what was previously mentioned (referring back to the opening of the poem). Then he lists the fourfold subject: (1) God's essence considered in terms of what is necessary, impossible, and permissible for Him; (2) the essence of the prophets likewise; (3) the possible contingent being (al-mumkin) as a means of proving the existence of its Creator (i.e., using the world as evidence); and (4) the sam'iyyat (matters heard from revelation) in terms of belief in them. This classification covers theology, prophethood, natural theology, and eschatology.

واجب

جائز
الرسل
الممكن
السمعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ilm al-kalam only deals with God.
Correction: It also deals with prophets, the contingent world as evidence, and revealed matters (sam'iyyat).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ilm al-kalam studies God, prophets, the contingent world as proof for God, and revealed beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Fruit and Virtue of Ilm al-Kalam
The author states the fruit of kalam and explains its superior virtue.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the fruit of ilm al-kalam is both intellectual (knowledge of God by decisive proofs) and spiritual (eternal happiness), and its virtue stems from its noble subject matter.

Core Idea
Kalam's fruit is knowledge and eternal bliss; its virtue is being the noblest science.

Every science has a fruit and a virtue. The fruit is what you gain from studying it, and the virtue is why it is superior. In this passage, the author of the Jawharah al-Tawhid states both for ilm al-kalam. Let's read his concise yet profound words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get the literal meaning.
Notice the grammatical point the author makes at the end.
2
Identify the twofold fruit
The fruit has two components: knowledge of God through decisive proofs and attaining eternal happiness. Notice how both are mentioned together.
3
Understand the virtue argument
The virtue is that kalam is the noblest science. The reason is that nobility derives from the subject matter. Since kalam's subject is God and His messengers, it is the most noble.
The author reinforces this with a grammar point: the 'related thing' is honored by the 'thing related to'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثمرته : معرفة الله بالبراهين القطعية، والفوز بالسعادة الأبدية. وفضله : أنه أشرفُ العلوم ؛ لكونه متعلقاً بذاته تعالى وذاتِ رسله وما يتبع ذلك، والمتعلّق - بكسر اللام - يشرف بشرف المتعلَّق - بفتحها -.
And its fruit: knowledge of God by decisive proofs, and attaining eternal happiness. And its virtue: that it is the noblest of sciences, because it is related (mut'allaq) to His essence, Exalted, and the essence of His messengers and what follows that; and the related thing (al-mut'allaq) derives nobility from the nobility of the thing related to (al-mut'allaq bihi).
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage explains the fruit and virtue of kalam. The fruit (thamara) is twofold: (1) knowledge of God through definitive proofs (al-barahin al-qat'iyya), and (2) attaining everlasting happiness (al-sa'ada al-abadiyya) in the Hereafter. The virtue (fadl) is that it is the most noble of sciences. The reason is that nobility derives from the subject; since the subject is the essence of God and His messengers, the science that studies them is the noblest. The author makes a grammatical point: the 'related thing' (al-mut'allaq, with kasra on the lam) is honored by the 'thing related to' (al-mut'allaq bihi, with fatha). This reinforces that the honor of a science derives from its subject.

متعلق
متعلق به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the fruit of kalam is merely intellectual understanding without spiritual benefit.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the fruit includes both knowledge (intellectual) and eternal happiness (spiritual reward).

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Ilm al-kalam is the noblest science because its subject is the essence of Allah and His messengers, and its fruit is both knowledge of God and eternal happiness.

Kalam as the Root of Religious Sciences
Author states kalam's primacy over other sciences and cites a poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why kalam is considered the foundational root of all religious sciences and how a classical poem reinforces this primacy.

Core Idea
Kalam is the root; all other religious sciences are its branches.

In this lesson, we explore a concise yet powerful statement from the author about the relative status of kalam. He declares it the root (asl) of all religious sciences, with other sciences being branches (far'). To drive the point home, he cites a beautiful poem that criticizes those who study fiqh but neglect kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Notice the clear declaration and the poetic citation.
The poem is in the meter al-khafif.
2
Understand the key concepts: root vs branch
The passage states that kalam is the 'asl' (root/principal) and other sciences are 'far' (branches). Grasp this hierarchy.
This establishes kalam as the source from which other sciences derive.
3
Analyze the poem's message
The poem says 'every science is a servant to the science of kalam' and criticizes those who seek fiqh to correct rulings but neglect 'the Revealer of rulings'.
The poem emphasizes the necessity of kalam over even fiqh.
4
Reflect on the implication
Understand that kalam is not just one science among many; it is the foundation. Without kalam, other religious sciences lack their basis.
This prioritization is a key point in the study of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونسبته : أنه أصلُ العلوم الدينيَّة وما سواه فرع عنه، وما أحسن قول القائل : [من الخفيف] أَيُّهَا المُقْتَدِي لِتَطْلُبَ عِلْماً كُلُّ عِلْمٍ عَبْدٌ لِعِلْمِ الكَلَامِ تَطْلُبُ الفِقْهَ كَي تُصَحِّحَ حُكْماً ثُمَّ أَغْفَلْتَ مُنْزِلَ الأَحْكَامِ
And its relation: that it is the root of the religious sciences, and other than it is a branch from it. How excellent is the saying of the poet: [in the meter al-khafif] 'O you who follow [the path] seeking knowledge, every science is a servant to the science of kalam. You seek fiqh to correct a ruling, then you neglect the Revealer of rulings.'
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage describes the relative status (nisba) of kalam to other sciences. It is the fundamental root (asl) of all religious sciences, and all others are branches (far') upon it. To illustrate, the author quotes a poem in the meter al-khafif. The poem addresses the one who seeks knowledge, stating that every other science is a servant to kalam. It specifically criticizes those who study fiqh (jurisprudence) to correct rulings but neglect the source of rulings, i.e., kalam, which provides the foundational knowledge of God and His attributes. The poem emphasizes the necessity of kalam over even the study of fiqh.

Key Terms

الخفيف
الكلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fiqh is the most important religious science because it deals with practical rulings.
Correction: The poem shows that kalam is the root and fiqh is a branch serving kalam. Neglecting kalam while studying fiqh is like missing the source of rulings (the Revealer). Kalam is foundational; without it, other sciences are incomplete.

Checkpoint

A) They are all equal.
B) They are all branches of kalam.
C) They are all servants to kalam.
D) They are all independent.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is the root science; without it, other religious sciences lack a foundation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study deen, do you find yourself focusing more on acquiring rulings (fiqh) without investing time in knowing the One who revealed those rulings? What would it look like for you to prioritize kalam as the 'root' in your personal learning journey?
?
The poet says every science is a servant to kalam. If you truly believed that, how would your attitude toward studying theology change? Are you treating kalam as a servant to your own interests instead?
?
Neglecting the Revealer while busy with the rulings — does this describe your approach to any area of your life? Write down one concrete step you will take this week to turn your attention back to Allah as the Source of everything you seek to learn.

The Founders of Ilm al-Kalam: Ash'ari and Maturidi
The author identifies the founders of kalam as Ash'ari and Maturidi and defines their role.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand who the founders of ilm al-kalam are, what their role was in codifying and defending tawhid, and why they did not invent a new belief.

Core Idea
The founders of kalam codified and defended tawhid, not invented it.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Murid identifies the two main founders of ilm al-kalam: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi. It clarifies their role as compilers and defenders of the science against Mu'tazila doubts, while emphasizing that the essential truth of tawhid is prophetic and pre-dates them. Understanding this distinction prevents misunderstanding about the nature of kalam as a discipline.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the structure: first the naming, then the explanation, then the qualification.
2
Identify the founders and their role
The passage names two founders and their followers. The word 'wadi'' means 'founder' or 'originator' in a specific sense. Look at how the author explains this: they compiled books and refuted doubts.
The Mu'tazila are mentioned as the group whose doubts were refuted.
3
Understand the qualification about tawhid
The author immediately clarifies that tawhid was not invented by these founders. It was brought by every prophet. Ask yourself: Why is this clarification important?
This prevents the misunderstanding that kalam introduced a new religion.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this passage frames ilm al-kalam as a defensive and organizational science, not a source of new doctrine. This is key to understanding the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools.
The founders are seen as renewers and systematizers, not innovators in belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وواضعه أبو الحسن الأشعري ومَنْ تبعَهُ، وأبو منصور الماتريدي ومَنْ تبعه؛ بمعنى: أنهم دوّنوا كتبه وردُّوا الشُّبة التي أوردتها المعتزلة، وإلا فالتوحيد جاءَ به كل نبي من لدن آدم إلى يوم القيامة.
And its founder: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and those who followed him, and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi and those who followed him. Meaning: they compiled its books and refuted the doubts that the Mu'tazila had brought forth. Otherwise, tawhid (monotheism) was brought by every prophet from Adam until the Day of Resurrection.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage identifies the founders (wadi') of ilm al-kalam as a formal discipline. Two major figures are named: Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari (d. 324 AH) and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi (d. 333 AH), along with their respective followers. The author clarifies that 'founder' here means they organized the science into written works and systematically refuted the doubts (shubah) raised by the Mu'tazila. The author immediately qualifies this by stating that the essential content of tawhid (monotheism) was not invented by them; rather, it was brought by every prophet from Adam to Muhammad. Thus, the role of these imams was to codify and defend the existing truth, not to originate a new belief.

Key Terms

أبو الحسن الأشعري
أبو منصور الماتريدي
المعتزلة
التوحيد
شبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ash'ari and Maturidi invented a new religion or introduced innovative theological doctrines.
Correction: The passage clarifies that they only compiled books and refuted doubts, while tawhid itself was brought by every prophet.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by calling Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari and Abu Mansur al-Maturidi the 'founders' of ilm al-kalam?
A) They invented the belief in tawhid.
B) They were the first to speak about God.
C) They compiled books and refuted the Mu'tazila.
D) They introduced new theological doctrines.

Key Takeaway
The founders of kalam systematized and defended the existing doctrine of tawhid, not invented it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that the scholars of kalam were adding to the religion? How does knowing that their work was solely a defense of the same tawhid taught by every prophet reshape your gratitude toward them? Will you make it a habit to thank Allah for such defenders of the faith?
?
The passage says tawhid was brought by every prophet from Adam until the Day of Resurrection. Does your heart truly embrace that tawhid is the unchanging core of all divine messages, or do you sometimes treat it as just one belief among many? If it is that central, what are you willing to sacrifice today to live it more fully?
?
The founders of kalam faced doubts and refuted them. What doubts about tawhid do you secretly entertain or fail to confront? Are you willing to write down the one doubt that troubles you most and actively seek its refutation from the Qur'an and the scholars who defended this path?

The Name of the Science of Kalam: Ilm al-Tawhid
Sanusi names the science and lists early Salaf who engaged in kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary name of the science (ilm al-tawhid) and its continuity from the time of the Companions through the early scholars.

Core Idea
Kalam is not an innovation; its roots lie with the Companions and early Salaf.

The author begins by stating that the science is called 'the science of tawhid' because tawhid is its noblest subject. He then immediately demonstrates that its practice goes back to the Companions and the early Salaf, listing prominent figures who engaged in refuting doubts. This sets the stage for the later defense of kalam as a legitimate and continuous tradition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the name and early practitioners
Read the Arabic and translation for this part. Notice that the author gives two names and then lists earlier scholars who practiced kalam.
The list includes Ali, Ibn Abbas, Ibn Mas'ud, and al-Hasan al-Basri.
2
Identify the two names of the science
The first name is 'ilm al-tawhid. What is implied second name? (It is 'ilm al-kalam). The author uses 'also called' to indicate another name.
3
Reflect on the historical continuity
Why does the author list early Salaf? He is establishing that kalam is not a later innovation but practiced by the best generations.
This is a key apologetic move.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واسمه : علم التوحيد ؛ لأن مبحث الوحدانية أشرف مباحثه، ويُسمى أيضاً : لدن الصحابة الكرام إلى زمنهما ممن تصدَّى لرد الشبه وتفريع القول؛ كسيدنا علي وابن عباس بن مسعود وغيرهم رضي الله عنهم أجمعين، وكذا أئمة الهدى من أعلام السلف كالحسن البصري الذي مزج بين علم الإيمان وعلم الرضوان ؛ إذ ليس كل من وجد في عصر السلف إماماً يُقتدى به، فإنما وُجدت رؤوس الفرق الضالة في القرون الأولى، وهكذا إلى أن وصلت النوبة إليهما، وانظر ترجمات أعلام أهل السنة من كتاب الحافظ ابن عساكر تبيين كذب المفتري». وقال العلامة المحقق ابن حجر الهيتمي في فتاواه الحديثية ص (۲۰۸) عن علم الكلام: (وما قيل : إنه بدعةٌ لأنه لم ينظر فيه السلف، مع أنه يُورث المراء والجدال والشبهات .. رُدَّ بأنه نظر فيه السلف قطعاً، منهم عمر وابنه وعلي وابن عباس رضي الله عنهم، ومن التابعين عمر بن عبد العزيز وربيعة وابن هرمز ومالك والشافعي رضي الله عنهم، وألف مالك رضي الله عنه فيه رسالة قبل أن يولد الشافعي - كذا ، ولعله الأشعري - رضي الله عنه، وإنما نُسب للأشعري لأنه بيَّنَ مناهج الأوَّلين، ولخص موارد البراهين، ولم يحدث فيه بعد السلف إلا مجرد الألقاب والاصطلاحات، وقد حدث مثل ذلك في كل فن من فنون العلم، والقول بأن السلف نُهوا عن النظر فيه باطل، وإنما الذي نهوا عنه علم الجهمية والقدرية وغيرهم من أهل البدع، وهم الذين ذمَّهم الشافعي وغيره من السلف. لطيفة : قال الإمام القشيري - وهو من أعلام المتكلمين، ومن تلامذة الأستاذ ابن فورك والإمام الرباني أبي علي الدقاق، وهما من تلامذة الإمام علم السنة أبي الحسن الباهلي، وهو أبرز تلامذة شيخ السنة أبي الحسن الأشعري - في الرسالة القشيرية» (٣٤/١) : (اعلموا - رحمكم الله تعالى - أن المسلمين بعد رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لم يتسم أفاضلهم في عصرهم بتسمية علم سوى صحبة رسول الله إذ لا فضيلة فوقها، فقيل لهم الصحابة، ولما أدركهم أهل العصر الثاني .. سمّى مَن صحب الصحابة : التابعين، ورأوا ذلك أشرف سمةٍ، ثم قيل لمن بعدهم : أتباع التابعين، ثم اختلف الناسُ، وتباينت المراتب ، فقيل لخواص الناس ممن لهم شدَّة عناية بأمر الدين: الزماد والعباد، ثم ظهرت البدعُ، وحصل التداعي بين الفرق فكلُّ فريق ادعوا أن فيهم زهداً، فانفرد خواص أهل السنة المراعون أنفاسَهُم مع الله تعالى الحافظون قلوبهم عن طوارق الغفلة باسم التصوُّف، واشتهر هذا الاسم لهؤلاء الأكابر قبل المئتين من الهجرة)، وقد نقل الإمام الشاطبي هذا النص في الاعتصام (۱۲۰/١) وقال عقبه : (فقد عُدَّ هذا اللقب مخصوصاً باتباع السنة ومباينة البدعة، وفي ذلك ما يدلُّ على خلاف ما يعتقده الجهَّال ومن لا عبرة به من المدعين للعلم).
And its name: the science of tawhid, because the topic of oneness is the noblest of its topics. It is also called: from the time of the noble Companions until their era, those who undertook to refute doubts and elaborate discourse: such as our master Ali, Ibn Abbas, Ibn Mas'ud, and others, may Allah be pleased with them all; likewise the imams of guidance from the prominent Salaf, like al-Hasan al-Basri who blended the science of faith and the science of divine pleasure. For not everyone found in the era of the Salaf was an imam to be followed; rather, the heads of the deviant sects existed in the early centuries. And so it continued until the turn reached them (Ash'ari and Maturidi). And look at the biographies of the Ahl al-Sunna luminaries from the book of al-Hafiz Ibn 'Asakir, 'Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari'. And the great scholar Ibn Hajar al-Haytami said in his Fatawa Hadithiyya (p. 208) about kalam: 'What is said that it is an innovation because the Salaf did not look into it, while it is said that it leads to contention, debate, and doubts — the response is that the Salaf definitely looked into it, among them Umar and his son, Ali, Ibn Abbas, and from the Tabi'un, Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz, Rabi'a, Ibn Hurmuz, Malik, al-Shafi'i; and Malik wrote a treatise on it before al-Shafi'i was born — so it is said, perhaps it was al-Ash'ari. It is attributed to al-Ash'ari only because he clarified the methodologies of the predecessors and summarized the sources of proofs; nothing occurred after the Salaf except mere titles and terminologies, as happened in every field of knowledge. The claim that the Salaf were forbidden from examining it is false; rather, they were forbidden from the theology of the Jahmiyya, Qadariyya, and other people of innovation, and it is these whom al-Shafi'i and other Salaf condemned.' A gem: Imam al-Qushayri said in al-Risala al-Qushayriyya (1/34): 'Know, may Allah have mercy on you, that the Muslims after the Messenger of Allah did not designate their virtuous ones with a name besides the companionship of the Messenger, so they were called the Companions. When the people of the second generation came, they called those who accompanied the Companions the Followers, and they considered that the most honorable designation. Then they called those after them the Successors. Then people differed and ranks varied, and so the elite among the people of strong care for religion were called the ascetics and worshippers. Then innovations appeared and contention among sects occurred, so each sect claimed that their members had zuhd. Thus the elite of Ahl al-Sunna who were mindful of their breaths with Allah and guarded their hearts from distractions distinguished themselves with the name 'tasawwuf', and this name became famous for these greats before the year 200 AH.' Al-Shatibi transmitted this text in al-I'tisam (1/120) and said after it: 'This title has been considered specific to followers of the Sunna and distinguishing from innovation, and in that is evidence contrary to what the ignorant and those of no account claim of knowledge.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This long passage covers two main points: the name of the science and a historical defense of its legitimacy. First, the author states that the science is called 'ilm al-tawhid' because tawhid is its noblest topic, implying other names like 'ilm al-kalam' are also used. Then the text transitions into a dense historical argument. It begins by listing early Salaf such as Ali, Ibn Abbas, Ibn Mas'ud, and al-Hasan al-Basri as practitioners of kalam-like refutations. It explains that not everyone from the Salaf was an imam; deviant sects existed early on. The role of Ash'ari and Maturidi was to systematize. Then a long quote from Ibn Hajar al-Haytami refutes the claim that kalam is an innovation (bid'a) because the Salaf did not practice it. Ibn Hajar lists companions and tabi'un who did engage in it, including Malik. He notes that only new terminologies were added, as in any science. He asserts that the Salaf forbade only the theology of the Jahmiyya and Qadariyya. Finally, a 'lathifa' from al-Qushayri about the emergence of the term 'sufi' is cited, which al-Shatibi uses to show that the name 'sufi' is specific to Ahl al-Sunna and distinguishes them from innovation. This entire section defends the science and its practitioners.

Key Terms
اسم
علم التوحيد
الصحابة
السلف
الحسن البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The science of kalam, properly called 'ilm al-tawhid, has roots in the earliest generations of Islam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage warns that not everyone from the Salaf era was an imam to be followed. How do you discern whom to take your religion from? Are you careful about your sources of knowledge, or do you passively accept whatever is popular?
?
Al-Hasan al-Basri blended the science of faith with the science of divine pleasure. Is your own understanding of faith merely intellectual, or does it move your heart toward longing for Allah's pleasure? What concrete step can you take this week to make your faith more than just knowledge?

The Historical Defense of Kalam: Refuting the Innovation Claim
Ibn Hajar al-Haytami refutes the claim that kalam is a bid'a.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Hajar al-Haytami refutes the claim that kalam is an innovation (bid'a) by citing Salaf who practiced it and showing that only new terminologies were added.

Core Idea
Kalam is not a bid'a; the Salaf engaged in it, and what is called 'kalam' today is a systematization of their method.

This passage presents a powerful defense of kalam against those who label it a blameworthy innovation. Ibn Hajar al-Haytami lists numerous Companions and Successors who engaged in kalam, including Umar, Ali, Ibn Abbas, Malik, and al-Shafi'i. He explains that the science is only attributed to al-Ash'ari because he systematized it, not because he invented it. The passage also includes a beautiful anecdote from al-Qushayri about the emergence of the term 'sufi,' showing that such labels are natural in the development of Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the quote from Ibn Hajar al-Haytami
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. He is responding to the accusation that kalam is a bid'a.
Note the list of Salaf he provides.
2
Analyze the three arguments in the quote
Ibn Hajar makes three points: 1) The Salaf did engage in kalam. 2) What Ash'ari did was only clarify and summarize. 3) The prohibition was against Jahmiyya, not kalam. Identify each.
The third point is crucial.
3
Read the lathifa from al-Qushayri
This is a side note but reinforces the idea that terms like 'sufi' emerged to distinguish Ahl al-Sunna from innovation. How does this support the defense?
Al-Shatibi's comment shows that the term 'sufi' is a mark of following the Sunna.

It is also called: from the time of the noble Companions until their era, those who undertook to refute doubts and elaborate discourse: such as our master Ali, Ibn Abbas, Ibn Mas'ud, and others, may Allah be pleased with them all; likewise the imams of guidance from the prominent Salaf, like al-Hasan al-Basri who blended the science of faith and the science of divine pleasure. The claim that the Salaf were forbidden from examining it is false; rather, they were forbidden from the theology of the Jahmiyya, Qadariyya, and other people of innovation, and it is these whom al-Shafi'i and other Salaf condemned.' When the people of the second generation came, they called those who accompanied the Companions the Followers, and they considered that the most honorable designation. Then they called those after them the Successors. Then people differed and ranks varied, and so the elite among the people of strong care for religion were called the ascetics and worshippers. Then innovations appeared and contention among sects occurred, so each sect claimed that their members had zuhd. Thus the elite of Ahl al-Sunna who were mindful of their breaths with Allah and guarded their hearts from distractions distinguished themselves with the name 'tasawwuf', and this name became famous for these greats before the year 200 AH.' Al-Shatibi transmitted this text in al-I'tisam (1/120) and said after it: 'This title has been considered specific to followers of the Sunna and distinguishing from innovation, and in that is evidence contrary to what the ignorant and those of no account claim of knowledge.'
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This long passage covers two main points: the name of the science and a historical defense of its legitimacy. First, the author states that the science is called 'ilm al-tawhid' because tawhid is its noblest topic, implying other names like 'ilm al-kalam' are also used. Then the text transitions into a dense historical argument. It begins by listing early Salaf such as Ali, Ibn Abbas, Ibn Mas'ud, and al-Hasan al-Basri as practitioners of kalam-like refutations. It explains that not everyone from the Salaf was an imam; deviant sects existed early on. The role of Ash'ari and Maturidi was to systematize. Then a long quote from Ibn Hajar al-Haytami refutes the claim that kalam is an innovation (bid'a) because the Salaf did not practice it. Ibn Hajar lists companions and tabi'un who did engage in it, including Malik. He notes that only new terminologies were added, as in any science. He asserts that the Salaf forbade only the theology of the Jahmiyya and Qadariyya. Finally, a 'lathifa' from al-Qushayri about the emergence of the term 'sufi' is cited, which al-Shatibi uses to show that the name 'sufi' is specific to Ahl al-Sunna and distinguishes them from innovation. This entire section defends the science and its practitioners.

Key Terms
ابن حجر الهيتمي
بدعة
السلف
القشيري
التصوف
السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Kalam is a blameworthy innovation (bid'a) because the Salaf never engaged in it and it leads to arguments and doubts.
Correction: Ibn Hajar shows the Salaf did engage in it, and the only prohibited theology is that of the Jahmiyya and Qadariyya.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Hajar al-Haytami, why is kalam attributed to al-Ash'ari?
A) Because al-Ash'ari invented the science.
B) Because he clarified the methodologies of the predecessors and summarized proofs.
C) Because he was the first to write on kalam.
D) Because he opposed the Salaf.

Key Takeaway
Kalam is not a bid'a; it is a continuation of the method of the Salaf, and its attribution to Ash'ari is due to systematization.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever dismissed a branch of Islamic knowledge (like kalam, fiqh usul, or tasawwuf) as an innovation without verifying whether the Salaf actually engaged in its principles? What might that reveal about your attachment to the truth versus your comfort with familiar labels? Before you judge again, will you commit to studying the actual sources and seeking guidance from qualified scholars?
?
The evolution of titles from 'Sahabi' to 'Sufi' shows that later terms can carry legitimate meanings. Is there a term or group you have automatically rejected because it seemed new? How much of that rejection is based on personal bias rather than sound evidence? Will you ask Allah to soften your heart and grant you the humility to learn before condemning?
?
Ibn Hajar states that the Salaf forbade only the theology of the Jahmiyya and Qadariyya, not kalam itself. Have you ever mistakenly conflated a general category (like 'theology') with specific deviant forms, thereby closing your mind to an entire field of beneficial knowledge? What will you do today to repent of that error and reopen the door to learning that Allah has not closed?

The Names of the Science: Tawhid and Kalam
The commentator states the two names of the science and explains the reason for the name 'ilm al-kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two primary names of the science of Islamic theology and the reasoning behind the name 'ilm al-kalam.

Core Idea
The science is called 'ilm al-tawhid for its noblest topic, and 'ilm al-kalam due to its method or central controversy.

Before diving into the doctrines themselves, it is essential to know what we are studying and why it is called what it is. This passage from the commentary clarifies the two most common names of the science—'ilm al-tawhid and 'ilm al-kalam—and provides reasons for the latter, setting the stage for the theological discussions to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Then read the translation. Notice the two names mentioned.
2
Understand the first name
The science is called 'ilm al-tawhid because the investigation of divine oneness (al-wahdaniyya) is the noblest of its topics.
This highlights the central importance of tawhid in Islamic theology.
3
Understand the second name
The science is also called 'ilm al-kalam for one of two reasons: either because early scholars introduced topics by saying 'discourse (al-kalam) concerning...', or because of the extensive debate over God's attribute of speech (al-kalam).
The second reason connects the name to a famous theological controversy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واسمه : علم التوحيد ; لأن مبحث الوحدانية أشرف (۱) مباحثه، ويُسمى أيضاً : علم الكلام ; لأن المتقدمين كانوا يقولون في الترجمة عن مباحثه : الكلام في كذا، أو لأنه قد كثر الاختلاف في مسألة الكلام، وذكر بعضهم أن له ثمانية أسماء (١) .
And its name: the science of tawhid (علم التوحيد), because the topic of oneness is the noblest of its topics. And it is also called: the science of kalam (علم الكلام), because the early scholars used to say in the introduction to its topics: 'The discourse (الكلام) about such-and-such', or because differences abounded concerning the topic of the Divine Speech (الكلام). And some mentioned that it has eight names.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage identifies the two primary names of the science—'ilm al-tawhid and 'ilm al-kalam—and explains the reason for the latter. The author first states that it is named 'ilm al-tawhid because the investigation of divine oneness (al-wahdaniyya) is the most noble of its subjects. He then notes that it is also called 'ilm al-kalam, and gives two possible reasons: either because early scholars would say 'discussion (al-kalam) concerning such-and-such topic' when referring to its subjects, or because extensive disagreement occurred regarding the attribute of divine speech (al-kalam). Finally, he adds that some scholars have mentioned that the science has eight names in total, implying that there are other less common names.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Some think 'ilm al-kalam is named after the human act of speaking.
Correction: The passage gives two reasons: either the method of discussion or the attribute of God's speech, not human speech.

Key Takeaway
The science of Islamic theology is known as both 'ilm al-tawhid (focus on God's oneness) and 'ilm al-kalam (from the method of discussion or the topic of God's speech).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you giving tawhid the place of honor it deserves, or is it crowded out by lesser concerns? Commit today to a specific practice that reminds you of Allah's oneness several times a day.
?

Sources, Ruling, and Subject Matters of Kalam
The commentator defines the sources, legal ruling, and subject matters of ilm al-kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sources, legal ruling, and subject matters of ilm al-kalam as defined in classical Sunni theology.

Core Idea
Kalam combines reason and revelation and is an obligation on every Muslim.

Ilm al-Kalam is not just a speculative discipline; it has a firm basis in both reason and revelation. This passage defines its foundations: where its proofs come from, who must learn it, and what questions it investigates. Let's explore these essential principles as laid out by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the three main components
The passage covers sources (al-adilla), ruling (al-hukm), and subject matters (al-masa'il). Find each part.
The fourth sentence explains the term 'muqaddimat al-'ilm'.
3
Understand the legal ruling
The ruling is 'wajib 'ayni' (individual obligation). Reflect on why this is significant for every Muslim.
This counters the misconception that kalam is only for scholars.
4
Grasp the three categories
The subject matters are the necessary, possible, and impossible. These are the core of kalam's investigations.
5
Reflect on the introduction
The passage says these principles are called 'muqaddimat al-'ilm'. Think about why foundational knowledge is essential before studying the science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستمداده من الأدلة العقلية والنقلية. وحكم الشارع فيه : الوجوب العيني على كل مكلف من ذكر وأنثى (٢) . ومسائله : قضاياه الباحثة عن الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات (٣) وهذه المبادئ هي التي تُسمى مقدمة العلم؛ لأنها اسم لمعان يتوقف عليها الشروع في المقصود.
And its sources are the intellectual and transmitted proofs. And its ruling in the Shari'a is: individual obligation (al-wujub al-'ayni) upon every legally responsible person, male or female. And its subject matters: its propositions investigating the necessary, the possible, and the impossible. And these principles are what are called the introduction to the science, because it is a name for meanings upon which the entry into the intended purpose depends.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage gives a concise definition of the science of kalam. First, it states that the sources (istimdad) of this science are both rational and transmitted proofs, meaning that it uses both intellectual reasoning and revelation. Second, it declares that the legal ruling (hukm al-shari') regarding its acquisition is an individual obligation (wajib 'ayni) on every mature Muslim, male and female. This underscores that basic creedal knowledge is mandatory for all. Third, it defines the subject matters (masa'il) of the science as propositions that investigate three categories: the necessary (al-wajibat), the possible (al-ja'izat), and the impossible (al-mustahilat). These three categories are fundamental to kalam discussions about God and the world. Finally, it explains that these introductory concepts are called the 'introduction to the science' (muqaddimat al-'ilm) because they are a name for meanings on which the start of the intended purpose depends; in other words, they are the foundational principles without which the science cannot be properly studied.

Introduction to the Science of Kalam
Definition of kalam with names, sources, ruling, and three issues categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the science of kalam is, why it is so named, its sources, its ruling as an individual obligation, and the three categories of issues it investigates.

Core Idea
Kalam studies what is necessary, possible, and impossible about Allah using reason and revelation.

The author begins by defining the science of kalam and explaining the origin of its name. He then clarifies its sources, its ruling as a personal duty, and the three rational categories that form its subject matter. This foundational passage sets the stage for the entire study of Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact style and the enumeration of key points.
The passage contains several distinct elements in a short space.
2
Understand the name and definition
Identify why the science is called 'kalam' and note that some scholars mention it has eight names. This helps situate the field historically.
3
Grasp the ruling and sources
The text states that kalam is derived from rational and transmitted proofs and that learning it is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) on every responsible person.
This counters the misconception that kalam is optional or communal.
4
Learn the three categories of issues
The masa'il (issues) of kalam are propositions about the necessary (wajib), the possible (ja'iz), and the impossible (mustahil). These are the core subject matter.
These three categories will recur throughout the study of kalam.
5
Reflect on the introduction
The passage ends by explaining that these definitions and classifications are the 'introduction' (muqaddima) of the science, without which one cannot proceed to the intended purpose.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة
علم الكلام ؛ لأن المتقدمين كانوا يقولون في الترجمة عن مباحثه : الكلام في كذا ، أو لأنه قد كثر الاختلاف في مسألة الكلام، وذكر بعضهم أن له ثمانية أسماء (١) . و استمداده من الأدلة العقلية والنقلية.
وحكم الشارع فيه : الوجوب العيني على كل مكلّف من ذكر وأنثى (٢) .
ومسائله : قضاياه الباحثة عن الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات (۳)
وهذه المبادئ هي التي تُسمَّى مقدمة العلم؛ لأنها اسم لمعان يتوقف عليها الشروعُ في المقصود.
The introduction. The science of kalam: because the early scholars used to say in the translation of its topics: 'discourse about such-and-such', or because disputes increased concerning the issue of (God's) speech. Some of them mentioned that it has eight names. Its source (istimdad) is from rational and transmitted proofs. The ruling of the Lawgiver concerning it: individual obligation (wujub 'ayni) upon every responsible person, male and female. Commentary
This passage is a definition of the science of kalam, outlining its key aspects. First, the author explains why it is called 'kalam': either because early scholars would say 'discourse about such-and-such' when introducing its topics, or because of the many disputes concerning the attribute of speech (kalam) of Allah. He also notes that some scholars count eight names for this science. Then he states its sources: both rational proofs and transmitted proofs (Qur'an and Sunnah). Next, the author gives the ruling of the Lawgiver (shar') with respect to kalam: it is an individual obligation (fard 'ayn) upon every responsible person, male or female, because correct belief is required individually. He then defines the issues (masa'il) of kalam as propositions that investigate the three rational categories: necessary, possible, and impossible. Finally, he explains that these definitions and divisions are the 'principles' (mabadi') that form the introduction (muqaddima) of the science, because they are concepts that must be understood before delving into the actual subject matter.

The Expanded Definition of Religion: Divine Institution and Human Effort
The commentator presents the second definition of religion and explains 'wad'' and 'ilāhi' with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the expanded definition of religion in kalam, the meanings of 'wad'' and 'ilāhi', and how ijtihādī rulings are considered divinely instituted despite human effort.

Core Idea
A divine institution that your own reasoning uncovers, not creates.

This passage presents the second, more detailed definition of religion from the matn. The commentator explains each part—'wad'' as a placed thing, 'ilāhi' as divine—and then tackles a common objection about human effort in fiqh. Understanding this definition is crucial for grasping how kalam frames the source and scope of religious law.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: definition, then breakdown of 'wad'' and 'ilāhi', then objection and answer.
2
Focus on 'wad'' and 'ilāhi'
Pay attention to how the commentator explains 'wad'' as a verbal noun meaning 'something placed' and 'ilāhi' as excluding human institutions. Note the mention of political laws and crafts as examples.
The commentator says human institutions are only apparently excluded, as Allah is the ultimate creator.
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues that if humans have acquisition (kasb) in ijtihādī rulings, then those rulings cannot be divine institutions. See if you can anticipate the answer.
4
See the commentator's answer
The commentator confirms that ijtihādī rulings are divine institutions, but hidden; mujtahids only manifest them through legal reasoning, not create them.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة وثانيهما مطوَّلٌ : وهو وضع إلهي سائق لذوي العقول السليمة باختيارهم المحمود إلى ما هو خير لهم بالذات (١) .

فقولهم: (وضع) أي موضوع، فهو مصدر بمعنى اسم المفعول؛ أي شيءٌ موضوع بقطع النظر عن أن يكونَ حُكْماً أو غيره لأجل الإخراجات الآتية، ودخل المجاز التعريف لشهرته (٢) .

وقولهم : (إلهي) أي: منسوب للإله ؛ وهو الله تعالى، وخرج به الوضع البشري ظاهراً، وإلا فالواضع لجميع الأشياء هو الله في الحقيقة، وذلك نحو الرسوم السياسية؛ أي: القوانين التي ترجع إليها سياسة العالم ؛ كعلم إصلاح المنزل، وحسن العشرة مع الأهل والإخوان، والأوضاع الصناعية كالتجارة والقزازة وغير ذلك، وقد كانت الحكماء القدماء يؤلفون كتباً في سياسة الرعية وإصلاح المدن، فيحكم بها ملوك من لا شرعَ لهم، فإنه وإن كان الخالقُ لكل الأشياء هو الله تعالى، إلا أن البشر لهم في هذه كسب .

لا يقال : يلزم على ذلك أن أحكام الفقه الاجتهادية ليست من الدين؛ لأنَّ البشر أعني المجتهدين - لهم فيها كسب، وإنما منه ما ورد نصّا لا خلاف فيه؛ لأنا نقول : هي من الدين قطعاً، وهي موضوع إلهي غاية الأمر أنه يخفى علينا، والمجتهدون يعانون إظهارها والاستدلال عليها بقواعد الشرع، ولا مدخل لهم في وضعها (۳).
The introduction. And the second of them is a lengthy [definition]: It is a divine institution driving those of sound intellects by their praiseworthy choice to what is good for them essentially. For their saying: 'institution' (wad') is an object [placed], so it is a verbal noun in the sense of a passive participle; that is, something placed without considering whether it is a ruling or other, for the sake of the exceptions to come, and the metonymy entered the definition due to its fame. And their saying: 'divine' (ilāhi) means attributed to Allah, and He is Allah, exalted is He, and by it is excluded human institution apparently, otherwise the placer of all things is Allah in reality, and that is like political statutes; that is, the laws to which the governance of the world refers; like the science of house management, and good company with family and brothers, and industrial institutions such as commerce and glassmaking and other than that, and the ancient sages used to author books on governance of subjects and improvement of cities, and they were judged by kings who had no law, for although the Creator of all things is Allah, exalted is He, human beings have acquisition (kasb) in these. It cannot be said: It follows from this that the ijtihādī rulings of fiqh are not from the religion, because human beings—I mean the mujtahids—have acquisition (kasb) in them, and only that which is textually transmitted without disagreement is from it; for we say: They are from the religion definitively, and they are a divine institution, the only matter is that it is hidden from us, and the mujtahids are occupied with manifesting them and deducing evidence for them by the principles of the Sharia, and they have no role in their institution.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a definition and its explanation. The commentator presents the second, expanded definition of religion (al-din) as a divine institution (wad' ilāhi) that drives those with sound intellects to essential good through their praiseworthy choice. He then breaks down the definition term by term. First, he explains 'wad'' (institution) as a verbal noun meaning 'something placed', referring to any divinely placed thing, whether a ruling or not, and notes that it is a metonymy (majāz) for the concept. Second, he explains 'ilāhi' (divine) as attributed to Allah, excluding human institutions such as political laws, household management, arts and crafts, and the political writings of ancient sages used by non-law-bearing kings. He adds that while humans have acquisition (kasb) in these, the ultimate Creator is Allah. The commentator then addresses an objection: if human ijtihād involves kasb, are ijtihādī fiqh rulings not from religion? He answers that they are definitely from religion; they are a divine institution, but hidden (khafiyyah), and mujtahids do not institute them but rather manifest them through legal reasoning, so there is no contradiction.

Key Terms
وضع (wad')
إلهي (ilāhi)
كسب (kasb)
اجتهاد (ijtihād)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That human effort in fiqh (ijtihād) implies those rulings are not divinely instituted.
Correction: The passage clarifies that mujtahids uncover, not create, the divine rulings; ijtihādī rulings are still part of the divine institution.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between ijtihādī fiqh rulings and the divine institution of religion?
A) They are human institutions because mujtahids have kasb in them.
B) They are divine institutions that are hidden; mujtahids manifest them through legal reasoning.
C) They are not part of religion because they are not textually transmitted without disagreement.
D) They are divine institutions but only the purely textual ones are from religion.

Key Takeaway
Religion is divinely instituted, and even rulings derived through ijtihād are part of that divine institution because mujtahids uncover them, not invent them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a ruling derived through ijtihād, do you ever doubt it is truly from Allah? How does this passage's distinction between institution and manifestation change your trust in the scholars who derive these rulings? Will you commit to accepting ijtihādī rulings as part of your religious practice, even when their divine origin is not immediately obvious?
?
The commentator says that human beings have acquisition (kasb) in political laws and crafts. How do you view your own daily decisions — are they merely your own, or do you see them as part of a larger divine order? What would change if you treated every praiseworthy choice as an act of uncovering divine guidance? What is one decision today where you will actively seek the hidden divine institution rather than rely solely on your own reasoning?
?
Consider an area of your life where you rely heavily on your own reasoning — perhaps career, relationships, or personal ethics. Does this passage challenge you to see that even your best efforts are ultimately uncovering rather than creating? Are you willing to surrender the pride of 'inventing' and instead humbly seek what Allah has already placed? What will you do this week to practice 'uncovering' rather than 'creating'?
