The Driving Force of Religion and Its Recipients
The commentator explains the term 'sā'iq' (driving) and refines the recipients as 'those of sound intellects', excluding animals and other non-rational beings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'sā'iq' (driving) in the definition of religion and why the recipients are specified as 'those of sound intellects', excluding animals and unintelligible divine phenomena.

Core Idea
Religion drives rational beings through knowledge of reward and punishment, not through natural instincts.

The commentator now refines two key terms from the definition of religion: what it means for religion to be 'driving' and who exactly it drives. He clarifies that the driving is through hearing about consequences, and it is directed only to humans with sound intellects – not to animals or those without reason. This passage sharpens our understanding of religion's unique function.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the different examples used.
2
Define the key terms
Identify 'sā'iq' and its synonyms (bā'ith, hāmil). Then identify 'li-dhawī al-ʻuqūl al-salīmah' and understand who it refers to.
Note that 'sound' intellects are those free from disbelief.
3
Examine the driving mechanism
The commentator explains that driving happens when the mukallaf hears about reward for obligations and punishment for prohibitions. This hearing drives them to act.
Contrast this with natural phenomena that do not drive us to moral action.
4
Explore the exclusion of animals
The second part specifies that religion is only for those with sound intellects. Animals are excluded because they are guided by natural inspirations (ilhāmāt), not by rational understanding of reward and punishment.
Examples: spider weaving, bee building hives, sheep fleeing wolf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : (سائق) أي : باعث وحامل؛ لأنَّ المكلَّف إذا سمع ما يترتب على فعل الواجب من الثواب أو على فعل الحرام من العقاب انساق إلى فعل الأول وتركِ الثاني وهكذا قالوا (٤) وخرج به الوضع الإلهي غيرُ السائق؛ كإنبات الأرض وإمطار السماء، وبحث في ذلك بأنه سائق لإصلاح المعاش، فالأحسنُ التمثيل لغير السائق بالوضع الإلهي الذي لا اطلاع لنا عليه كالذي تحت الأرضين؛ فإن ما لا نعرفه لا يسوقنا لشيء.

وقولهم : لذوي العقول السليمة أي لأصحاب العقول السليمة من الكفر، والمراد سائق لهم فقط، وخرج به ما يسوقهم وغيرهم من الحيوانات؛ كالأوضاع الطبيعية التي يهتدي بها الحيوانات وهي الإلهامات التي تسوق الحيوانات لفعل منافعها؛ كنسج العنكبوت، واتخاذ النحل بيوتاً واجتناب مضارها؛ وهي كنفر الشاة من الذئب وغير ذلك.
And their saying: 'driving' (sā'iq) means impeller and motivator; because the responsible person (mukallaf) when he hears what is consequent upon doing the obligatory of reward or upon doing the forbidden of punishment, he is driven to doing the first and leaving the second, and thus they said. And by it is excluded the divine institution that is not driving, like the sprouting of the earth and the raining of the sky, and there was discussion about that because it is driving for the improvement of livelihood, so the best example for the non-driving is the divine institution of which we have no knowledge, like that which is under the earths; for what we do not know does not drive us to anything. And their saying: 'to those of sound intellects' means the owners of intellects sound from disbelief, and the intended [is] driving them only, and by it is excluded what drives them and other than them of animals, like the natural institutions by which animals are guided, and they are the inspirations (ilhāmāt) that drive animals to do what benefits them, like the spider's weaving, and the bees' making of houses and avoiding harms; and like the sheep's fleeing from the wolf and other than that.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage explains two terms from the definition of religion: 'sā'iq' (driving) and 'li-dhawī al-ʻuqūl al-salīmah' (to those of sound intellects). First, the commentator defines 'sā'iq' as an impeller (bā'ith) and motivator (hāmil). He explains that the driving mechanism is through hearing about reward for obligations and punishment for prohibitions, which drives the mukallaf to act. This excludes non-driving divine institutions, such as the growth of plants and rain, which are divine but do not drive humans to actions directly. However, a discussion is noted: some might argue that rain drives livelihood, so the commentator gives a better example: divine institutions of which we have no knowledge (like underground phenomena), since the unknown does not drive us. Second, 'li-dhawī al-ʻuqūl al-sālimah' specifies that the driving is only for beings with sound intellects (free from disbelief). This excludes animals, which are also driven by divine instincts (ilhāmāt) to benefit themselves (e.g., spiders weaving webs, bees building hives, sheep fleeing wolves). Thus, religion's addressing of rational humans is emphasized.

Key Terms
سائق (sā'iq)
باعث (bā'ith)
حامل (hāmil)
مكلَّف (mukallaf)
ثواب (thawāb)
عقاب (ʻiqāb)
ذوي العقول السليمة (those of sound intellects)
إلهامات (ilhāmāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that natural phenomena like rain are part of religion's driving mechanism.
Correction: The commentator shows that rain is a non-driving divine institution; it does not drive humans to moral action because it is not known to cause reward or punishment. The driving mechanism is specifically through knowledge of consequences, not through physical benefits.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator suggest that the best example of a non-driving divine institution is something under the earth of which we have no knowledge?
A) Because such phenomena are not divine.
B) Because they are not visible and thus cannot be known.
C) Because what we do not know cannot drive us to action.
D) Because they are not beneficial to humans.

Key Takeaway
Religion's driving power is unique: it operates through knowledge of reward and punishment, addressed only to rational beings, unlike natural instincts that guide animals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform religious acts, do you find yourself more driven by the knowledge of reward and punishment, or by habit and comfort? Which of the two does your heart truly follow? Commit to checking your intention (niyyah) in one action today to see if it is driven by knowledge or mere habit.
?
The commentator excludes unknown divine institutions because they do not drive us. What unknown truths about Allah have you neglected to seek, and how is that neglect affecting your commitment to His commands? This week, choose one unknown aspect of your faith to learn about so that it may become a driver for you.
?
Animals are guided by instinct without choice, but you are addressed by revelation. In what area of your life are you still acting by mere instinct or imitation rather than by conscious knowledge of what Allah desires? Name one habit you continue without reflection, and decide to study its ruling (hukm) this week.

Praiseworthy Choice and Essential Good in the Definition of Religion
The commentator explains praiseworthy choice, essential good, and excludes worldly crafts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how praiseworthy and blameworthy choices are distinguished and what 'essential good' means in the definition of religion.

Core Idea
Only praiseworthy choice driving to essential good defines religion.

The commentator refines the definition of religion by explaining the kind of choice that leads to true good. He distinguishes between institutions that compel without choice and those driven by blameworthy desire, then identifies the ultimate good that religion aims at.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the technical terms: ikhtiyār maḥmūd, ikhtiyār madhmūm, wijdāniyyāt.
2
Identify the three categories of institutions
The passage describes institutions that drive people: (1) without choice, (2) by blameworthy choice, (3) by praiseworthy choice. Find each one and note the examples given.
The first includes pain and hunger; the second includes love of this world leading to abandoning zakāt; the third is more general.
3
Understand 'essential good' and its exclusion
The commentator defines essential good as eternal happiness and nearness to Allah. Then he explains why crafts like medicine and agriculture are excluded from religion.
Think about why these crafts, though beneficial, are not part of religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقولهم : (باختيارهم المحمود خرج به الأوضاع السائقة لهم لا باختيارهم، أو باختيارهم المذموم؛ فالأولى كالآلام السائقة للأنين رغماً، وكالوجدانيات كالجوع والعطش؛ فإنهما يسوقان إلى الأكل والشرب قهراً والثانية كحب الدنيا؛ فإنه وضع إلهي يبعث ذوي العقول إلى ترك الزكاة باختيارهم المذموم، ومتى كان الاختيار محموداً، لا يسوقُ إلا إلى خير فقوله: (إلى ما هو خير لهم) إنما ذكروه توصلاً لقولهم : (بالذات) فهو متعلق بـ (خير ) وذلك الخيرُ الذاتي عبارة عن السعادة الأبدية، والقرب من رب البرية، وخرج بذلك صنعتا الطب والفلاحة؛ فإنهما وإن تعلقتا بوضع إلهي سائق لذوي العقول باختيارهم المحمود، لكن لا إلى الخير الذاتي، بل إلى صنفٍ من الخير، وهو حفظ صحة أبدانهم بالحكمة والعقاقير؛ أي: أجزاء الأدوية وبنحو الأغذية.

وحاصل هذا التعريف مع طوله : أن الدين هو الأحكام التي وضعها الله الباعثة للعباد إلى الخير الذاتي.

فائدة: أمور الدين أربعة كما قاله النووي؛ أي علامات وجوده، وقد نظمها بعضهم فقال : [من الطويل]
:
أُمُورٌ لِدِينِ صِدْقُ قَصْدِ وَفَا العَهْدِ وَتَرْكُ لِمَنْهِيِّ كَذَا صِحَّةُ العَقْدِ
And their saying: 'by their praiseworthy choice' excludes the institutions that drive them without their choice, or by their blameworthy choice; the first are like pains that drive to groaning against one's will, and like felt experiences (wijdāniyyāt) such as hunger and thirst; for they drive to eating and drinking forcibly. And the second is like love of this world (ḥubb al-dunyā); for it is a divine institution that drives those of intellect to abandoning zakāt by their blameworthy choice. And whenever the choice is praiseworthy, it drives only to good. So His saying: 'to what is good for them' they only mentioned it as a means to their saying: 'essentially' (bi-al-dhāt), so it is connected to 'good'. And that essential good is expressed as eternal happiness (al-saʻādah al-abadiyyah) and nearness to the Lord of creation. And by it are excluded the crafts of medicine and agriculture; for although they are connected to a divine institution driving those of intellects by praiseworthy choice, yet not to essential good, but to a kind of good, which is preserving the health of their bodies through wisdom and drugs (ʻaqāqīr); that is, parts of medications and like foods. And the summary of this definition with its length is: Religion is the rulings that Allah placed, impelling the servants to essential good. Benefit: The matters of religion are four as al-Nawawī said; that is, signs of its existence, and one of them versified them saying [in Ṭawīl meter]: Amrūn li-dīnī ṣidqu qaṣdi wafā al-ʻahdi wa-tarku li-manhiyyi kadhā ṣiḥḥatu al-ʻaqdi.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage completes the explanation of the definition of religion. The commentator explains 'by their praiseworthy choice' (bi-ikhtiyārihim al-maḥmūd), noting that it excludes institutions that drive people without their choice (like pain forcing groaning, or hunger forcing eating) and those that drive through blameworthy choice (like love of the world leading to abandoning zakāt). Praiseworthy choice drives only to good. Next, 'to what is good for them essentially' specifies that the good is essential (dhātī), which is eternal happiness and nearness to Allah. This excludes crafts like medicine and agriculture, which aim at bodily health, not the essential good. The commentator then gives a summary: religion is the divine rulings that impel servants to essential good. Finally, he adds a benefit (fāʼidah) from al-Nawawī: the four signs of religion are sincerity of intention, fulfillment of covenant, abandonment of what is forbidden, and soundness of contract—versified in a line of poetry.

وجدانيات
خير ذاتي
سعادة أبدية
قرب رب البرية

فلاحة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religion includes all divine institutions, even those aiming at worldly benefits like health or agriculture.
Correction: Religion only concerns divine institutions that drive humans by praiseworthy choice to essential good, i.e., eternal happiness and nearness to Allah. Medicine and agriculture, though beneficial and divinely instituted, aim at bodily health, not the ultimate good.

Checkpoint
What does the commentator exclude from religion by the phrase 'to what is good for them essentially'?
A) All human institutions.
B) Pain and hunger that drive without choice.
C) Crafts like medicine and agriculture that aim at bodily health.
D) Love of this world that leads to sin.

Key Takeaway
Religion aims only at essential good (eternal happiness and nearness to Allah), not at worldly goods like health.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on your daily activities: which of them are directed towards the essential good (eternal happiness and nearness to Allah) and which are merely for worldly good like health or wealth? Are you satisfied with that balance? What concrete step will you take tomorrow to shift one hour from a worldly pursuit to a spiritual one?
?
The commentator says that even praiseworthy choices like medicine and agriculture are not religion because they don't aim at essential good. Many of us spend decades mastering worldly crafts. Does this make you uncomfortable? What would it mean for you to reorient your work and learning so that it becomes a means to the essential good rather than an end in itself?
?
Examine your 'praiseworthy choices'—are any of them actually tainted by blameworthy motives like love of this world? For example, do you ever give charity seeking recognition or avoid obligations out of attachment to wealth? Identify one such blameworthy inclination and resolve to purify your intention in that specific action starting today.

Summary Definition of Religion and Its Four Signs
The commentator provides a summary definition and a benefit on four signs of religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will remember the concise definition of religion given by the commentator and the four signs that indicate its presence.

Core Idea
Religion is divine rulings impelling to essential good, known by four signs: intention, covenant, abstinence, and sound contract.

After the detailed explanation, the commentator offers a crisp summary: religion is a set of divine laws that drive people toward ultimate good. He then enriches the discussion with a benefit listing the four practical signs by which religion can be recognized in behavior.

The signs are practical and measurable.
3

Try to memorize the poetic couplet that combines the four signs. It will help you recall them easily.
The meter is ṭawīl.

The Definition of Religion (Deen) Explained
Commentary on the definition of deen phrase by phrase with summary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise scholarly definition of religion (deen) as articulated in the commentary, including its key components and exclusions.

Core Idea
Religion is a divine ordinance that drives sound intellects by praiseworthy choice to essential good.

This lesson unpacks a classic definition of deen from a commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid. The commentator carefully dissects each phrase to show what is included and excluded, culminating in a concise summary. Understanding this definition is foundational for Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Pay attention to how each phrase is qualified.
2
Identify the key phrases being explained
Look for the phrases like 'those of sound intellects', 'by their praiseworthy choice', and 'to what is good for them essentially'. Notice how the commentator refines each.
Each phrase narrows the definition.
3
Understand the exclusions
The commentator excludes animals, involuntary drives, blameworthy choices, and worldly professions. Understand why each is excluded and how it sharpens the definition.
Exclusions clarify what deen is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السماء، وبحث في ذلك بأنه سائق لإصلاح المعاش، فالأحسنُ التمثيل لغير السائق بالوضع الإلهي الذي لا اطلاع لنا عليه كالذي تحت الأرضين؛ فإن ما لا نعرفه لا يسوقنا لشيء (1) وقولهم : لذوي العقول السليمة أي لأصحاب العقول السليمة من الكفر، والمراد سائق لهم فقط، وخرج به ما يسوقهم وغيرهم من الحيوانات؛ كالأوضاع الطبيعية التي يهتدي بها الحيوانات وهي الإلهامات التي تسوق الحيوانات لفعل منافعها؛ كنسج العنكبوت، واتخاذ النحل بيوتاً واجتناب مضارها ؛ وهي كنفر الشاة من الذئب وغير ذلك . وقولهم : (باختيارهم المحمود خرج به الأوضاع السائقة لهم لا باختيارهم، أو باختيارهم المذموم؛ فالأولى كالآلام السائقة للأنين رغماً، وكالوجدانيات كالجوع والعطش ؛ فإنهما يسوقان إلى الأكل والشرب قهراً والثانية كحب الدنيا ؛ فإنه وضع إلهي يبعث ذوي العقول إلى ترك الزكاة باختيارهم المذموم، ومتى كان الاختيار محموداً، لا يسوقُ إلا إلى خير فقوله: (إلى ما هو خير لهم) إنما ذكروه توصلاً لقولهم : (بالذات) فهو متعلق بـ (خير ) وذلك الخيرُ الذاتي عبارة عن السعادة الأبدية، والقرب من رب البرية، وخرج بذلك صنعتا الطب والفلاحة؛ فإنهما وإن تعلقتا بوضع إلهي سائق لذوي العقول باختيارهم المحمود، لكن لا إلى الخير الذاتي، بل إلى صنفٍ من الخير، وهو حفظ صحة أبدانهم بالحكمة والعقاقير؛ أي: أجزاء الأدوية وبنحو الأغذية. وحاصل هذا التعريف مع طوله : أن الدين هو الأحكام التي وضعها الله الباعثة للعباد إلى الخير الذاتي.
The heavens, and a discussion about that, that it is a driver for the improvement of livelihood, so the best example for what is not a driver is the divine disposition of which we have no knowledge, like that which is beneath the earths; for what we do not know does not drive us to anything. (1) And their statement: 'for those of sound intellects' means for the possessors of intellects sound from disbelief, and the intended meaning is a driver for them only, and what drives them and other animals is excluded by it, like the natural dispositions by which animals are guided, which are the inspirations that drive animals to do what benefits them, such as the spider's weaving, the bees' making of hives and avoiding harm, and the sheep's fleeing from the wolf and other things. And their statement: 'by their praiseworthy choice' excludes the dispositions that drive them not by their choice, or by their blameworthy choice. The first are like pains that drive one to groan unwillingly, and like the instinctive feelings of hunger and thirst, for they drive to eating and drinking by compulsion. The second is like love of the world, for it is a divine disposition that urges those with intellects to abandon zakat by their blameworthy choice. And whenever the choice is praiseworthy, it only drives to good. So their statement: 'to what is good for them' they only mentioned it as a means to their statement: 'essentially', so it is related to 'good', and that essential good is everlasting happiness and nearness to the Lord of creation. By that, the professions of medicine and agriculture are excluded, for even though they are connected to a divine disposition driving those of intellects by their praiseworthy choice, they do not lead to essential good, but to a kind of good, which is preserving the health of their bodies by wisdom and drugs, i.e., parts of medicines and kinds of foods. The summary of this definition, despite its length, is that religion is the ordinances that God has laid down, driving servants to essential good.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the definition of religion (deen). The commentator dissects each phrase of a previously given definition. He first discusses an objection about the definition being too broad or narrow. Then he explains the phrase 'for those of sound intellects' meaning those free from disbelief, and excludes animals that are also driven by divine inspiration but not by choice. He then explains 'by their praiseworthy choice' distinguishing between involuntary drives (like pain) and blameworthy choice (like love of world). The key point is that only praiseworthy choice leads to good. Then he explains 'to what is good for them' as essential good, i.e., everlasting happiness, and excludes professions like medicine and agriculture which aim at worldly good. The summary is that deen is the divine ordinances that drive servants to essential good.

وضع إلهي
اختيار محمود
خير ذاتي
سعادة أبدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That religion is merely a set of rules or rituals without a driving purpose.
Correction: The definition emphasizes that deen is a driving force (sa'iq) from God, directing humans through praiseworthy choice to the ultimate essential good, not just a list of commands.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Religion is the divine ordinances that drive sound intellects through praiseworthy choice to everlasting happiness.

The definition excludes actions driven by blameworthy choice, like neglecting zakat due to love of the world. The Four Signs of Religion
The commentator lists and explains the four signs of religion: sincerity, covenant, avoidance, and sound belief.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four signs that indicate the presence of true religion in a person, as explained by al-Nawawi.

Core Idea
Four signs reveal religion's presence: sincerity, covenant, avoidance, and sound belief.

Al-Nawawi teaches us that religion is not just a label but manifests in specific outward and inward signs. This short passage, complete with a mnemonic poem, lists four unmistakable markers of genuine faith. As you read, pay attention to how each sign is defined and why they are signs rather than the essence of religion itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and poem
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the four signs in the versified line. This is a benefit (fā'ida) from al-Nawawi.
The poem helps memorize the four signs.
2
Identify each sign
List the four signs: truth of intention (sincerity), fulfillment of covenant, abandonment of what is forbidden, and soundness of contract (firm belief).
3
Understand the explanations
For each sign, the author provides a concise explanation: sincerity means worship with intention and sincerity; covenant means performing obligations; avoidance means staying away from prohibitions; soundness of contract means affirming Sunni beliefs with certainty.
Note that 'soundness of contract' refers to creedal firmness.
4
Distinguish signs from definition
The passage calls these 'signs of its existence' (علامات وجوده), not the definition of religion. They show that religion is present; they are not what religion is.
This answers a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة: أمور الدين أربعة كما قاله النووي؛ أي علامات وجوده، وقد نظمها بعضهم فقال : [من الطويل] : أُمُورٌ لِدِينِ صِدْقُ قَصْدِ وَفَا العَهْدِ وَتَرْكُ لِمَنْهِيِّ كَذَا صِحَّةُ العَقْدِ قصدقُ القصد أداء العبادة بالنية والإخلاص ؛ ووفاء العهد: الإتيان بالفرائض، وتركُ المنهي : اجتناب المحرمات، وصحة العقد : جزمه بعقائد أهل السنة.
Benefit: The matters of religion are four, as al-Nawawi said, meaning the signs of its existence. And some of them versified them in the meter al-tawīl: Matters of religion: truth of intention, fulfillment of covenant, abandonment of what is forbidden, and likewise soundness of contract. Truth of intention is performing worship with intention and sincerity; fulfillment of covenant is performing obligatory acts; abandonment of what is forbidden is avoiding prohibited things; and soundness of contract is affirming with certainty the beliefs of Ahl al-Sunnah.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage is a fā'ida (benefit) that lists the four signs that indicate the presence of religion in a person. Al-Nawawi is cited. The signs are: 1) sincerity of intention in worship, 2) fulfillment of covenant which means performing obligatory duties, 3) abandoning what is forbidden, and 4) soundness of contract which means firm belief in the Sunni creed. The poem serves as a mnemonic.

Misconception: Some might think that these four signs are the definition of religion itself.
Correction: The passage explicitly says they are 'signs of its existence' (علامات وجوده), not the essence or definition of religion. They are indicators that religion is present in a person.

The Sword as Instrument of Guidance: Grammatical and Theological Analysis
Grammatical and theological analysis of 'bisaifihi' in the poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'bisaifihi' is interpreted grammatically and theologically, distinguishing between guidance by indication and guidance by compulsion.

Core Idea
The sword guides not by logic but by compulsion; metaphor bridges the specific and general.

This passage delves into a seemingly simple phrase 'with his sword' from the poem. The commentator unpacks its grammatical possibilities and theological implications, showing that the sword is not a tool for logical guidance but a means of compulsion. We will explore how the ba' (preposition) functions differently depending on the meaning of 'guidance'.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once without analysis, then read the literal translation to get the full picture.
Notice the typo 'بِسَبْفِهِ' which is likely 'بِسَيْفِهِ'.
2
Identify the two possible meanings of 'arshada'
The commentator presents two interpretations: 'indicated' (dalla) and 'made righteous' (sayyarahum rashidin). Understand how each affects the grammar.
3
Understand the grammatical role of the ba'
When 'arshada' means 'indicated', the ba' is for accompaniment (circumstantial); when it means 'made righteous', the ba' is for causation (sababiyya).
The ba' cannot be transitive because guidance is not done with a sword.
4
Recognize the metaphorical extension
The 'sword' is a specific weapon, but it stands for any instrument of jihad, even stones. This is a majaz mursal (transferred metaphor) from specific to general.
This shows that the metaphor includes both literal and metaphorical meanings.
5
Reflect on the miraculous story of 'Ukasha
The Prophet gave 'Ukasha a piece of firewood that turned into a sword, emphasizing that the sword is not ordinary but divinely guided.
This reinforces the idea that the sword is a means of guidance through compulsion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِسَبْفِهِ) يحتمل أن يكونَ متعلقاً بحال محذوفة من فاعل (ارشد) أي: أرشد الخلق لدين الحقِّ في حال كونه ملتبساً بسيفه أو حال كونه مُلْجِنَّاً لهم بسيفه ؛ لأن الإرشاد والدلالة ليسا بالسيف حتى تكون الباء للتعدية بل باللسان قطعاً (١) ، وهذا بالخلق إذا جعل (أرشد) بمعنى دلَّ، أما إذا جعل بمعنى صيَّرهم راشدين على أن المراد أمَّةُ الإجابة، فالباء للسبية، وإضافة سيف للضمير لأدنى ملابسة؛ لأن المراد بالسيف: السيفُ (۲) الذي جاءَ بمشروعية مقاتلة أعداء الله به، سواء كان بيده أو بيد من تبعه ولو إلى يوم القيامة، والمراد بالسيف آلة الجهاد التي يباح قتال الحربيين بها، أحد حتى الحجارة، فقد رمى بالحجر في يوم ففي كلام المصنف مجاز مرسل ؛ من إطلاق الخاص وإرادة العام فهو من باب عموم المجاز ؛ أي: المجاز العام الشامل للحقيقة والمجاز .
وقد كان له الله سيوف متعدّدة ؛ منها المأثورُ ؛ وهو أوَّلُ سيف ملكه ؛ لأنه ورثَهُ من أبيه، ومنها القضيب بالقاف والضاد، ومنها ذو الفقار بفتح الفاء وكسرها ، ومنها غير ذلك، وقد دفع النبي الله العكاشة جزل حطب حين انكسر سيفه يوم بدر وقال : اضرب بهِ»، فعادَ في يدِهِ سيفاً صارماً طويلاً أبيض شديد المتن، فقاتل به (٤).
His saying: 'with his sword' – it is possible that it is related to an omitted circumstantial accusative from the subject of 'guided', meaning: He guided creation to the religion of truth while he was attired with his sword or while he compelled them with his sword; because guidance and indication are not done with the sword such that the ba' is for transitive, but rather with the tongue certainly. And this with creation if 'guided' is taken to mean 'indicated'. As for if it is taken to mean 'made them righteous', assuming the intended is the nation of response, then the ba' is for causation, and the attribution of 'sword' to the pronoun is for the slightest connection; because the intended by 'sword' is the sword that came with the legislation of fighting the enemies of Allah by it, whether it be in his hand or in the hand of those who follow him until the Day of Resurrection. And the intended by 'sword' is the instrument of jihad by which it is permitted to fight combatants, even up to stones, for he threw a stone on a day. So in the author's speech there is a transferred metaphor (majaz mursal) from the specific to the general, so it is from the chapter of general metaphor; i.e., the general metaphor that encompasses the literal and the metaphorical. And he had, may Allah bless him, multiple swords: among them al-Ma'thur, which was the first sword he owned because he inherited it from his father; among them al-Qadib (the rod), with qaf and dad; among them Dhul-Fiqar, with fath or kasr of the fa'; and others. And the Prophet, peace be upon him, gave 'Ukasha a piece of firewood when his sword broke on the day of Badr and said: 'Strike with it,' and it became in his hand a sharp, long, white, strong-bladed sword, and he fought with it.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical and theological analysis of the word 'bisaifihi' (with his sword) in the poem. The commentator begins by noting that the ba' could be attached to an implied circumstantial accusative from the verb 'arshada' (guided), meaning the Prophet guided while being adorned with his sword or while compelling them with his sword. He explains that guidance and indication are not done with a sword, so the ba' cannot be transitive; it is rather with the tongue. If 'arshada' means 'indicated', then the ba' is for accompanying circumstant. If it means 'made righteous', then the ba' is for causation. He clarifies that the sword here refers to the instrument of jihad, even up to stones, citing the prophetic tradition of throwing a stone. He then identifies this as a transferred metaphor (majaz mursal) from the specific to the general. The text then lists some of the Prophet's swords: al-Ma'thur (inherited from his father), al-Qadib, and Dhul-Fiqar. It recounts the story of 'Ukasha, who was given a piece of firewood that turned into a sword, emphasizing the miraculous nature of the sword as a means of guidance.

Key Terms
سيف
إرشاد
ملابسة
سببية
مجاز مرسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sword is a tool for logical guidance and indication.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that guidance and indication are done with the tongue, not the sword. The sword is a means of compulsion, not logical persuasion.

Checkpoint
What is the grammatical function of the ba' in 'bisaifihi' when 'arshada' means 'made righteous'?
A) Transitive
B) For accompaniment (circumstantial)
C) For causation
D) For swearing

Key Takeaway
The word 'bisaifihi' illustrates how a single phrase can carry multiple grammatical and theological layers, with the sword representing compulsion rather than intellectual guidance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own efforts to guide others, do you more often rely on the 'sword' of argumentation and force, or on the 'tongue' of gentle persuasion? Reflect on a recent situation where you tried to correct someone—was your approach closer to compulsion or compassionate dialogue? What would it take for you to shift your method?
?
The Prophet's sword was a means of compulsion for those who rejected the truth after it was made clear. Have you ever felt tempted to impose your views on others, forgetting that true guidance comes only from Allah and that you are merely a messenger? How can you remind yourself of this lesson in your daily interactions?
?
When Ukasha's firewood turned into a sword, it demonstrated that Allah can transform the humblest means into instruments of His power. Do you truly believe that your weakest efforts—your few words, your small acts—can be turned by Allah into tools of guidance? Or do you rely on your own strength and eloquence? What is one thing you can do today to trust more in Allah's transformative power?

Parsing 'Wahdiyhi Lilhaqq' and the Prophet's Guidance
Grammatical and semantic analysis of the phrase 'wahdiyhi lilhaqq' and its connection to the sword.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical parsing of the phrase 'wahdiyhi lilhaqq', the dual role of the ba' preposition, and how scholars interpret 'huda' as the Quran and Sunnah to resolve apparent inconsistencies.

Core Idea
The ba' on 'huda' depicts guidance; the ba' on 'saif' signifies accompaniment or causation.

This lesson examines a key phrase from the poem that describes the Prophet's guidance. The apparent conflict between the sword and guidance is resolved through careful grammatical analysis, revealing the Prophet's method of first calling with the Quran and only then preparing for jihad.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how it discusses the conjunction 'waw' and the role of the preposition 'ba'.
2
Understand the two possible roles of ba' on sword
Identify that the ba' on 'sword' can indicate either accompaniment (mulabasa) or causation (sababiya). Consider how each affects the meaning.
3
Explore the interpretation of huda as Quran and Sunnah
Some scholars interpret 'huda' as the Quran and Sunnah, explaining the Prophet's methodology: first call with Quran, then jihad if refused.
4
Analyze the paronomasia on 'haqq'
Notice that the word 'haqq' is used in two senses: what matches reality and Allah. The author employs complete paronomasia (jinas tamm) to avoid repetition.
5
Review al-Ajhuri's conclusions
Read the footnote from al-Ajhuri which resolves the grammatical objections and concludes that 'huda' must be the Quran and Sunnah, and the ba' on both sword and guidance is for causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَهَدْيِهِ لِلحَقِّ) عطفٌ على (سيفه)، فيصير التقدير وأرشدَهُم بهديه للحقِّ، لكن يلزم عليه تهافت ؛ إذ التقدير ودلَّهُم بدلالته إلا أن تُجعل الباء للتصوير، فتحصل أن الباء من حيث دخولها على السيف للملابسة (٥) أو للسبية كما تقدَّم بيانه، ومن حيث دخولها على هديه للتصوير، وبعضُهم حمل الهدي على القرآن والسنة؛ فقد كان لا يراسل الناسَ أولاً بالقرآن والدعوة للإسلام، فإن أجابوا بالإسلام، فظاهر، وإلا أعلمهم بالتهيؤ للجهاد، وهكذا خلفاؤُهُ وأصحابُهُ من بعده والمراد بـ (الحق) هنا : ما طابقه الواقع إن أريد بالحقِّ الأول الله تعالى، أو المراد به هنا الله تعالى إن أريد به في الأول (۲) ما طابقه الواقع، فليس في كلام المصنف إيطاء، بل فيه الجناسُ التام، وفيه ما تقدَّم من أنها ليست من المشطور. (٥) قوله : (للملابسة) والمعنى على الملابسة أرشد الخلق؛ أي: دلَّهم ملتبساً عند ذلك بسيفه إرشاداً مصوّراً بهديه، ويَرِدُ عليه أن الواو حينئذ لا تكون لتشريكِ ما بعدها مع ما قبلها في الحكم؛ لأن حكم ما قبلها كونه متلبساً به عند الإرشاد، وما بعدها ليس بهذا الحكم، بل هو تصوير للإرشاد، والمعنى على جعلها للسببية : أرشد الخلق؛ أي: صيَّرهم راشدين بسبب سيفه إرشاداً مصوّراً بهديه، ويَرِدُ عليه ما تقدَّم ،بعينه، ويَرِدُ عليه أيضاً أن الإرشاد حينئذ بمعنى التصيير راشدين، وهو بهذا المعنى لا يصوّر بالهدي، فتعين حمل الهدي على القرآن والسنة، وحينئذ تكون الباء للسببية بالنظر إلى السيف والهدي جميعاً . انتهى أجهوري.
His saying: 'and his guidance to the truth' is a conjunction with 'his sword', so the estimation becomes 'and he guided them with his guidance to the truth', but there is inconsistency in it; for the estimation is 'and he indicated them with his indication' unless the ba' is made for depiction. Thus it results that the ba', in terms of its entry upon the sword, is for accompaniment or for causation as previously explained, and in terms of its entry upon his guidance, it is for depiction. Some carried 'guidance' to mean the Quran and Sunnah; for he would not first correspond with people with the Quran and the call to Islam, and if they responded with Islam, it is clear, otherwise he informed them of preparation for jihad, and likewise his caliphs and companions after him. And the intended by 'truth' here is that which matches reality if the first 'truth' is intended as Allah, or the intended here is Allah if in the first it was intended as what matches reality. So in the author's speech there is no repetition (ita'), but rather complete paronomasia (jinas tamm). And it contains what was mentioned before that it is not from the broken (mashṭur). (5) His saying: 'for accompaniment' – the meaning on accompaniment: He guided creation, i.e., he indicated them, being attired at that time with his sword, a guidance depicted by his guidance. It is objected that the waw then is not for sharing what comes after with what came before in the ruling; because the ruling of what came before is his being attired with it at the time of guidance, and what comes after is not with this ruling, but rather it is a depiction of the guidance. And the meaning on making it for causation: He guided the creation, i.e., he made them righteous by means of his sword, a guidance depicted by his guidance. And it is objected by the same as before, and it is also objected that then guidance means making righteous, and with this meaning it is not depicted by guidance. So it is determined that guidance is carried on the Quran and Sunnah, and then the ba' is for causation with respect to both the sword and guidance. End of al-Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage analyzes the phrase 'wahdiyhi lilhaqq' (and his guidance to the truth). The commentator first notes that it is conjoined with 'saihihi' (his sword), leading to the estimation 'he guided them with his guidance to the truth'. He points out an inconsistency if taken literally, and suggests that the ba' on 'hudah' is for depiction (taswir). He then discusses the two possibilities for the ba' on 'saif': either for accompaniment (mulabasa) or causation (sababiya). Some scholars interpret 'huda' as the Quran and Sunnah, explaining that the Prophet first called people with the Quran, and if they refused, he prepared for jihad. The commentator also clarifies the meaning of 'haqq' (truth) in context, noting it is either Allah or what matches reality, and that the author uses a paronomasia (jinas). The footnote from al-Ajhuri elaborates on the grammatical objections and concludes that guidance must be the Quran and Sunnah, and the ba' on both 'saif' and 'huda' is for causation.

Key Terms
وهدى
حق
باء
ملابسة
سببية
تصوير
جناس تام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The conjunction waw implies that the Prophet was simultaneously armed and guiding with the Quran.
Correction: The waw conjoins the sword and guidance, but the second item (guidance) depicts the first; the sword is either an accompaniment or a cause, not a simultaneous action.

Checkpoint
According to al-Ajhuri, what is the correct interpretation of 'huda' (guidance) in this context?
A) The sword
B) The Quran and Sunnah
C) Making righteous
D) The truth

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'wahdiyhi lilhaqq' is grammatically conjoined with 'saihihi', and the apparent conflict is resolved by interpreting 'huda' as the Quran and Sunnah, with the ba' on guidance depicting the manner and the ba' on sword indicating causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call others to the truth, do you rely more on gentle guidance or on the 'sword' of authority and warning? How does the Prophet's balanced methodology—combining the Quran and preparation for jihad—challenge your current approach?
?
The passage shows that a single letter (ba) can carry multiple meanings, and getting it right changes the entire understanding of the Prophet's actions. In your own life, how often do you examine the 'prepositions'—the small details of intention and context—that give your actions their true meaning?

The Definitions of al-Haqq and al-Sidq
The commentator defines haqq and sidq and explains their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definitions of al-haqq and al-sidq in Islamic theology and the nuanced difference between them based on attribution of correspondence.

Core Idea
Al-haqq and al-sidq both mean correspondence to reality, but differ in which side the correspondence is attributed to.

In this passage, the commentator explains the meanings of two key terms: al-haqq and al-sidq. While both are often translated as 'truth', they have distinct nuances in Islamic theological discourse. This lesson will unpack their definitions and show how they relate to each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its translation. Pay attention to the definitions given.
2
Identify the definitions
Find how haqq and sidq are defined. Note that both are defined as a judgment that corresponds to reality.
3
Understand the nuance
Notice that correspondence is attributed to reality for haqq and to the judgment for sidq. Reflect on why this distinction is made based on etymology.
4
Consider the synonymous view
Read the opinion of some scholars that haqq and sidq are synonymous, both meaning correspondence of the report to reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمراد بـ (الحق) هنا : ما طابقه الواقع إن أريد بالحق الأول الله تعالى، أو المراد به هنا الله تعالى إن أريد به في الأول ما طابقه الواقع، فليس في كلام المصنف إيطاء، بل فيه الجناس التام، وفيه ما تقدم من أنها ليست من المشطور. واعلم أنهم فسروا الحق بأنه الحكم الذي طابقه الواقع، وضده الباطل، وفسروا الصدق بأنه الحكم الذي طابق الواقع، وضده الكذب، فأسندوا المطابقة في تفسير الحق إلى الواقع، وفي تفسير الصدق إلى الحكم وذلك أن المطابقة وإن كانت مفاعلة من الجانبين إلا أنه لما كان الحق مأخوذاً من حق الشيء: إذا ثبت، والثابت إنما هو الواقع، ناسب أن تنسب المطابقة في جانب الحق إلى الواقع، بخلافه في الصدق. واختار بعض المحققين أن الحق والصدق شيء واحد، وهو مطابقة الخبر للواقع؛ لأن الواقع شيء ثابت في نفسه يقاس عليه غيره، والمراد بالواقع : علم الله تعالى، وقيل: اللوح المحفوظ، وقيل غير ذلك. بهذا المعنى لا يصور بالهدي، فتعين حمل الهدي على القرآن والسنة، وحينئذ تكون الباء للسببية بالنظر إلى السيف والهدي جميعاً. انتهى أجهوري.
And the intended meaning of 'al-haqq' here is: that which corresponds to reality if by the first 'haqq' is meant Allah Almighty, or the intended meaning here is Allah Almighty if by the first is meant that which corresponds to reality. So there is no repetition in the author's words, but rather perfect homonymy, and there is what preceded that it is not of the truncated type. And know that they interpreted 'al-haqq' as the judgment that corresponds to reality, and its opposite is falsehood; and they interpreted 'al-sidq' as the judgment that corresponds to reality, and its opposite is falsehood. So they attributed correspondence in the interpretation of 'al-haqq' to reality, and in the interpretation of 'al-sidq' to the judgment. That is because correspondence, although it is mutual from both sides, yet when 'al-haqq' is derived from 'haqqa al-shay'u: when it is established, and what is established is only reality, it is appropriate to attribute correspondence on the side of 'al-haqq' to reality, contrary to 'al-sidq'. And some investigators chose that 'al-haqq' and 'al-sidq' are one thing, namely the correspondence of the report to reality; because reality is something established in itself by which other things are measured, and the intended meaning of 'al-waqi'' is: the knowledge of Allah Almighty, and it is said: the Preserved Tablet, and other things said. In this sense it cannot be pictured with 'al-huda', so it is determined that 'al-huda' be interpreted as the Qur'an and the Sunnah, and then the preposition 'ba' is for causality with regard to both the sword and 'al-huda'. End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the terms 'al-haqq' and 'al-sidq' as they appear in the poem. The author begins by clarifying the word 'al-haqq' in the poem line: it can either mean 'that which corresponds to reality' (if the previous 'haqq' referred to Allah) or 'Allah' (if the previous 'haqq' meant correspondence). This is a case of perfect homonymy (جناس تام), not a fault. Then the author explains the general definitions: al-haqq is a judgment that corresponds to reality, its opposite is falsehood; al-sidq is also a judgment that corresponds to reality, its opposite is falsehood. However, they differ in that correspondence is attributed to reality for haqq and to the judgment for sidq, due to the etymological root of haqq (establishment). Some scholars consider them synonymous, both meaning 'correspondence of the report to reality'. Reality is defined as Allah's knowledge or the Preserved Tablet. Finally, the author applies this to the poem: since 'haqq' in this sense cannot be pictured with 'huda', 'huda' must mean the Qur'an and Sunnah, and the 'ba' in the poem is for causality.

Key Terms
الحق
الصدق
الواقع
المطابقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That al-haqq and al-sidq are completely different concepts with no overlap.
Correction: The passage shows that they share the same core meaning (correspondence to reality) and some scholars consider them synonymous; the difference lies only in attribution.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between al-haqq and al-sidq according to some investigators?
A) They are opposites.
B) They are the same thing, both meaning correspondence of the report to reality.
C) Al-haqq is for judgment, al-sidq is for reality.
D) They are unrelated.

Key Takeaway
Both al-haqq and al-sidq refer to correspondence with reality, but haqq emphasizes the reality's establishment while sidq emphasizes the judgment's correspondence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If 'al-waqi'' is ultimately Allah's knowledge, does that change how you view your own statements about the world? Are you careful to align your words with what Allah knows to be true, even when no one is watching?
?
When you speak, do you consider whether your words are 'sidq'—corresponding to reality as it truly is? Reflect on a recent time you said something that was not fully accurate; how can you ensure your speech is always in line with this lesson?
?
The lesson teaches that some consider haqq and sidq to be one thing: correspondence of the report to reality. If your entire life were a report, would it correspond to the reality Allah desires? What one change can you make today to bring your life into greater alignment with the truth?

Applying al-Haqq and al-Sidq to the Poem
The commentator discusses the homonymy in the poem and its implications for interpreting 'al-huda'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the term 'al-haqq' in the poem can be interpreted in two ways, and why this leads to the interpretation of 'al-huda' as Qur'an and Sunnah.

Core Idea
The term 'al-haqq' has a dual interpretation in the poem, creating perfect homonymy and guiding the meaning of 'al-huda'.

The commentator now turns to the specific line of poetry, analyzing the word 'al-haqq'. He shows that it can be read in two ways, either as 'Allah' or as 'correspondence to reality'. This helps resolve potential repetition and leads to a discussion of the word 'al-huda'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on homonymy
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the two possible meanings of 'al-haqq' in the poem.
2
Understand the two meanings
Recognize that 'al-haqq' can mean either 'Allah' or 'that which corresponds to reality', depending on the interpretation of the first occurrence.
3
Notice the homonymy
The dual interpretation results in perfect homonymy (tajnis tam) and avoids repetition (ita').
4
Apply to 'al-huda'
See how the determined meaning of haqq (as correspondence) requires that 'al-huda' be interpreted as Qur'an and Sunnah, and the preposition 'ba' as causative.

Definition and Exceptional Cases of the Companion (Sahabi)
Defines 'Sahabi' and lists exceptional cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical definition of a Companion (Sahabi) and the exceptional individuals who qualify as companions according to Shafi'i jurists.

Core Idea
A Companion is more than just a human; it includes prophets, angels, and the blind.

In this passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed, the author clarifies the technical definition of a Companion (Sahabi) and its scope. He explains that the benefit of referring to the Shafi'i school is regarding naming and competency. Then he lists several exceptional cases: blind individuals like Ibn Umm Maktum, prophets like Jesus, al-Khidr, and Elias, and even angels who met the Prophet. This expands our understanding of who can be considered a Companion.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its purpose
Read the opening sentence which states the benefit of referring to the Shafi'i school: it concerns naming and competency (kafa'a). Understand that a Companion is called so and is considered a suitable match for the daughter of a Companion.
This shows the practical legal implication of the definition.
2
Identify the exceptional cases
List the three categories of individuals who are also considered Companions: blind companions like Ibn Umm Maktum, prophets (Jesus, al-Khidr, Elias), and angels who met the Prophet. Note that Jesus is stated as the last Companion among visible humans.
These expand the scope beyond what one might initially assume.
3
Reflect on the implications about al-Khidr and Elias
The passage concludes with the relied-upon view that al-Khidr and Elias are still alive. al-Khidr will die when the Qur'an is raised. Consider how this affects their status as Companions.
This shows that Companionship is not limited to those who died before the Prophet.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة (1) ، وفائدة من الرجوع في ذلك لمذهب الشافعية على ما كان يرتضيه بعضُ أشياخهم عودها التسمية والكفاءة ; فيسمَّى صحابيا، ويكون كفؤاً لبنت الصحابي. ويدخلُ في الصحابي ابنُ أم مكتوم ونحوه من العميان، وكُنيت أمه به لكنم بصره، واسمُهُ عبد الله ; أحد المؤذنين له ، ويدخل عيسى والخضر وإلياس عليهم الصلاة والسلام (۲) ، وتدخل الملائكة الذين اجتمعوا به في الأرض(۳)، فعيسى عليه صلى الله عليه وسلم الصلاة والسلام آخرُ الصحابة من البشر الظاهرين، وأما الملائكة فباقون إلى النفخة، والخضرُ يموتُ عند رفع القرآن، وقيل: بل مات والحاصل أن الخضر وإلياس حيَّان على المعتمد

And included in the Companion is Ibn Umm Maktum and similar blind persons, and his mother was nicknamed for the blindness of his sight, and his name is `Abdullah; one of his mu'adhdhins. And included are Jesus, al-Khidr, and Elias, peace be upon them (2), and the angels who met him on earth are included (3). So Jesus, peace and blessings be upon him, is the last of the Companions from visible humans. As for the angels, they remain until the Blast, and al-Khidr will die when the Qur'an is raised, and it is said: rather he died. The conclusion is that al-Khidr and Elias are alive according to the relied-upon view.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage defines the technical term 'Sahabi' and discusses its scope. The author begins by stating that according to certain Shafi'i shaykhs, the benefit of referring to that school is regarding naming and competency: a person is called a Companion and is considered a suitable match for the daughter of a Companion. Then the author lists exceptional cases: Ibn Umm Maktum (a blind Companion) and similar blind individuals are included. Jesus, al-Khidr, and Elias are included as Companions because they met the Prophet in their earthly bodies before death. Angels who met the Prophet are also included. The passage then clarifies that Jesus is the last Companion among visible humans. Angels remain until the Trumpet Blast. Al-Khidr will die when the Qur'an is raised from the earth, though some say he has already died. The final conclusion is that the relied-upon view is that al-Khidr and Elias are alive.

Definition of Knowledge (al-ʿIlm)
Sanusi defines knowledge and clarifies its intended meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of knowledge as perception of reality, its customary uses in Islamic sciences, and why the intended meaning here is the first definition.

Core Idea
Knowledge is perceiving things as they truly are.

The commentator begins by defining the term that underpins all of ʿilm al-kalām: knowledge itself. He provides a concise technical definition from authoritative scholars, then notes that the word 'knowledge' is also used in a broader sense for the codified disciplines and for the faculty of reasoning. Identifying the precise meaning is crucial for understanding the subsequent discussion on its obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of knowledge
Read the Arabic slowly, noting the authoritative sources cited.
2
Distinguish the customary uses
Notice that the commentator mentions two customary uses: the codified principles and the faculty. Ask yourself why he mentions these.
3
Identify the intended meaning
The commentator says the intended meaning here is the first definition. Look for the evidence he provides (the ruling of obligation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعلم : إدراك الشيء بحقيقته كما قاله الراغب (۲) ، وهو كقول شيخ الإسلام: إدراك الشيء على ما هو به (۳)، ويطلق حقيقة عرفية على القواعد المدوّنة، وعلى الملكة التي يُقتدرُ بها على إدراكات جزئية ) ، والمراد هنا الأول؛ بدليل الحكم عليه بالتحتُم، ومقابله الجهلُ؛ وهو إما بسيط وإما مركب فالأول: عدم العلم بالشيء شأنه العلم والثاني : إدراك الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه في الواقع، وإنما سمي مركباً لاستلزامه جهلين؛ جهله بالشيء، وجهله بأنه جاهل؛ وفي ذلك قيل : [من الطويل] جَهِلْتَ وَمَا تَدْرِي بِأَنَّكَ جَاهِلٌ وَمَنْ لِي بِأَنْ تَدْرِي بِأَنَّكَ لَا تَدْرِي ؟ (٥)
And knowledge: perception of a thing according to its reality, as al-Raghib said, and it is like the saying of Shaykh al-Islam: perception of a thing as it is in itself, and it is used in a customary real sense for the codified principles, and for the faculty by which one is able to perceive particulars, and what is intended here is the first; by evidence of the ruling of it as obligatory. Its opposite is ignorance; and it is either simple or compound. The first: lack of knowledge of a thing that should be known. The second: perception of a thing contrary to what it is in reality, and it is called compound because it entails two ignorances: his ignorance of the thing, and his ignorance that he is ignorant. And concerning that it is said: [Tawil] 'You were ignorant and do not know that you are ignorant, and how I wish that you would know that you do not know?'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge (al-'ilm) and its opposite, ignorance (al-jahl). Knowledge is defined as perception (idrak) of a thing according to its reality (haqiqa), citing al-Raghib and Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari. The author then notes that 'ilm is used in a customary sense to refer to the codified sciences (al-qawa'id al-mudawwana) and to the faculty (al-malaka) enabling one to perceive particulars. However, the intended meaning here is the first definition, as evidenced by the ruling that it is obligatory. Ignorance is then divided into two types: simple ignorance (jahl basit), which is the absence of knowledge of something that should be known, and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), which is perceiving something contrary to its reality. This is called compound because it necessitates two ignorances: ignorance of the thing itself and ignorance of being ignorant. The poem attributed to al-Anbari illustrates the tragedy of compound ignorance, where one does not even know that they are ignorant.

Key Terms
العلم
الإدراك
الملكة
القواعد المدونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the intended meaning is the first definition—perception of reality—and that the other uses are secondary.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge in its primary sense is the perception of a thing as it truly is; this is the meaning relevant to theological obligation.

Ignorance: Simple and Compound
Sanusi divides ignorance into simple and compound, explaining the latter's severity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of ignorance—simple and compound—and why compound ignorance is considered more severe.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is double ignorance: not knowing, and not knowing that you don't know.

Having defined knowledge, the commentator turns to its opposite: ignorance (al-jahl). He divides it into two kinds, each with a distinct character. The distinction is not merely academic; recognizing compounded ignorance helps us understand why some errors persist. The poetic verse at the end captures the tragedy of the one who is unaware of his own ignorance.

Guided Path
1
Read the types of ignorance
Read the Arabic for simple and compound ignorance. Notice the definitions.
2
Understand why compound ignorance is called 'compound'
The commentator gives the reason: it entails two ignorances. Make sure you can identify both.
3
Reflect on the poetic illustration
The verse expresses the lament over compound ignorance. Read it and see how it reinforces the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والعلم : إدراك الشيء بحقيقته كما قاله الراغب (۲) ، وهو كقول شيخ الإسلام: إدراك الشيء على ما هو به (۳)، ويطلق حقيقة عرفية على القواعد المدوّنة، وعلى الملكة التي يُقتدرُ بها على إدراكات جزئية ) ، والمراد هنا الأول؛ بدليل الحكم عليه بالتحتُم، ومقابله الجهلُ؛ وهو إما بسيط وإما مركب فالأول: عدم العلم بالشيء شأنه العلم والثاني : إدراك الشيء على خلاف ما هو عليه في الواقع، وإنما سمي مركباً لاستلزامه جهلين؛ جهله بالشيء، وجهله بأنه جاهل؛ وفي ذلك قيل : [من الطويل] جَهِلْتَ وَمَا تَدْرِي بِأَنَّكَ جَاهِلٌ وَمَنْ لِي بِأَنْ تَدْرِي بِأَنَّكَ لَا تَدْرِي ؟ (٥)
And knowledge: perception of a thing according to its reality, as al-Raghib said, and it is like the saying of Shaykh al-Islam: perception of a thing as it is in itself, and it is used in a customary real sense for the codified principles, and for the faculty by which one is able to perceive particulars, and what is intended here is the first; by evidence of the ruling of it as obligatory. Its opposite is ignorance; and it is either simple or compound. The first: lack of knowledge of a thing that should be known. The second: perception of a thing contrary to what it is in reality, and it is called compound because it entails two ignorances: his ignorance of the thing, and his ignorance that he is ignorant. And concerning that it is said: [Tawil] 'You were ignorant and do not know that you are ignorant, and how I wish that you would know that you do not know?'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage defines knowledge (al-'ilm) and its opposite, ignorance (al-jahl). Knowledge is defined as perception (idrak) of a thing according to its reality (haqiqa), citing al-Raghib and Shaykh al-Islam Zakariyya al-Ansari. The author then notes that 'ilm is used in a customary sense to refer to the codified sciences (al-qawa'id al-mudawwana) and to the faculty (al-malaka) enabling one to perceive particulars. However, the intended meaning here is the first definition, as evidenced by the ruling that it is obligatory. Ignorance is then divided into two types: simple ignorance (jahl basit), which is the absence of knowledge of something that should be known, and compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), which is perceiving something contrary to its reality. This is called compound because it necessitates two ignorances: ignorance of the thing itself and ignorance of being ignorant. The poem attributed to al-Anbari illustrates the tragedy of compound ignorance, where one does not even know that they are ignorant.

Correction: The passage shows that simple ignorance is a lack, but compound ignorance is a false belief combined with unawareness of that false belief.

Why Singular 'Foundation' Means All Principles
Commentator explains the phrase 'asl al-din' and justifies its singular form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the poet used the singular 'asl' to refer to the multiple principles of religion and how this reflects a standard linguistic concession in Islamic poetry.

Core Idea
One word can mean many when context and meter demand it.

In this lesson, we examine a brief but important commentary on a line from the Jawharah. The commentator explains why the poet used the singular 'foundation' instead of the plural 'principles'—a choice driven by poetic meter, not theological meaning. This teaches us about the interplay between language and theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the commentator's point.
Notice the phrase 'مفرد مضاف يعم' – a singular construct that is general in meaning.
2
Identify the commentator's explanation
The commentator says 'asl al-din means 'by its principles and bases'. So the singular actually refers to multiple things.
3
Understand why singular is used
The poet used the singular because of the meter of the poem (darurat al-nazm). This is a permissible linguistic concession.
This is a common technique in Arabic poetry.
4
Reflect on the broader lesson
Theological terms can be adjusted for poetic form without changing their meaning. This shows the flexibility of language in conveying doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : بأصل الدين ) أي : بأصوله وقواعده، فهو مفرد مضاف يعم، وأفرد الأصل مع أن هذا الفنّ ملقب بأصول الدين، لضرورة النظم، فهو من التصرُّف في العلم لما ذكر .
His saying: "by the foundation of religion" means: by its principles and bases. It is a singular construct that encompasses. The singular 'foundation' was used even though this art is called 'Principles of Religion' due to the necessity of the meter, so it is a linguistic concession in the science as mentioned.

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase "بأصل الدين" from the poem. The commentator explains that 'asl al-din means the principles (usul) and bases (qawa'id) of religion. The word 'asl is singular but in a construct form that indicates generality ('ummum). The author uses the singular 'asl' even though the science is called 'usul al-din' (plural) because of poetic necessity (nature al-nazm). This is a permissible linguistic concession.

The Necessity of Brevity and the Three Modes of Expression
Commentator defines three modes of expression and justifies brevity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale for brevity in theological writing, the definitions of tatwil, ikhtisar, and musawah, and the balance required to avoid deficient abbreviation.

Core Idea
True brevity is not mere shortness but delivering meaning without distortion.

The commentator begins by justifying the necessity of abbreviation in this art, despite its need for clarification. He warns against excessive elongation that leads to boredom, and then precisely defines three modes of expression: elongation, abbreviation, and equality. This lesson unpacks these definitions and the underlying principle that abbreviation must be non-deficient to be praiseworthy.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Problem
Read why the commentator says elongation causes weariness and why abbreviation becomes obligatory. Notice the distinction between basic elongation and excessive elongation.
The line is an istidrak (correction) on a previous statement.
2
Learn the Three Definitions
Identify the definitions of tatwil, ikhtisar, and musawah. Focus on how each relates the amount of wording to the conventional level.
The conventional level is defined relative to people of average eloquence.
3
Analyze 'Aspirations Became Weary'
Examine the explanation of 'kallat al-himam' and the concept of himma. Understand the mental metaphor and the classification of aspirations into high, low, or neither.
This illustrates the psychological effect of lengthy discourse.
4
Grasp the Condition of Abbreviation
Note that abbreviation is obligatory only when it is non-deficient. Deficient abbreviation is blameworthy. This balances the need for brevity with the duty to preserve meaning.
The quote from al-Isfarayini shows how brevity can condense all of tawhid into two principles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٦ - لَكِنْ مِنَ التَّطْوِيلِ كَلَّتِ الهِمَمْ فَصَارَ فِيهِ الاخْتِصَارُ مُلْتَزَمُ 
قوله : (لَكِنْ ... إلى آخره: استدراك على قوله : (يحتاج للتبيين) لأنه يقتضي مزيد التطويل، فدفع ذلك بقوله : (لكن ... إلى آخره، فكأنه قال: هذا الفن وإن احتاج للتبيين إلا أنه لا ينبغي المبالغة معه في تطويل العبارة؛ لأنها تؤدي إلى الملل والسامة .
وقوله : (مِنَ التَّطْوِيلِ) أي : من أجله وسببه، فـ (من) للتعليل، والمراد: التطويلُ الكامل، فـ (أل) فيه للكمال، فالمحذورُ إنما هو المبالغة فى التطويل، وأما أصلُ التطويل فلا يضرُّ ؛ والتطويلُ : أداء المقصود بلفظ زائد على المتعارف لأوساط الناس الذين ليس لهم فصاحة ولا بلاغةٌ، وأما الاختصارُ : فهو أداء المقصود بأقل من العبارة المتعارفة، والمساواة : أداء المقصود بلفظ مساو للمتعارف .
قوله : (كَلْتِ الهِمَمْ) أي : تعبت أصحابها، ففيه مجاز عقلي، و(الهمم): جمع همة؛ وهي لغة القوَّةُ والعزم، وعرفاً حالةٌ للنفس يتبعها غلبه انبعاث إلى نيل مقصود ما، ثم إن تعلَّقت بمعالي الأمور، فعليَّة، وإلا فدنيَّة، وإذا لم تتعلق بواحد منهما، فليست علية ولا دنية .
قوله : (فَصَارَ فِيهِ الاِخْتِصَارُ مُلْتَزَمُ هذه الفاء تفريعية على ما قبلها، والمعنى: فصار في هذا الفنّ تأليفاً وتدريساً الاختصارُ ملتزماً ؛ تقريباً على المتعلمين القاصرين، ولا يخفى أن الاختصار اسم صار وملتزم خبرها لكن وقف عليه بالسكون على لغة ربيعة، والملتزم إنما هو الاختصارُ غير المخلّ، وإلا فهو مذموم، وقد كان الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني يقول : (جميع ما قاله المتكلمون في التوحيد قد جمعه أهل الحق في كلمتين :
الأولى: اعتقاد أن كل ما تصور في الأوهام فالله بخلافه .
والثانية : اعتقاد أن ذاتَهُ تعالى ليست مشبهةً للذوات، ولا معطّلة عن الصفات).
انتهى ملخصاً من حاشية الشيخ الشنواني
6 - But from elongation, the aspirations became weary, so abbreviation became obligatory in it. His saying: (But ... to the end): an istidrak (correction) on his saying: (needs clarification) because it requires more elongation, so he repelled that by his saying: (But ... to the end), as if he said: This art, although it needs clarification, yet one should not exaggerate with it in elongation of expression, because it leads to boredom and weariness. And his saying: (from elongation) means: for its sake and cause, so (min) is for causation, and what is meant is complete elongation, so (al) in it is for perfection, so what is objectionable is only exaggeration in elongation; as for the basic elongation, it does not harm. And elongation is: delivering the intended meaning with wording exceeding the conventional for the middle class of people who have neither eloquence nor rhetoric. And abbreviation is: delivering the intended meaning with less than the conventional expression. And equality is: delivering the intended meaning with wording equal to the conventional. His saying: (the aspirations became weary) means: their possessors became tired, and in it is a mental metaphor. And (al-himam) is plural of himma; which linguistically is strength and resolve, and conventionally is a state of the soul followed by a prevailing impulse to attain something sought. Then if it is attached to lofty matters, it is high; otherwise, base; and if it is attached to neither, it is neither high nor base. His saying: (so abbreviation became obligatory in it) – this fa' is for branching from what preceded. The meaning: so in this art, both in authorship and teaching, abbreviation became obligatory, as a facilitation for deficient students. And it is not hidden that (al-ikhtisar) is the noun of (sara) and (multazam) is its predicate, but he paused on it with sukun in the dialect of Rabi'ah. And what is obligatory is only non-deficient abbreviation; otherwise it is blameworthy. And the master Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini used to say: 'All that the mutakallimun have said concerning tawhid has been collected by the people of truth in two words: The first: the belief that everything that is imagined in fantasies, Allah is contrary to it. The second: the belief that His essence, exalted, is not likened to essences, nor stripped of attributes.' End, summarized from the gloss of Shaykh al-Shanwani.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem. The commentator begins by explaining that the line is an istidrak (correction) on a previous statement that the art needs clarification. He clarifies that while the art does need clarification, one should not go to extremes in elongating the explanation, as that leads to boredom and weariness. Then he analyzes the phrase "min al-tatwil" (from elongation), explaining that "min" is causal and that what is meant is complete elongation, with the definite article indicating perfection; what is objectionable is exaggeration, not elongation per se. He then defines three modes of expression: tatwil (elongation) which is using more words than the conventional level appropriate for average people without eloquence; ikhtisar (abbreviation) which is using fewer words; and musawah (equality) which is using the conventional number of words. Next, he explains the phrase "kallat al-himam" (the aspirations became weary) as meaning that the possessors of those aspirations became tired, noting that this is a mental metaphor (majaz 'aqli). He defines himma (aspiration) linguistically and conventionally, and categorizes it as either high (concerning lofty matters), base (concerning lowly matters), or neither. Then he discusses the line "fa-sara fihi al-ikhtisar multazam" (so abbreviation became obligatory in it), noting that the conjunction "fa" indicates a branching conclusion. The meaning is that in this art, both in writing and teaching, abbreviation is necessary to make it accessible to weak students. He notes a grammatical point about the predicate being stopped with sukun due to the dialect of Rabi'ah, and stresses that only non-deficient abbreviation is obligatory; deficient abbreviation is blameworthy. Finally, he recounts a quote from Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini summarizing all that the mutakallimun said about tawhid in two principles: (1) belief that everything imagined is contrary to Allah, and (2) belief that Allah's essence is neither likened to other essences nor stripped of attributes. This summary is taken from al-Shanwani's gloss.

Key Terms
تطويل
اختصار
مساواة
همة
مجاز عقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that brevity means always using the fewest words possible.
Correction: The commentator distinguishes between deficient abbreviation (blameworthy) and non-deficient abbreviation (praiseworthy). True brevity conveys the full meaning concisely without distortion.

Checkpoint

A) Delivering meaning with wording exceeding the conventional.
B) Delivering meaning with wording exactly equal to the conventional.
C) Delivering meaning with wording less than the conventional expression.
D) Delivering meaning with wording ambiguous.

Key Takeaway
