The Referent of 'هذه' and al-Jurjani's Seven Possibilities
Explains the referent of 'هذه', cites al-Jurjani's seven possibilities, and raises two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical discussion about the referent of the word 'this' in the opening of the Jawharat al-Tawhid, including the seven possibilities considered by al-Jurjani and the logical reasons for choosing one.

Core Idea
The referent of 'this' is not the uttered words but the mental words signifying meanings.

The commentator delves into a precise logical analysis of the demonstrative 'هذه' in the poem's opening line. He clarifies that the words that are the 'urjuza' exist mentally, not physically after utterance. Al-Jurjani's seven possibilities show the thoroughness of kalam methodology in determining the exact referent. This passage demonstrates how kalam scholars apply logic and ontological distinctions to theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial clarification
Read the first part where the commentator explains that 'هذه' refers to words present in the mind, not external utterances, because words perish upon utterance. Note the refutation of the alternative view.
This sets the stage for the seven possibilities.
2
Review al-Jurjani's seven possibilities
Al-Jurjani lists seven possibilities for what the book's name refers to: words, meanings, written marks, or combinations. Identify each option and note his chosen view: words in consideration of their indication of meanings.
The seventh possibility is qualified—it is words considering their meaning-indication.
3
Follow the elimination of six possibilities
The commentator explains why meanings and written marks cannot be the referent: meanings are dependent on words, written marks are not universally or constantly available. Trace how this eliminates four possibilities involving meanings and two involving written marks, leaving only the seventh.
This logical elimination is key to understanding kalam reasoning.
4
Examine the two objections
Two objections are raised: (1) mental words are summary (mujmal) whereas 'urjuza' is detailed, and (2) the referent is particular (the author's mind) but 'urjuza' is universal. Note that the answer by Shaykh 'Abd al-Salam is not fully quoted but involves supplying an extra word (taqdir mudafayn).
The lesson ends mid-answer; consider consulting the full text for completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تسمية الأرجوزة وعدد أبياتها] - وَهَذِهِ أُرْجُوزَةٌ لَقَيْتُهَا جَوْهَرَةَ التَّوْحِيدِ قَدْ هَذَبْتُهَا قوله : (وَهذِهِ) الواو للاستئناف، والمشار إليه بهذه هو الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن باعتبار دلالتها على المعاني المخصوصة؛ سواء كانت الخطبة متقدمة على التأليف أو متأخرةً عنه، وما قيل : من أنه إن كانت الخطبة سابقةً على التأليف فالمشار إليه الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن، وإن كانت متأخرةً عنه فالمشار إليه الألفاظ الموجودة في الخارج غير مستقيم ؛ لأن الألفاظ أعراض تنقضي بمجرد النطق بها، فلا تبقى موجودة في الخارج، بل تنعدم حرفاً بعد حرف وهكذا . وقد أبدى السيد الجرجاني في مُسمّى الكتب والتراجم - بالكسر ـ احتمالات سبعة هل هو الألفاظ فقط، أو المعاني فقط، أو النقوش فقط، أو الألفاظ والمعاني، أو الألفاظ والنقوش، أو المعاني والنقوش أو الثلاثة؟ واختار أنه الألفاظ باعتبار دلالتها على المعاني (۲) ، وهل هذا الاحتمال من السبعة أو احتمال ثامن؟ قولان؛ والأظهر أنه منها غاية الأمر أنه مقيد باعتبار المعاني. وأما ما وقع في عبارة بعضهم من أن المختار أنه المعاني المستحضرة ذهناً، فهو خلافُ المشهور، ووجه عدم اختيار باقي الاحتمالات أن المعاني غير مستقلة؛ لتوقفها على الألفاظ، فلا تصح (۳) أن تكون مدلولاً ولا جزء مدلول، فبطل أربع احتمالات؛ أن المشار إليه هو المعاني وحدها، أو مع الألفاظ، أو مع النقوش، أو معهما، وهي وأن النقوش لا تتيسر من كلِّ أحد، ولا في كل وقت كتيسر الألفاظ، فلا تصح أن تكون مدلولاً ولا جزءً مدلول، فبطل احتمالان وهما كون المشار إليه هو النقوش وحدها، أو مع الألفاظ ؛ فبطلت احتمالات سنةٌ، وتعيَّن (۱) الاحتمال السابع. وها هنا سؤالان: أحدهما : أن الألفاظ المستحضرة في الذهن مجملةً، مع أن الأرجوزة اسم للمفصَّل بابا بابا (۳) ، فلم يحصل (٤) التطابق بين المبتدأ والخبر . (1) (۲) وثانيهما (٥) : أن المشار إليه ما في ذهن المصنف فقط، فهو جزئي ، مع أن الأرجوزة اسمٌ للألفاظ سواءٌ كانت في ذهن المؤلف أو في ذهن غيره، فهي اسم للكلي لا للجزئي. وقد أجاب الشيخ عبد السلام عن هذين السؤالين بتقدير مضافين حيث قال:
[Naming of the urjuza and number of its verses] - 'And this is an urjuza (didactic poem) that I have called Jawharat al-Tawhid (The Jewel of Monotheism), which I have refined.' His saying: (And this) — the waw is for resumption, and the referent of 'this' is the words present in the mind in consideration of their indication of specific meanings, whether the preface precedes the composition or follows it. And what is said — that if the preface precedes the composition, then the referent is the words present in the mind, and if it follows it, then the referent is the words existing externally — is not sound, because words are accidents that pass away upon their utterance, so they do not remain existing externally; rather, they cease letter by letter, and so on. And al-Sayyid al-Jurjani presented, in the naming of books and biographies (with kasra), seven possibilities: is it only the words, or only the meanings, or only the written marks, or the words and meanings, or the words and written marks, or the meanings and written marks, or all three? And he chose that it is the words in consideration of their indication of the meanings. And is this possibility one of the seven or an eighth? Two opinions; the more apparent is that it is from them, except that it is qualified by the consideration of meanings. And as for what occurred in the expression of some, that the chosen view is that it is the meanings present in the mind, that is contrary to the well-known. And the reason for not choosing the remaining possibilities is that the meanings are not independent, because they depend on words, so they cannot be a referent or part of a referent. Thus four possibilities are invalidated: that the referent is only the meanings, or with the words, or with the written marks, or with both. And that the written marks are not easily available from everyone, nor at all times as words are easily available, so they cannot be a referent or part of a referent. Thus two possibilities are invalidated: that the referent is only the written marks, or with the words. So six possibilities are invalidated, and the seventh possibility is determined. And here are two questions: The first: that the words present in the mind are summary (mujmal), while 'urjuza' is a name for something detailed chapter by chapter, so there is no correspondence (tatabuq) between the subject and predicate. The second: that the referent is only what is in the mind of the author, so it is particular (juz'i), while 'urjuza' is a name for words whether they are in the mind of the author or in the mind of another, so it is a name for the universal (kulli), not the particular. And Shaykh 'Abd al-Salam answered these two questions by presuming an extra word (taqdir mudafayn), saying:
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exegetical and logical analysis of the opening verse of the Jawharat al-Tawhid. It begins by quoting the verse and then explains the demonstrative 'هذه'. The commentator argues that the referent is the words in the mind (as mental entities) that signify specific meanings, not the external uttered words, because words perish upon utterance. He then introduces al-Jurjani's seven possibilities for what 'the book' refers to (words, meanings, written marks, or combinations). Al-Jurjani's choice is that it is words in their capacity as indicating meanings. The discussion then examines whether this is the seventh or an eighth possibility, settling that it is the seventh but qualified. The other six possibilities are refuted: meanings are dependent on words, so cannot be the sole referent; written marks are not universally or constantly available. After eliminating six, the seventh is confirmed. Two objections arise: 1) the mental words are summary, but 'urjuza' denotes something detailed, creating a mismatch; 2) the referent is particular (the author's mental words), but 'urjuza' is a universal term. The answer is given by Shaykh 'Abd al-Salam: by assuming an extra word (e.g., 'these words that I have composed') the issues are resolved. The answer is not fully quoted, but the direction is clear.

Correction: The commentary shows it refers to the mental words present in the mind that signify specific meanings, not external copies or sounds.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The referent of 'هذه' is the mental words signifying specific meanings, not the physical copy, and this is determined through a logical elimination of six other possibilities.

?

?
The second objection highlights a mismatch between a particular instance and a universal term. In your own life, have you ever mistaken a personal experience for a universal truth, or vice versa? What steps can you take to distinguish between the two?
?
The passage shows that even a single word like 'this' requires deep analysis to avoid misunderstanding. When you recite the Qur'an, do you pause to consider what each demonstrative 'هذا' or 'ذلك' is pointing to, or do you gloss over it? Definition of 'Urjuzah' and Its Verse Counts
Al-Bajuri defines the term 'urjuzah' and explains its verse counts and advantages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what an 'urjuzah' is, its metrical structure, and the significance of its verse counts.

Core Idea
An 'urjuzah' is a small poem in rajaz meter; its verse count depends on complete or half classification.

Al-Bajuri begins by defining the term 'urjuzah' that appears in the poem's title. He explains its metrical form and gives two possible verse counts, highlighting the features that make this poem approachable and beneficial for students.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms: أرجوزة, بحر الرجز, كامل, مشطور.
This is a definition passage.
2
Understand the two verse counts
The commentator provides two counts: 144 if full rajaz, 288 if half-rajaz. Ask: why two possibilities?
The poem itself is composed in rajaz, but the classification affects the count.
3
Appreciate the encouragement
Notice the three reasons given for encouraging engagement: verse form, easy meter, small size.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أرجوزة) أي: منظومة من بحر الرجز صغيرة الحجم، أبياتها مئة وأربعة وأربعون بناء على أنها من كامل الرجز، ومئتان وثمانية وثمانون بناء على أنها من مشطوره، ففيه ترغيب في تعاطيها من جهة كونها نظما؛ لأن النظم أعذب وأحلى من النثر، ومن جهة كونها من بحر الرجز؛ لأنه أسهل من غيره من البحور، ومن جهة كونها صغيرة الحجم؛ فإن لفظ (أرجوزة) دال على القلة عرفا.
His saying: (An urjuzah) i.e., a versified composition in the meter of rajaz, small in size, its verses are one hundred and forty-four according to it being from the complete form of rajaz, and two hundred and eighty-eight according to it being from its half-meter. So in it is encouragement to engage with it from the aspect of it being verse, because verse is sweeter and more pleasant than prose, and from the aspect of it being in the meter of rajaz, because it is easier than other meters, and from the aspect of it being small in size, for the word 'urjuzah' indicates smallness by convention.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the term 'أرجوزة' (urjuzah), which is the title of the poem being commented upon. The commentator defines it as a poetic composition in the rajaz meter, small in size. He then provides two possible counts for the number of verses: 144 if the poem is considered full-rajaz (كامل الرجز), or 288 if considered half-rajaz (مشطور الرجز). The commentator then encourages engagement with the poem for three reasons: first, it is in verse, which is sweeter than prose; second, it uses the rajaz meter, which is easier than other meters; third, it is small in size, making it easy to memorize.

Defining Acceptance (Qabul) and Its Nuance
Commentator defines qabul and explains the distinction between acceptance and satisfaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of qabul (acceptance) in theological context and how it differs from mere satisfaction.

Core Idea
Acceptance means recompense plus satisfaction without objection.

This passage clarifies a crucial term in Islamic theology: qabul (acceptance). The commentator first explains the literal meaning, then refines it by distinguishing acceptance from satisfaction, which can exist with objection. Understanding this nuance prevents confusion in discussions of divine reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the technical terms used.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find the two definitions given for qabul. One is 'recompense for correct action'; the other is 'satisfaction with something while leaving off objection'.
The second definition adds a crucial condition.
3
Reflect on the nuance
Consider why the commentator emphasizes 'leaving off objection'. Why can't mere satisfaction be enough? The provided example from Ibn Malik helps illustrate.
This distinction ensures that acceptance is complete approval.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فِي القَبُولِ) أي : في حصول القبول فهو على تقدير مضاف، ومعنى القبول : الإثابة على العمل الصحيح، وقيل: الرضا بالشيء مع ترك الاعتراض عليه. وإنما قلنا : مع ترك الاعتراض لأن الرضا قد يكون مع اعتراض كما يقتضيه قول ابن مالك : [من الرجز] وَنَقْتَضِي رِضاً بِغَيْرِ سُخْطِ. نبه على ذلك الشيخ الملوي.
His saying: 'concerning acceptance (al-qabul)' means: concerning the occurrence of acceptance, so it is on the basis of an omitted genitive construction. The meaning of 'acceptance' is: recompense for correct action. And it is said: satisfaction with something while leaving off objection to it. We only say 'while leaving off objection' because satisfaction may occur with objection, as is necessitated by the saying of Ibn Malik: 'And we demand satisfaction without displeasure.' Al-Mallawi drew attention to this.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'qabul' (acceptance) as used in the author's work. The commentator first explains that the phrase 'fi al-qabul' implies an omitted genitive construction, meaning 'concerning the occurrence of acceptance.' He then gives two definitions: first, acceptance is recompense for correct action; second, it is satisfaction with something while forgoing objection. The nuance of forgoing objection is highlighted because satisfaction itself can coexist with objection, as Ibn Malik's poetic line suggests: 'We demand satisfaction without displeasure.' This distinction ensures that acceptance in the theological sense is complete approval without reservation, not merely a state of being satisfied while internally objecting.

Key Terms
قبول
إثابة
رضا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Acceptance (qabul) is the same as satisfaction (rida).
Correction: Acceptance requires both satisfaction and the absence of objection; satisfaction alone may coexist with objection.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what additional element distinguishes qabul from mere satisfaction?
A) It includes recompense only.
B) It includes satisfaction without objection.
C) It excludes any form of displeasure.
D) It is a genitive construction.

Key Takeaway
Acceptance in theological context means divine recompense for correct action, characterized by complete approval without any trace of objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Consider the definition of qabul as 'recompense for correct action.' Does this challenge your understanding of earning Allah's acceptance? What actions in your life are you performing with the hope of acceptance, and how can you ensure they are correct and sincere?

Definition of True Knowledge and Its Exclusions
Al-Bajuri defines ma'rifa as certain knowledge matching reality from evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of true knowledge (ma'rifa/ilm) as certainty matching reality from evidence, and the five things that are excluded from it.

Core Idea
True knowledge is certain, real, and evidence-based — nothing else counts.

Before we can discuss what must be known about Allah, we need a clear criterion for what counts as knowledge. Al-Bajuri, commenting on the poem, provides a precise definition that distinguishes true belief from guesswork, doubt, and blind imitation. This definition will be the foundation for all subsequent theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic source passage slowly. Then read the literal translation. This will give you the author's own words before any interpretation.
2
Identify the three components of the definition
The definition of knowledge has three parts: certainty, correspondence to reality, and being based on evidence. Find each part in the text and think about why each is necessary.
3
List the five exclusions
The passage explicitly excludes conjecture (zann), doubt (shakk), illusion (wahm), non-corresponding certainty (like Christian Trinity), and taqlid (blind following). Make a mental note of why each fails to meet the definition.
4
Reflect on the difference between taqlid and the other four
Notice that the first four exclusions make someone a disbeliever unanimously, but taqlid is debated. This highlights the importance of evidence in defining true knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَجَبَا عَلَيْهِ ... إلخ) : هذه الجملة خبر المبتدأ الذي هو : كل من كلف)؛ و(عليه) متعلق بـ (وجبا ) ، والألف فيه للإطلاق. وقوله : (أَنْ يَعْرِفَ) أي معرفة، فـ (أن) والفعل في تأويل مصدر، وهو فاعل (وجب). والمعرفة والعلم مترادفانِ على معنى واحدٍ على التحقيق، وهذا المعنى الواحد هو الجزم المطابق للواقع عن دليل. فخرج بالجزم الظنُّ والشكُّ والوهم . وبالمطابق : غيرُ المطابق ؛ كجزم النصارى بالتثليث . وبما بعده التقليد، فليس كل منهما معرفة. والمتصف بشيء من الأربعة الأولِ في شيء من العقائد الآتية)، كافر اتفاقاً ؛ وأما المتصف بالتقليد فسيأتي ذكرُ الخلاف فيه . قوله : (مَا قَدْ وَجَبًا الله ) أي : جميع ما وجب الله ؛ لأنَّ (ما) من صيغ العموم، لكن ما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه تفصيلاً - وهو العشرون الآتية - يجب على المكلَّف أن يعرفه كذلك ؛ أعني تفصيلاً، وما قامت الأدلة العقلية أو النقلية عليه إجمالاً - وهو سائر الكمالات - يجب على المكلّف أن يعرفه كذلك؛ أعني : إجمالاً، وكذا يقال في المستحيل . وفي البيت التضمينُ المتقدم ، والألف في (وجبا ) للإطلاق، ولا إيطاء في کلامه وإن قلنا : إن هذه المقدمة من مشطور الرجز، كما تقدَّم في نظيره؛ لأن الوجوب الأوّل بالشرع، والثاني بالعقل غالباً.
His saying: 'It is obligatory upon him... etc.': This sentence is the predicate of the subject which is 'everyone who is obligated'; and 'upon him' is related to 'obligatory', and the alif is for absolute termination. And his saying: 'that he knows' means knowledge, so 'that' and the verb are in the interpretation of a verbal noun, and it is the subject of 'obligatory'. And knowledge (maʿrifa) and knowledge (ʿilm) are synonymous in one meaning according to verification, and this one meaning is certainty (al-jazm) corresponding to reality (al-muṭābiq li-l-wāqiʿ) based on evidence (ʿan dalīl). So by 'certainty' are excluded conjecture (al-ẓann), doubt (al-shakk), and illusion (al-wahm). By 'corresponding' is excluded that which does not correspond, like the Christians' certainty of the Trinity. And by what follows it (i.e., the condition of evidence) is excluded taqlīd (blind following), so neither of these is knowledge. And the one described by any of the first four in any of the following beliefs is a disbeliever unanimously; but as for the one described by taqlīd, the disagreement concerning it will come. His saying: 'what has been obligated by God' means all that God has obligated, because 'ma' is among the forms of generality. However, what is established by rational or transmitted evidences in detail—and they are the twenty coming [attributes]—it is obligatory for the obligated person to know it likewise, i.e., in detail; and what is established by rational or transmitted evidences in general—and they are the rest of the perfections—it is obligatory for the obligated person to know it likewise, i.e., in general. And similarly is said concerning the impossible. And in the verse there is the aforementioned inclusion (al-taḍmīn al-mutaqaddim), and the alif in 'wajabā' is for absolute termination, and there is no iṭāʾ (bad prosody) in his speech even if we say that this preamble (al-muqaddima) is from the portion of rajaz, as was mentioned in its counterpart, because the first obligation is by revelation, and the second by reason in most cases.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed commentary on a line of the poem about the obligation to know God. It first explains the grammatical structure of the verse. Then it defines true knowledge (maʿrifa or ʿilm) as: certainty (al-jazm) that corresponds to reality (al-muṭābiq li-l-wāqiʿ) and is based on evidence (ʿan dalīl). From this definition, four things are excluded: (1) conjecture (ẓann), (2) doubt (shakk), (3) illusion (wahm), and (4) non-corresponding certainty (like the Christians' belief in the Trinity). Also excluded is taqlīd (blind acceptance without evidence). A person who holds any of these four (ẓann, shakk, wahm, or non-correspondence) regarding essential beliefs is unanimously a disbeliever. For taqlīd, there is disagreement, which will be addressed later. The passage then explains that the phrase 'what God has obligated' includes all obligations. The obligated person must know God's attributes in detail (the twenty essential attributes) and the impossible attributes in general. The first obligation (to know God) is by revelation (sharʿ), while the second obligation (to know by evidence) is by reason (ʿaql) in most cases.

Key Terms
المعرفة
العلم
الجزم
المطابقة
الدليل
الظن
الشك
الوهم
التقليد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that blindly following authority (taqlīd) is a valid way to have true religious knowledge.
Correction: Taqlīd is excluded because true knowledge requires evidence. Without evidence, it is not certain knowledge, though it may be more excusable than other invalid forms.

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following is excluded from true knowledge?
A) Certainty based on evidence
B) Certainty that does not correspond to reality
C) Correspondence with reality
D) Rational proofs

Key Takeaway
True knowledge is certain, corresponds to reality, and is based on evidence; anything less or different is not genuine knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Bajuri said that true knowledge must be certain, correspond to reality, and be based on evidence. Think of a belief you hold about God that you have only accepted because you were taught it without ever investigating the proof. Are you willing to examine that belief today and seek the evidence for it? What is one step you can take this week to replace taqlīd with knowledge?
?
The Christians are certain of the Trinity, but Al-Bajuri says that certainty without correspondence to reality is not knowledge. You might be certain of many things about your faith, but have you ever considered the possibility that your certainty might be misinformed? Does this thought disturb you? Will you commit to verifying your most certain beliefs against revelation and reason?
?
Al-Bajuri excludes conjecture, doubt, and illusion from knowledge. In your daily practice of faith, do you act on guesses or weak probabilities rather than certain knowledge? For example, do you assume a religious ruling without checking the evidence? What would change in your practice if you insisted on knowledge before action?

Imam al-Haramayn's Definition of the Intellect
The footnote elaborates two interpretations of Imam al-Haramayn's equation of intellect with knowledge of the three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of Imam al-Haramayn's equation of intellect with knowledge of the three rational judgments.

Core Idea
Imam al-Haramayn defined the intellect as knowledge—either conceptual or affirmative—of the three categories.

Imam al-Haramayn's definition of the intellect as knowledge of the necessary, impossible, and possible is foundational in Ash'ari theology. This lesson unpacks two interpretations: one emphasizing conceptual grasp, the other emphasizing axiomatic belief. The source passage explains both.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice that the passage presents two distinct interpretations marked by 'qīl' (it is said).
The passage is a footnote expanding on an earlier reference.
2
Understand the first interpretation
The first interpretation equates intellect with conceptualization (taṣawwur) of the three categories: necessary, impossible, and possible. Focus on the definitions given for each category.
Conceptualization is a mental grasp of meaning without judgment.
3
Understand the second interpretation
The second interpretation equates intellect with affirmation (taṣdīq) of necessary propositions that exemplify the three categories. The examples (one half of two, fire hot, opposites cannot coexist) illustrate each category.
Affirmation involves assent to a proposition as true.
4
Compare and reflect
Consider the difference between mere conceptual understanding and affirmed belief. Ask: Which interpretation seems closer to our everyday notion of 'intellect'?
Both interpretations agree that intellect is knowledge of the three categories, but differ in the type of knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (لأن إمام الحرمين ... إلى آخره قيل : المراد بالمعرفة التي جعلها إمام الحرمين نفس العقل تصور المفاهيم الثلاثة : بأن يتصور أن الواجب ما لا يقبل العدم، وأن المستحيل ما لا يقبل الوجود، وأن الجائز ما يصحُ وُجوده وعدمه . وقيل : المراد بتلك المعرفة: التصديق ببعض الضروريات من الأقسام الثلاثة، كأن يصدق بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين، وبأن النار حارة، وأن النقيضين لا يجتمعان : فكون الواحد نصف الاثنين واجب ضرورةً، وثبوت الحرارة للنار جائز ضرورة، واجتماع النقيضين مستحيل ضرورةً، فالتصديق بذلك وما شابهه هو العقل بناءً على هذا القول، يُؤخذ ذلك من حاشية الشرقاوي على الهدهدي». انتهى أجهوري
His saying: (Because Imam al-Haramayn ... to the end) It is said: What is meant by the knowledge that Imam al-Haramayn made identical to the intellect is the conceptualization (taṣawwur) of the three concepts: that one conceptualizes that the necessary is that which does not admit non-existence, that the impossible is that which does not admit existence, and that the possible is that whose existence and non-existence are sound. And it is said: What is meant by that knowledge is affirmation (taṣdīq) of some necessary things from the three categories, like one's affirming that one is half of two, that fire is hot, and that two opposites cannot coexist: for one being half of two is necessarily true, the heat of fire is necessarily possible, and the coexistence of opposites is necessarily impossible. So affirmation of that and the like is the intellect according to this second opinion. That is taken from the gloss of al-Sharqawi on al-Hadadi. End of al-Ajhuri.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a footnote that expands on the earlier mention of Imam al-Haramayn's view. It presents two interpretations of what Imam al-Haramayn meant by saying that knowledge (ma'rifa) of the three categories is the intellect itself. The first interpretation (al-qīl) is that this knowledge is mere conceptualization (taṣawwur): mentally grasping the definitions of necessary, impossible, and possible. The second interpretation is that it is affirmative belief (taṣdīq) in certain necessary propositions that exemplify these categories: for instance, that one is half of two is necessarily true (wājib), that fire is hot is necessarily possible (jā'iz bi al-ḍarūra), and that opposites cannot coexist is necessarily impossible (mustaḥīl bi al-ḍarūra). According to this second view, the intellect is the set of such axiomatic affirmations. The commentator cites al-Sharqawi's gloss as the source.

Key Terms
إمام الحرمين
المعرفة
العقل
تصور
تصديق
ضروريات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that 'intellect' here means the faculty of reason rather than the actual knowledge of first principles.
Correction: Imam al-Haramayn identifies the intellect with the knowledge itself—either conceptual or affirmative—of the three categories, not the faculty that acquires such knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to the second interpretation in the footnote, what type of knowledge is the intellect?
A) Conceptualization of the three categories
B) Affirmation of certain necessary propositions that exemplify the three categories
C) Deductive reasoning
D) Empirical observation

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Haramayn defines the intellect as knowledge of the three necessary categories—either as conceptual understanding or as affirmed axiomatic beliefs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat your belief in the impossibility of opposites coexisting as a mere concept you've memorized, or as a deep-seated affirmation that shapes how you reason? Which interpretation of the intellect would you say is actually at work in your own mind?
?
When you assert that 'fire is hot,' are you relying on conceptual understanding or on a kind of necessary affirmation? If your intellect is only conceptual, would your certainty be as firm?
?
Which of the two interpretations—conceptualization or affirmation—do you find yourself neglecting in your daily pursuit of knowledge? How might intentionally adopting the other strengthen your intellectual foundation?

The Definition of Taqlid and the Faith of the Muqallid
Commentary defines taqlid and explains the line about the muqallid's faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of taqlid and why the faith of a muqallid is considered to involve hesitation.

Core Idea
Taqlid is accepting without proof; such faith lacks genuine certainty.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a foundational concept: what it means to be a muqallid (one who follows without proof). It defines taqlid precisely and explains why blind following in matters of creed leaves faith imperfect. We will examine the commentator's definitions, the exclusion of guided students, and the example of the crescent moon.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully, then its English translation. Notice how the commentator explains the line of poetry.
The phrase 'idh' indicates causality.
2
Identify the definition of taqlid
Find the sentence: 'wa al-taqlid huwa al-akhdhu bi qawl al-ghayr min ghayri an yu'rafa daliluh.' Understand each component: 'accepting', 'statement of another', 'without knowing its proof'.
3
Examine the crescent moon example
Read the example from Sheikh al-Sanusi. Understand the difference between believing based on testimony and being guided to see the proof oneself.
This illustrates the exclusion of guided students from being muqallids.
4
Grasp what 'hesitation' means here
The line says the muqallid's faith is not free from hesitation. The commentator explains that his certainty (jazzm) is without proof, so it lacks full conviction.
The footnote adds three conditions: certainty, correspondence, and being derived from proof.

Source Text

حكمُ إيمانِ المُقلِّدِ والخلاف فيه ]
۱۱ - إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ فِي التَّوْحِيدِ إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ
قوله : (إِذْ كُلُّ مَنْ قَلَّدَ ... إلخ)، هذا تعليل (۲) لوجوب المعرفة السابقة، فكأنه قال : وإنما وجب على المكلَّف معرفةُ ما ذُكر لأن كلَّ من قلد ... إلى آخره، فـ (إذ) للتعليل.
والتقليد : هو الأخذُ بقول الغير من غير أن يُعرف دليله.
والمراد بالأخذ الاعتقاد؛ أي: اعتقاد مضمون قول الغير. :
والمراد بالقول : ما يشمل الفعل والتقرير أيضاً .
وخرج بقولنا (من غير أن يعرف دليله : التلامذة بعد أن يرشدَهُمُ الأشياخُ للأدلَّة، فهم عارفون لا مقلّدون، وضربَ لهم الشيخ السنوسي مثلاً للفرق بينهم وبين المقلدين بجماعة نظروا للهلال، فسبقَ بعضُهم لرؤيته فأخبرهم ،به ، فإن صدقوه من غير معاينة، كانوا مقلدين وإن أرشدهم بالعلامة حتى عاينوه لم يكونوا مقلدين(۳) وقوله : (فِي التَّوْحِيدِ) أي : في علم العقائد ولو تعلَّقَتْ بالرسل، فليس المراد بالتوحيد إثبات الوحدة بخصوصه .
وقوله : (إِيمَانُهُ لَمْ يَخْلُ مِنْ تَرْدِيدِ هذه الجملةُ خبرٌ عن المبتدأ الذي هو كل من قلد ... ) إلى آخره والمراد بإيمانه: جزمُهُ بأحكام التوحيد من غير دليل، وليس
(۲) قوله : (هذا التعليل ... إلى آخره أي باعتبار ما تضمنه من جواب الدليل؛ لأن وجوب المعرفة يتضمن وجوب أمور ثلاثة : الجزم، وكونه مطابقاً للواقع، وكونه ناشئاً عن الدليل، وهذه علة للثالث، وهو كونه ناشئاً عن الدليل. انتهى أجهوري.
(۳) قاله في شرح الجزائرية» (ص ٥٢ ) .
The ruling on the faith of the muqallid and the dispute concerning it. 11 - 'Since everyone who engages in taqlid in tawhid, his faith is not free from hesitation.' His statement: 'Since everyone who engages in taqlid... etc.' This is a justification (2) for the obligation of the prior knowledge, as if he said: Knowledge of what was mentioned is obligatory upon the responsible person only because everyone who engages in taqlid... etc. So 'idh' is for justification. And taqlid is: accepting the statement of another without knowing its proof. What is meant by 'accepting' is belief; i.e., belief in the content of the other's statement. What is meant by 'statement' includes action and approval as well. What is excluded by our saying 'without knowing its proof' are the students after the teachers have guided them to proofs, for they are knowers, not muqallids. Sheikh al-Sanusi gave an example for the difference between them and the muqallids: a group looking for the crescent moon, and one of them saw it first and informed them of it; if they believed him without actual sighting, they are muqallids; but if he guided them by a sign until they saw it themselves, they are not muqallids (3). His statement: 'in tawhid' means in the science of creed (ilm al-aqa'id), even if it pertains to the messengers; the intent is not specifically the affirmation of oneness. His statement: 'his faith is not free from hesitation' – this sentence is a predicate for the subject which is 'everyone who engages in taqlid...' etc. What is meant by 'his faith' is his certainty of the rulings of tawhid without proof. (2) His statement: 'This justification... etc.' i.e., considering what it contains of an answer to the proof; because the obligation of knowledge entails the obligation of three things: certainty, being in accordance with reality, and being derived from proof, and this is a cause for the third, which is being derived from proof. Ended al-Ujhuri. (3) He said it in his commentary on al-Jaza'iriyya (p. 52).
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the ruling on the faith of the muqallid (one who follows without proof) and the scholarly dispute about it. It begins by citing the line 'Idh kullu man qallada fi al-tawhid imanuhu lam yakhlu min tardid' (Since everyone who engages in taqlid in tawhid, his faith is not free from hesitation). The commentator explains that this line serves as a justification for the obligation of the knowledge discussed earlier: i.e., why knowledge is obligatory upon the responsible person. The letter 'idh' indicates causality. Then the commentator provides a precise definition of taqlid: 'accepting the statement of another without knowing its proof.' He clarifies that 'accepting' means believing the content of the statement, and 'statement' includes actions and tacit approvals as well. Importantly, the definition excludes students who are guided to proofs by their teachers; these are considered 'knowers' (arifun) rather than muqallids. To illustrate, the commentator cites an example from Sheikh al-Sanusi: a group looking for the crescent moon. If one person sees it and the others believe him without seeing it themselves, they are muqallids. But if he identifies a sign that leads them to see it themselves, they are no longer muqallids. Next, the commentary clarifies the phrase 'fi al-tawhid' (in tawhid): it means the science of creed, not the specific affirmation of God's oneness; it can include matters pertaining to the messengers. Finally, the commentator explains the meaning of 'imanuhu lam yakhlu min tardid' (his faith is not free from hesitation): the muqallid's faith is a mental assent to the rulings of tawhid, but because it is not based on proof, it lacks the full certainty required; it is accompanied by a form of hesitation. The footnote adds that the obligation of knowledge involves three things: certainty (jazzm), correspondence with reality (mutabaqa li al-waqi'), and being derived from proof (nashi'an an al-dalil). The justification specifically addresses the third condition (being derived from proof).

Key Terms
تقليد
المقلد
إيمان
ترديد
جزم
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Following a teacher without understanding the evidence is acceptable and still constitutes valid faith.
Correction: True taqlid is defined as accepting a statement without knowing its proof. If one is guided to understand the proof, they are no longer a muqallid. The muqallid's faith is not free from hesitation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of taqlid?
A) Accepting a statement after verifying its evidence
B) Accepting a statement without knowing its proof
C) Following a teacher in all matters
D) Being uncertain about religious matters

Key Takeaway
Taqlid in matters of creed is deficient because it lacks certainty derived from proof, leaving faith marked by hesitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about a core belief you hold about Allah. Did you come to it through your own investigation of proof, or did you accept it merely because someone told you? Are you comfortable with that?
?
If you were asked today to provide a clear proof for your belief in Tawhid from the Quran or reason, could you do it? What would you say?
?
Consider the crescent moon example. In your spiritual life, are you someone who waits for a sign and then sees for yourself, or do you take others' word without verifying? What step will you take this week to move from blind acceptance to knowing?

Defending Kalam: The Sun and the Blind
The commentator cites a poem defending kalam and explains its connection to the issue of mixing with philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a classical poem serves as a rhetorical rebuttal to the charge that kalam is corrupted by philosophy.

Core Idea
Kalam's value is unaffected by its critics' ignorance, just as the sun's light is undimmed by the blind.

In this lesson, we encounter an elegant defense of kalam against a common accusation. The commentator cites a poem that draws a powerful analogy between the sun and the discipline of kalam. As we read the source passage, notice how the poem is used to refute the impression that mixing with philosophy makes kalam defective.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and its translation
Read the Arabic poem once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the imagery.
The poem uses the basīṭ meter, typical for didactic verses.
2
Understand the analogy
Identify what the sun represents (kalam) and what the blind represent (critics). The sun's light is unaffected by the blind's inability to see it.
The analogy is central to the rebuttal.
3
Connect the poem to the issue
The commentator explains that the poem is linked to the phrase 'mixed with philosophy' (المخلوط بالفلسفة). It rebuts the impression that this mixing makes kalam defective.
The term 'īhām' (إيهام) means 'false impression'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما أحسن قول بعضهم : [من البسيط] عَابَ الكَلامَ أُناسٌ لا خَلَاقَ لَهُمْ وَما عَلَيْهِ إذا هابُوهُ مِنْ ضَرَرٍ ما ضَرَّ شَمْسَ الضُّحَى فِي الأُفْقِ طَالِعَةٌ أَلَّا يَرَى ضَوْءَهَا مَنْ لَيْسَ ذَا بَصَرِ قوله : (وما أحسن قول بعضهم ... إلى آخره مرتبط بقوله : (المخلوط بالفلسفة)؛ فإن خلط الدليل بالفلسفة يُوهم أنه صار معيباً بذلك، فدفع ذلك الإيهام بقوله : (وما أحسن ... إلى آخره . انتهى أجهوري.
And how excellent is the saying of some of them: (al-basīṭ meter) People without good share criticized kalam, and no harm comes upon it if they fear it. The midday sun rising on the horizon is not harmed by the fact that one who lacks sight does not see its light. His statement: (And how excellent is the saying of some of them... etc.) is connected to his statement: (mixed with philosophy); for mixing the proof with philosophy gives the impression that it has thereby become defective, so he rebutted that impression by his statement: (And how excellent... etc.). End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the discipline of kalam (dialectical theology) against the accusation that it is defective because it is sometimes mixed with philosophy. The commentator first quotes a poem that compares those who disparage kalam to blind people who cannot see the light of the sun. The sun is unharmed by their blindness, and likewise kalam is not harmed by the criticism of ignorant people. The commentator then explains that this poem was introduced specifically to address a potential misunderstanding: when the author of the matn mentioned that some kalam is 'mixed with philosophy,' one might think that this mixing makes kalam inherently flawed. The poem is meant to rebut that impression (dafʿ al-īhām) by showing that the worth of kalam remains intact, just as the sun remains bright even if the blind do not see it. The passage thus affirms that the presence of philosophical elements does not detract from the truth or value of kalam.

Key Terms
كلام
فلسفة
إيهام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the presence of philosophical reasoning in kalam makes it invalid or harmful.
Correction: The poem analogizes critics to the blind; their inability to see does not diminish the sun's light. Similarly, mixing with philosophy does not corrupt kalam.

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator cite the poem about the sun and the blind man?
A) To praise the blind man for acknowledging his limitation.
B) To show that kalam is like the sun and its critics are like the blind; their criticism does not harm it.
C) To argue that mixing with philosophy makes kalam dark and hidden.
D) To prove that only the blind can understand kalam.

Key Takeaway
Kalam remains intrinsically valuable and true, regardless of the ignorant criticisms that arise from its association with philosophy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The First Obligation: Knowledge of Allah's Attributes
Commentary defining the first obligation as knowledge of attributes, not essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the first obligatory act of knowledge is to know Allah's attributes and the rulings of divinity, not His essence, which is beyond human comprehension.

Core Idea
The first obligation is to know Allah's attributes, not His essence.

This passage from the commentary on al-Jawhara clarifies a crucial point in Islamic theology: the first thing a Muslim must know is not the essence of Allah, which is unknowable, but His attributes and the rulings of divinity. The commentator grounds this in prophetic tradition and the saying of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the grammatical background
The commentator first clarifies the wording of the poem, showing how 'awwalan' and 'ma'rifa' are interpreted to set up the precise meaning.
This grammatical analysis ensures we correctly understand the text.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the explicit statement that 'ma'rifa' here means knowledge of attributes and rulings, not essence. This is the central point of the lesson.
The nunation on 'ma'rifatun' indicates magnification, emphasizing its importance.
3
Reflect on the hadith and quote
The hadith forbids reflection on the Creator, and the saying of Abu Bakr teaches that inability to perceive is itself perception. These reinforce the boundary of human knowledge.
Reflect on why seeking Allah's essence is considered akin to shirk.
4
Apply the lesson
Understand that seeking to know Allah's essence is inappropriate; instead, focus on His attributes and the rulings of divinity as the first obligation.
This is a practical lesson for one's faith and practice.

الإلهيات
أحدهما : أن يكون بمعنى (سابق) فيكون منصرفاً منوناً، ومنه قولهم: الحمد الله أولاً وآخراً ( ١ ) .
والثاني :
: أن يكون صفةٌ، فيكون أفعل تفضيل بمعنى (أسبق) فيكون غير منصرف ؛ للوصفية ووزن الفعل، فإن حمل ما في النظم على الأول، فلا إشكال، وإن حمل على الثاني، فصرفُهُ وحذف المضاف إليه لضرورة النظم.
،
:
وقوله : (مِمَّا يَجِبْ) أي من الذي يجب، فـ (ما) اسم موصول، و(من) تبعيضية، وهو صفة لـ (أولاً) على الاستعمال الأول، وللمضاف إليه المحذوف على الاستعمال الثاني والأصل أن أول شيء مما يجب. وقوله : (مَعْرِفَةٌ) خبر (أن)، والتنوين فيه للتعظيم، وهو عوض عن المضاف إليه، والأصل: معرفة الله والمراد: معرفةُ صفاتِهِ وسائر أحكام الألوهية، لا معرفةُ ذاتِهِ وكنه حقيقته ؛ إذ لا يعرف ذاته وكنه حقيقتِهِ إلا هو.
وفي الحديث: «تفكَّروا في الخلق ولا تفكَّروا في الخالق؛ فإنه لا تحيط به الفكرة» (۲) ، وفي الحديث أيضاً : إن الله احتجب عن البصائر كما احتجب عن الأبصار».
وبالجملة لا يعرفُ الله إلا الله، فترك الإدراك إدراك ، والبحث عن ذات الله إشراك (٣) .
وهو المعبر عنه من قول الصديق رضي الله عنه كما حكاه الطوسي في «اللمع، والخركوشي في تهذيب الأسرار»: (العجز عن درك الإدراك إدراك)، فقد أدرك أنه عاجز عن دَرَك ذات الحق سبحانه حقيقة صفاته وهذا هو إفراد القدم عن الحدث.
The divine matters. One of them: that it is in the sense of 'preceding', so it is fully declinable with nunation, and from it is their saying: 'Praise be to Allah, first and last' (1). The second: that it is an adjective, so it is an elative (af‘al tafḍīl) in the sense of 'more preceding', so it is indeclinable due to being an adjective and having the pattern of a verb. If what is in the poem is taken on the first usage, then there is no problem; if taken on the second, then its declension and the omission of the annexed noun is for the necessity of the meter. His saying: 'mimmā yajib' – i.e. from that which is obligatory; 'mā' is a relative pronoun, 'min' is partitive, and it is an adjective for 'awwalan' on the first usage, and for the omitted annexed noun on the second usage; the original is 'the first thing from that which is obligatory'. His saying: 'ma‘rifatun' is the predicateof 'an', and the nunation here is for magnification, and it stands in place of the annexed noun; the original is 'knowledge of Allah', and what is intended is knowledge of His attributes and all the rulings of divinity, not knowledge of His essence and the reality of His nature, for none knows His essence and the reality of His nature except Him. And in the ḥadīth: 'Reflect upon the creation and do not reflect upon the Creator, for thought cannot encompass Him' (2), and also in the ḥadīth: 'Indeed Allah has veiled Himself from inner sight just as He has veiled Himself from eyesight.' In sum, none knows Allah except Allah, so the abandonment of comprehension is comprehension, and seeking the essence of Allah is associationism (3). This is expressed in the saying of al-Ṣiddīq (may Allah be pleased with him), as related by al-Ṭūsī in al-Luma‘ and al-Kharkūshī in Tahdhīb al-Asrār: 'Incapacity to attain perception is perception', for he realized that he is incapable of attaining the essence of the Truth (exalted is He) and the reality of His attributes, and this is distinguishing the Eternal from the contingent.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of the poem al-Jawhara that begins 'And be certain that the first obligatory thing...' The commentator clarifies the wording used by the poet. First, he explains the word 'awwalan' (first): it can be interpreted in two ways, either as a noun meaning 'preceding' or as an adjective meaning 'more preceding'. The first is fully declinable, the second is not; the poet chose one to fit the meter. Then he explains the phrase 'mimmā yajib' (from that which is obligatory), analyzing it grammatically as a relative clause modifying 'awwalan' or an implied noun. The key term is 'ma‘rifatun' (knowledge), which is the predicate and carries nunation to emphasize greatness; it stands for 'ma‘rifat Allāh' (knowledge of Allah). The commentator stresses that this knowledge does not mean knowing Allah's essence (dhāt) or the inner reality of His nature (kunnu ḥaqīqatihi), for that is impossible for creation. Only Allah knows His own essence. He supports this with two ḥadīths: one forbidding reflection on the Creator because thought cannot encompass Him, and another stating that Allah is veiled from inner sight. He concludes that 'not grasping is a kind of grasping' – i.e., realizing one's incapacity is itself a form of knowledge – and that seeking to know Allah's essence is akin to shirk (associating partners with Him). He then quotes a saying of Abu Bakr al-Ṣiddīq (may Allah be pleased with him) to reinforce this: 'Incapacity to attain perception is perception', because it means one has grasped that one cannot grasp the essence. Thus the first obligation is not to know Allah's essence but to know His attributes and the rulings of divinity.

Key Terms
أول ما يجب
معرفة
صفات
الإدراك
إشراك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first obligation is to know Allah's essence and the reality of His nature.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the intended knowledge is of Allah's attributes and rulings, not His essence, which is unknowable except to Him.

Checkpoint
What is the intended meaning of 'ma‘rifa' (knowledge) in the context of the first obligation?
A) Knowledge of Allah's essence and the reality of His nature
B) Knowledge of Allah's attributes and all the rulings of divinity
C) Knowledge of the secret names of Allah
D) Knowledge of the creation of the universe

Key Takeaway
The first obligation is to know Allah's attributes, not His essence; true knowledge of Allah is to realize one's incapacity to grasp His reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Twelve Opinions on the First Obligatory Duty
Enumerates and discusses twelve opinions on the first obligatory duty, concluding with the correct view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twelve scholarly opinions on the first obligatory duty and how the commentator reconciles them into a single coherent view.

Core Idea
Knowledge is the ultimate duty, but it requires consideration and intention as means.

The question of what a person must do first in religion has sparked much debate. This passage lists twelve distinct answers given by scholars, from knowledge to doubt. After presenting each, the commentator resolves the disagreement by showing that the opinions refer to different aspects: the ultimate purpose, the close means, and the distant means.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration of opinions
Read through the twelve opinions listed in the Arabic passage and translation. Notice how each is attributed to a scholar or group.
Pay attention to the structure: each opinion is introduced by a number.
2
Note the refutations provided
Some opinions are briefly refuted (e.g., doubt is rejected because its removal is sought). Identify which opinions are criticized and why.
3
Identify the correct view
The commentator concludes with 'al-aṣaḥḥ' (the most correct). Find this part and understand the threefold distinction: maqṣadan, wasīlah qarībah, wasīlah baʿīdah.
This reconciliation shows the opinions are not contradictory.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider how this classification helps in understanding the priority of religious duties. Why is knowledge considered the ultimate purpose?
This connects to the broader theme of the source: the foundation of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجملة الأقوال في أول الواجبات اثنا عشر قولاً :
أولها : ما قاله الأشعري إمامُ هذا الفن : أنه المعرفةُ .
وثانيها : ما قاله الأستاذ أبو إسحاق الإسفرايني : أنه النظر الموصل للمعرفة، ويُعزى للأشعري أيضاً .
وثالثها : ما قاله القاضي الباقلاني : أنه أوّلُ النظر ؛ أي: المقدمة الأولى منه؛ نحو قولك : العالم حادث، وكل حادث لا بدَّ له من محدث؛ فمجموع المقدمتين هو النظر، والمقدمة الأولى هي أوَّلُ النظر .
ورابعها ما قاله إمام الحرمين : أنه القصد إلى النظر؛ أي : تفريغ القلب عن الشواغل، وعُزي للقاضي أيضاً.
وخامسها : ما قاله بعضُهم : أنه التقليد.
وسادسها : أنه النطق بالشهادتين .
وسابعها : ما قاله أبو هاشم في طائفة من المعتزلة وغيرهم : أنه الشك، ورُدَّ بأنه مطلوب زوالُه ؛ لأنَّ الشكّ في شيء من العقائد كفر ، فلا يكون مطلوباً حصوله؛ ولعلهم أرادوا ترديد الفكر، فيؤول إلى النظر .
وثامنها : أنه الإيمان.
وتاسعها : أنه الإسلام.
وهذان القولان متقاربان مردودانِ باحتياج كل من الإيمان والإسلام للمعرفة.
وعاشرها : اعتقاد وجوب النظر.
وحادي عشرها : أنه وظيفه الوقت؛ كصلاة ضاق وقتها، فتُقدّم.
وثاني عشرها : أنه المعرفةُ أو التقليد؛ أي: أحدُهُما لا بعينه فيكون مخيَّراً.
والأصح : أن أوَّلَ واجب مقصداً المعرفة، وأوَّلَ واجب وسيلة قريبة النظر، ووسيلة بعيدة القصد إلى النظر، وبهذا يُجمعُ بين هذه الأقوال الثلاثة .
The total of opinions on the first obligatory duty is twelve opinions: First: what al-Ashʿarī, the imam of this art, said: that it is knowledge (maʿrifah). Second: what the master Abū Isḥāq al-Isfarāyīnī said: that it is the consideration (naẓar) that leads to knowledge, and it is also attributed to al-Ashʿarī. Third: what al-Qāḍī al-Bāqillānī said: that it is the beginning of consideration; meaning: the first premise of it; like your saying: the world is originated, and every originated thing necessarily has an originator; so the total of the two premises is consideration, and the first premise is the beginning of consideration. Fourth: what Imām al-Ḥaramayn said: that it is the intention to consider; meaning: emptying the heart of distractions, and it is also attributed to al-Qāḍī. Fifth: what some said: that it is blind following (taqlīd). Sixth: that it is uttering the two testimonies. Seventh: what Abū Hāshim and a group of the Muʿtazila and others said: that it is doubt (shakk), and it was refuted by saying that its removal is sought; because doubt in any matter of creed is disbelief (kufr), so its acquisition is not sought; perhaps they meant the repetition of thought, which then reduces to consideration. Eighth: that it is faith (īmān). Ninth: that it is submission (islām). These two opinions are close and are rejected because both faith and submission require knowledge. Tenth: belief in the obligation of consideration. Eleventh: that it is the duty of the moment (waẓīfat al-waqt), like a prayer whose time is short, so it is given precedence. Twelfth: that it is knowledge or blind following; meaning: one of them, not specifically, so one is given a choice. And the most correct (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that the first obligatory duty in terms of intended purpose (maqṣadan) is knowledge, the first obligatory duty in terms of close means (wasīlah qarībah) is consideration, and the distant means (wasīlah baʿīdah) is the intention to consider, and by this the three opinions are reconciled.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a classification and discussion of the twelve different opinions among scholars concerning the first obligatory duty (awwal al-wājibāt) upon a responsible person (mukallaf). The commentator enumerates each opinion with its proponents and often adds a brief evaluation. The passage begins by listing the opinions in order.

1. The first opinion is that of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, that the first duty is knowledge (ma'rifah) of Allah.
2. The second is that of Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini, that the first duty is the consideration (nazar) that leads to knowledge. This is also attributed to al-Ash'ari.
3. The third, from al-Baqillani, says the first duty is the beginning of consideration, i.e., the first premise of a syllogism, like 'the world is originated'.
4. The fourth, from Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), says it is the intention to consider, meaning clearing the heart of distractions.
5. The fifth, from some unnamed scholars, is blind following (taqlid).
6. The sixth is uttering the two testimonies of faith.
7. The seventh, from Abu Hashim and some Mu'tazila, is doubt (shakk). The commentator refutes this by noting that doubt is undesirable and its removal is sought; they probably meant intellectual pondering.
8. The eighth is faith (iman).
9. The ninth is submission (islam). These two are rejected because both require prior knowledge.
10. The tenth is belief in the obligation of consideration.
11. The eleventh is that the duty is determined by the immediate circumstance (waẓīfat al-waqt).
12. The twelfth is knowledge OR blind following, giving a choice.

After listing all twelve, the commentator presents the most correct (al-aṣaḥḥ) view, which reconciles the three leading positions (knowledge, consideration, and intention): the first duty in terms of intended purpose (maqṣadan) is knowledge; the first duty as a proximate means (wasīlah qarībah) is consideration; and the first duty as a distant means (wasīlah baʿīdah) is the intention to consider. This synthetic resolution shows that the apparent disagreement is largely verbal.

التقليد
الشك
الإيمان
الإسلام
مقصداً
وسيلة قريبة
وسيلة بعيدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the twelve opinions are contradictory and that only one is correct.
Correction: The passage shows they can be reconciled by distinguishing between ultimate purpose (knowledge), close means (consideration), and distant means (intention to consider).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which scholar held that the first obligatory duty is the intention to consider (al-qaṣd ilā al-naẓar)?
A) Imam al-Ash'ari
B) Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini
C) Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni)
D) al-Qadi al-Baqillani

Key Takeaway
The first obligatory duty is knowledge of Allah, but achieving it requires intention and consideration as means, which explains the diversity of scholarly opinions.

Linguistic and Technical Definition of al-Nazar (Consideration)
Commentator defines النَّظَر linguistically and technically with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and technical definitions of al-nazar (consideration), and how it functions as the method of rational inference in kalam.

Core Idea
Al-nazar is the rational engine that moves from known premises to unknown conclusions.

Before we can understand how kalam arguments work, we need to grasp the tool they use: al-nazar (consideration). The commentator begins by distinguishing its everyday meaning from its precise technical meaning in theology. This definition sets the stage for all subsequent reasoning in the discipline.

Guided Path
1
Read the linguistic definition
Notice that nazar can mean both sight and thought. The commentator clarifies that the intended meaning here is thought, not physical vision.
2
Study the technical definition
The technical definition is: ordering two known things to arrive at knowledge of an unknown. This is the core of logical inference in kalam.
3
Examine the two examples
The first example shows nazar in judgments (tasdiqat) using a syllogism. The second shows it in concepts (tasawwurat) using a definition.
These illustrate two applications of the same method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والنظرُ - لغةً - : الإبصارُ ؛ أي: إدراك الشيء بحاسَةِ البصر، والفكر؛ أي حركة النفس في المعقولات) ، وأما في المحسوسات فتخيل ؛ وعلم من ذلك أن النظر مشترك بين الإبصار والفكر، والمراد منه هنا الثاني، وهو الفكر، فكأن المصنف قال : فتفكروا ... إلى آخره . وأما عُرْفاً ، فهو ترتيب أمرين معلومين ليتوصل بترتيبهما إلى علم أمر مجهول؛ کترتیب الصغرى مع الكبرى في قولنا : العالم متغيّر، وكلُّ متغير حادث فإنه موصل للعلم بـ (حدوث العالم المجهول قبل ذلك الترتيب، وكترتيب الجنس مع الفضل في قولنا : (الإنسانُ : حيوان ناطق). فالأوَّلُ مثال للنظر في التصديقات، والثاني : مثال للنظر في التصورات.
And al-nazar – linguistically – is sight (al-ibṣār), i.e., perception of a thing by the sense of sight; and thought (al-fikr), i.e., the movement of the soul in intelligibles. As for sensibles, it is imagination. It is understood from this that nazar is shared between sight and thought, but what is meant here is the second, i.e., thought, as if the author said: 'So reflect...' to the end. As for technically, it is the ordering of two known things to arrive through their ordering at knowledge of an unknown thing; like ordering the minor premise with the major premise in our saying: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is originated' – for it leads to knowledge of the origination of the world, which was unknown before that ordering; and like ordering the genus with the differentia in our saying: 'Man is a rational animal.' The first is an example of nazar in affirmations (taṣdīqāt), and the second is an example of nazar in concepts (taṣawwurāt).
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage defines the key term 'nazar' which is central to kalam methodology. First, the linguistic definition: nazar can mean physical sight (baṣar) or intellectual thought (fikr). It also can mean imagination for sensibles. The term is homonymous (mushtarak) between these meanings, but in this context it means thought or reflection. Then the technical definition: nazar is the arrangement of two known pieces of information to deduce something unknown. Two examples are given: (1) for judgments (tasdiqat): the syllogism 'the world is changing, everything changing is originated, therefore the world is originated'; (2) for concepts (tasawwurat): the definition 'man is a rational animal'. These illustrate how nazar operates in both types of knowledge acquisition.

Key Terms
النظر
الإبصار
الفكر
التصديقات
التصورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-nazar is just looking with the eyes or using the senses.
Correction: While nazar can mean sight linguistically, in kalam it specifically means rational thought and inference, not sensory perception.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-nazar (consideration) is the method of logical inference in kalam: arranging known premises to deduce unknown conclusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek certainty about a matter in your faith, do you use the structured method of nazar—ordering what you already know to reach conclusions—or do you rely on feelings or secondhand opinions? What would change if you applied nazar more intentionally?

The Validity of Reasoning Does Not Depend on Order
Sanusi explains that nazar's validity is independent of order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the validity of rational speculation (nazar) does not require a specific sequential order.

Core Idea
Validity of reasoning does not depend on a fixed order.

Sanusi begins by addressing a common assumption: that rational inquiry must follow a prescribed order of contemplating creation. He asserts that any valid sequence is acceptable, freeing the thinker from a rigid method. This principle has important implications for how we approach theological reflection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on order
Read the Arabic sentence about the validity of nazar not depending on order and the examples given.
Notice the variety of sequences mentioned: starting with the self, lower world, upper world, etc.
2
Identify the different orders
List the sequences mentioned in the passage: self→lower world→upper world, or upper world→lower world→self, and others.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this flexibility is important in theological reasoning. It means the method is adaptable, emphasizing sincerity and correctness of reasoning over a rigid order.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا تتوقف صحةُ النظر على هذا الترتيب بل يصح أن ينظر إلى النفس، ثم إلى العالم السفلي، ثم العلوي، أو ينظر إلى العالم العلوي، ثم إلى السفلي، ثم إلى النفس إلى غير ذلك من الصور الممكنة. والنظرُ - لغةٌ - : الإبصارُ ؛ أي: إدراك الشيء بحاسَةِ البصر، والفكر؛ أي حركة النفس في المعقولات) ، وأما في المحسوسات فتخيل ؛ وعلم من ذلك أن النظر مشترك بين الإبصار والفكر، والمراد منه هنا الثاني، وهو الفكر، فكأن المصنف قال : فتفكروا ... إلى آخره .
The validity of reasoning does not depend on this order, but it is valid to look at oneself, then at the lower world, then the upper, or to look at the upper world, then the lower, then oneself, and other such possible forms. And 'nazar' linguistically: sight; i.e., perceiving something by the sense of vision, and thought; i.e., the movement of the soul in intelligibles; as for sensibles, it is imagination. From this it is known that 'nazar' is shared between sight and thought. The intended meaning here is the second, which is thought. So it is as if the author said: 'so think...' etc.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining that the validity of rational speculation (nazar) does not depend on a fixed order of consideration; one may start with oneself, then the lower world, then the upper, or any other sequence. Then the author defines nazar linguistically: it means sight (ibṣār), i.e., sensory perception, and also thought (fikr), i.e., the mind's movement among intelligibles. For sensibles, it is imagination. From this, nazar is shared between sight and thought, but here the intended meaning is thought, as indicated by the author's phrase 'so think...'.

The Linguistic Meaning of Nazar: Sight and Thought
Sanusi gives the linguistic definition of nazar and clarifies its intended theological meaning.

Nazar linguistically means both sight and thought.

The Technical Definition of Nazar and Its Two Examples
Sanusi defines nazar technically, illustrates with examples, and defends against an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the technical meaning of nazar, how it applies to both judgments (tasdiqat) and concepts (tasawwurat), and why definitions using a single term still involve ordering.

Core Idea
Nazar is the ordering of two knowns to reach an unknown.

Sanusi now defines nazar in its technical sense, moving beyond its linguistic meaning. He explains that it involves arranging two known items to gain knowledge of something previously unknown, then illustrates with two classic examples: one from syllogistic reasoning and one from definition. He also addresses a potential objection about definitions that seem to use only one term.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the translation. Notice that Sanusi first defines nazar as an ordering of two known things to reach knowledge of an unknown.
The definition is concise; memorise it if possible.
2
Examine the two examples
The first example uses a syllogism ('The world is changing, every changing thing is originated') – this is nazar for judgments (tasdiqat). The second example uses a definition ('Man is a rational animal') – this is nazar for concepts (tasawwurat). Identify which is which.
The second example might seem to have only one predicate, but it actually involves ordering genus and difference.
3
Understand the objection and its rebuttal
The author preempts an objection: what about definitions using only the differentia (e.g., 'Man is rational') or a property (e.g., 'Man is laughing')? He argues they still imply an ordering because 'rational' means 'something with rationality' – a compound.
This shows that nazar is subtle and even single-term definitions involve implicit arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما عُرْفاً ، فهو ترتيب أمرين معلومين ليتوصل بترتيبهما إلى علم أمر مجهول؛ کترتیب الصغرى مع الكبرى في قولنا : العالم متغيّر، وكلُّ متغير حادث فإنه موصل للعلم بـ (حدوث العالم المجهول قبل ذلك الترتيب، وكترتيب الجنس مع الفضل في قولنا : (الإنسانُ : حيوان ناطق). فالأوَّلُ مثال للنظر في التصديقات، والثاني : مثال للنظر في التصورات (۲)، ولا يرد على ذلك التعريف بالفصل وحده أو بالخاصة وحدها ؛ كأن يقال : الإنسان: ناطق، أو ضاحاً؛ لأن فيه ترتيباً حكماً ؛ لأنَّ ( ناطق) في قوة (شيءٌ ذو نطق)، و(ضاحك) في قوة شيءٌ ذو ضحك.
As for the technical sense, it is the ordering of two known things so that through their ordering knowledge of an unknown matter is reached; such as ordering the minor premise with the major premise in our statement: 'The world is changing, and every changing thing is originated' – for it leads to knowledge of the origination of the world which was unknown before that ordering – and such as ordering the genus with the difference in our statement: 'Man is a rational animal'. The first is an example of nazar in judgments (tasdiqat), and the second is an example of nazar in concepts (tasawwurat). And the definition is not refuted by [using] the differentia alone or the property alone, such as saying: 'Man is rational' or 'Man is laughing'; because there is a judgmental ordering in them, for 'rational' is equivalent to 'something possessing rationality', and 'laughing' is equivalent to 'something possessing laughter'.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage provides the technical definition of nazar: it is the arrangement of two known items to attain knowledge of an unknown. Two examples follow: the first is from syllogistic reasoning (the world is changing, every changing thing is originated, therefore the world is originated) – this is an example of nazar in tasdiqat (judgments). The second is from definition (man is a rational animal) – this is an example of nazar in tasawwurat (concepts). Then the author addresses a potential objection: one might think that defining something by its differentia alone (e.g., 'man is rational') or by its property alone (e.g., 'man is laughing') would not fit the definition because it involves only one term. But the author explains that such expressions implicitly contain an ordering: 'rational' in force means 'something that has rationality', which is a compound, and similarly 'laughing' means 'something that has laughter'. Thus they do involve a tacit ordering and are valid instances of nazar.

Correction: The author shows that such expressions are equivalent to compound phrases (e.g., 'something that has rationality') and therefore do involve ordering.

Checkpoint

B) Both from tasawwurat
C) First from tasdiqat, second from tasawwurat
D) First from tasawwurat, second from tasdiqat

Key Takeaway
Nazar in kalam is the deliberate ordering of two known premises to reach a new conclusion, whether in judgments or concepts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you affirm that 'the world is created,' have you ever personally traced the reasoning from changing phenomena to a Creator, or do you take it for granted? What stops you from doing that inner work?
?
Your understanding of who Allah is—is it built on a deliberate ordering of known truths from revelation and reason, or is it a collection of unexamined slogans? Will you commit today to constructing at least one such chain of reasoning?
?
The definition 'Man is a rational animal' seems simple, but it implies an ordered composition. In your life, where do you lazily accept simple labels about yourself or your faith without unpacking the underlying structure that gives them meaning?

Definition of Iman as Tasdiq and the Dispute Over Including Speech
Commentator explains iman as tasdiq and the dispute over nutq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core definition of iman as inner belief (tasdiq) according to the majority of Ash'aris and Maturidis, and the nature of the disagreement about the role of verbal confession (nutq).

Core Idea
Iman is inner belief; the dispute is about whether speech is part of the definition.

This lesson introduces the foundational definition of iman (faith) in Islamic theology. The poet states that iman is defined as tasdiq (inner belief), and the controversy is about whether nutq (verbal confession) is included. The commentator explains the context and the reasoning behind placing this discussion early in kalam works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the commentator uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the definition of iman
The passage begins by stating that iman is defined as tasdiq (inner belief). Find this and note that the dispute is about the inclusion of nutq (speech).
3
Understand the dispute context
The commentator explains that theologians disagreed on where to place this topic in kalam works. Why did some delay it and others bring it early? Consider the pedagogical reason given.
4
Examine the two types of iman mentioned
The passage begins to enumerate five types of iman, but only two are given here: iman by taqlid and iman by knowledge. Observe how they are defined.
The remaining three types are not present due to page cut-off.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَفُسِّرَ الإِيمَانُ بِالتَّصْدِيقِ وَالنُّطْقُ فِيهِ الخُلْفُ بِالتَّحْقِيقِ. قوله: (وَفُسِّرَ الإِيمَانُ ... إلخ) لما كان الإيمان والإسلام باعتبار متعلقِ مفهومهما - وهو ما علم من الدين بالضرورة - من مباحث علم الكلام كما يعلم من قوله فيما يأتي: (ومن المعلوم ضرورة جحد)، ذكرهما المتكلمون في علم الكلام، لكن اختلفوا في وضعهما، فَأَخَرَهما قوم عن الإلهيات والنبوات والسمعيات، وقدَّمهما آخرون؛ لاحتياج الخائض في تلك المباحث إليهما، وقد سلك المصنف هذا الطريق، فلذلك قال: (وفسر الإيمان ... إلى آخره) ببناء الفعل للمفعول للعلم بفاعله والأصل: وفسَّرَ جمهور الأشاعرة والماتريدية وكذا غيرهم من المعتزلة كالصالحي وابن الراوندي. واعلم أن الإيمان على خمسة أقسام: إيمان عن تقليد: وهو الإيمان الناشئ عن الأخذ بقول الشيخ من غير دليل. وإيمان عن علم: وهو الإيمان الناشئ عن معرفة العقائد بأدلتها.
And iman is defined by [inner] belief, and speech concerning it is where the dispute truly lies. His statement: 'And iman is defined... etc.' Since iman and Islam, by consideration of the object of their concept—which is what is known of religion by necessity—are among the topics of the science of kalam, as is known from his statement later: 'And it is a matter of known by necessity that one denies,' the theologians mentioned them in the science of kalam. But they disagreed about their placement. Some groups placed them after the discussions of divine matters, prophethood, and sam'iyyat, while others placed them earlier due to the need of one who engages in those investigations for them. And the author followed this path, so he said: 'And iman is defined... etc.' with the verb built for the passive because the agent is known, and the original is: The majority of Ash'aris, Maturidis, and likewise others from the Mu'tazila like al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi defined it. And know that iman has five divisions: iman by taqlid: which is iman arising from taking the statement of a teacher without evidence; and iman by knowledge: which is iman arising from knowledge of beliefs with their evidences.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage discusses the definition of iman. The verse line states that iman is defined as tasdiq (inner belief), and the disagreement (khulf) is about the inclusion of nutq (verbal confession). The commentator explains that iman and Islam are topics of kalam because their object (what is necessarily known from religion) is a subject of kalam. Theologians differed on where to place these topics: some postponed them until after divine attributes, prophethood, and sam'iyyat, while others placed them earlier because the student needs them before engaging in those deeper topics. The author (al-Laqani) followed the latter approach. Then the commentator notes that the passive construction ('fussira') is used because the agent is well-known, namely the majority of Ash'aris, Maturidis, and some Mu'tazila like al-Salihi and Ibn al-Rawandi. The passage then begins to enumerate the five types of iman, but only two are given: iman by taqlid (blind acceptance) and iman by knowledge (based on evidence). The remaining three are not present due to the page cut-off.
