Definition of Iman: Linguistic and Legal
Defines iman linguistically and legally, compares general vs. detailed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and legal definitions of iman, and how general and detailed belief fulfill the obligation of faith.

Core Idea
Iman is affirmation of the heart, not just words or actions.

This lesson unpacks the classical definition of iman as presented in the matn. The author distinguishes between the linguistic meaning—absolute affirmation—and the legal meaning—affirmation of everything the Prophet brought from what is known by necessity. He also clarifies that belief can be general or detailed, and both are sufficient for exiting the obligation, though detailed is more complete.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow from comparing detailed and general belief to stating the legal and linguistic definitions.
The passage uses a concise style common in kalam texts.
2
Identify the two definitions
Find where the author defines iman linguistically (lughatan) and legally (shar'an). Notice how the linguistic definition is broader ('absolute affirmation') and the legal definition is specific ('affirmation of everything the Prophet brought...').
The legal definition includes the phrase 'what is known of the religion by necessity.'
3
Understand the comparison
The author says detailed belief is more complete in detail than general belief, but both fulfill the obligation. Ask yourself: Why does this distinction matter? It shows that a general belief in Islam is valid, but detailed knowledge brings more completeness.
This connects to the learning objective about how iman is actualized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والتفصيلي أكمل من الإجمالي من حيث التفصيلُ، وإلا فهو مثله من حيث الخروج من عُهدة التكليف بكل منهما . وبالجملة: فالإيمان شرعاً : هو التصديق بجميع ما جاء به النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ممَّا عُلم من الدين بالضرورة إجمالاً في الإجمالي، وتفصيلاً في التفصيلي، وأما لغة: فهو مطلق التصديق، ومنه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَمَا أَنتَ بِمُؤْمِنِ لَنَا) [يوسف: ١٧) أي : بمصدق .
And the detailed [belief] is more complete than the general in terms of detail, but otherwise it is like it in terms of exiting the responsibility of obligation through each of them. In summary: iman in the Shari'ah is affirmation (tasdiq) of everything that the Prophet (peace and blessings be upon him) brought from what is known of the religion by necessity, in general for the general [belief] and in detail for the detailed [belief]. As for linguistically, it is absolute affirmation. From it is the saying of the Exalted: 'And you are not believers in us' (Yusuf 12:17), i.e., affirmers.
Source pages p. 125

Commentary
This passage defines iman (faith) and distinguishes between its legal and linguistic meanings. Legally (shar'an), iman is affirmation (tasdiq) of all that the Prophet (peace be upon him) brought from what is known of the religion by necessity. This affirmation can be general (ijmali) or detailed (tafsili). The detailed is more complete in terms of detail, but both fulfill the obligation. Linguistically (lughatan), iman means absolute affirmation, as in the Qur'anic verse where Joseph's brothers say 'You are not believers in us' meaning 'affirmers'. The passage emphasizes that the essential component is affirmation in the heart, and the detail is a matter of completeness.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you using 'lack of knowledge' as an excuse to avoid heartfelt submission? What is one belief you already know to be true that you can actively affirm in your heart today?
?
Consider something you claim to believe from the religion by necessity (e.g., that Allah sees you). Does your daily behavior truly reflect that conviction? If not, is it possible that your 'iman' is still just knowledge or verbal profession, not yet the tasdiq that transforms actions? What needs to change for your actions to align with your affirmation?

Definition of Prayer and Its Obligation Timing
Defines prayer linguistically and legally, explains derivations, and states obligation timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic and legal definitions of prayer, the two possible derivations of the word salat, and the historical timing of when prayer was obligated.

Core Idea
Prayer connects the servant to Allah and straightens the soul.

This passage from the commentary on the Jawharat al-Tawhid defines the term 'salat' both linguistically and legally. It also explores its possible etymological roots and cites a key verse on the impact of prayer. Finally, it clarifies when prayer became obligatory in Islamic history.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Try to get a feel for the flow without analyzing every detail.
Notice how the author moves from definition to etymology to historical fact.
2
Identify the two definitions and two derivations
The passage gives a linguistic definition (supplication) and a legal definition (words and actions with takbir and taslim). It then suggests two derivations: from wasl (connection) or from a root meaning 'to straighten'. Note how each derivation is explained.
The verse from Surah al-Ankabut supports the second derivation.
3
Locate the timing of obligation
The author states that prayer was obligated one year before the Hijra, with the predominant view being that no prayer was required before that. Some say two rak'ahs morning and evening were obligatory earlier, but the five daily prayers came during the Night Journey (Isra').
This shows the gradual nature of religious obligations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَالصَّلَاةُ) هي لغة : الدعاء مطلقاً ، وقيل : بخير وشرعاً : أقوال وأفعال مفتتحة بالتكبير مختتمة بالتسليم بشرائط مخصوصة وهي إما مأخوذة من الوصل؛ لأنَّها وُصْلَةٌ بين العبد وربه، وإما مأخوذة من صليت العود بالنار) إذا قوَّمتُهُ بها ؛ لأنها تقيم العبد على طاعة الله تعالى وتنهاه عن خلافه، قال الله تعالى : وإن الصلوة تَنْهَى عَنِ الْفَحْشَاءِ وَالْمُنكَرِ) [العنكبوت: ٤٥] . واعلم أن الصلاةَ فَرُضَتْ قبل الهجرة بسنة والأرجحُ : أنه لم يفرض عليه قبلَها صلاةٌ ، وقيل : كان الواجب قبلها ركعتين بالغداة وركعتين بالعشي، ثم فُرضت الصلوات الخمس ليلة الإسراء.
His saying: 'And prayer' – it is linguistically: supplication absolutely, and it is said: for good; and legally: words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim with specific conditions. And it is either derived from connection (al-wasl) because it is a connection between the servant and his Lord, or derived from 'I straightened the wood with fire' (salaytu al-‘uda bi al-nar) when I straighten it with it, because it makes the servant obedient to Allah and prevents him from opposition. Allah said: 'Indeed, prayer prohibits immorality and wrongdoing' (29:45). And know that prayer was obligated one year before the Hijra, and the most preponderant is that no prayer was obligated upon him before it. And it was said: before it, two rak‘ahs in the morning and two rak‘ahs in the evening were obligatory, then the five daily prayers were obligated on the night of Isra’.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines prayer (salat) and discusses when it became obligatory. It begins with the linguistic definition: prayer is 'supplication' (du‘a’) in an absolute sense, or more specifically 'for good'. The legal definition is: specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim, subject to certain conditions. The author then explains two possible derivations of the word 'salat': from 'wasl' (connection) because it connects the servant to his Lord, or from 'salaytu al-‘uda' (I straightened wood with fire) because it straightens the servant in obedience. The verse from Surah al-‘Ankabut is cited as evidence that prayer prevents evil. Finally, the passage explains the timing of obligation: it was made obligatory one year before the Hijra, and the predominant view is that no prayer was required before that (though some say two rak‘ahs morning and evening were obligatory earlier), and the five daily prayers were instituted during the Night Journey (Isra’).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Linguistic Meaning of Fasting
Defines fasting linguistically and cites a Quranic example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic meaning of fasting (siyam) as abstention, and its Quranic application to speech.

Core Idea
Fasting linguistically means abstention, even from speech.

The author introduces fasting as another example of righteous action, similar to Hajj and prayer. He first gives its linguistic meaning—abstention—and supports it with the Quranic story of Maryam, where 'fasting' refers to silence. This lesson unpacks that definition and its significance.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the context
This passage is part of a series defining acts of worship. Fasting is given as an example, like Hajj and prayer before it.
The author uses 'likewise' to connect to previous definitions.
2
Learn the linguistic definition
The word 'siyam' (الصيام) is defined linguistically as 'imsak' (الإمساك), meaning abstention. This abstention can be from anything, even speech.
Note the broad scope: abstention is not limited to food and drink.
3
Examine the Quranic example
The author cites the Quranic verse about Maryam (19:26), where she says she has vowed a 'sawm' (fast) to the Most Merciful, meaning silence. This shows that fasting can apply to abstaining from speech.
The verse is: 'فَكُلِي وَاشْرَبِي وَقَرِّي عَيْنًا ۖ فَإِمَّا تَرَيِنَّ مِنَ الْبَشَرِ أَحَدًا فَقُولِي إِنِّي نَذَرْتُ لِلرَّحْمَٰنِ صَوْمًا فَلَنْ أُكَلِّمَ الْيَوْمَ إِنْسِيًّا'
4
Reflect on the broader meaning
Understanding that fasting linguistically means abstention prepares you to understand its legal definition, which is a specific form of abstention with intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَذَا الصِّيَامُ) أي : مثلُ ما ذُكر من الحج والصلاة في كونه مثالاً للعمل : الصيام، وهو لغةً: الإمساك ولو عن نحو الكلام، ومنه قوله تعالى حكاية عن مريم
His saying: 'Likewise fasting' – meaning: like what was mentioned of Hajj and prayer in being an example of action: fasting (al-siyam). It is linguistically: abstention, even from something like speech. And from it is His statement, exalted, narrating from Maryam:
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This short passage defines fasting (siyam) in a manner similar to the previous definitions of Hajj and prayer. The author explains that the word 'siyam' appears in the original poem as an example of righteous action. Linguistically, it means 'abstention' (imsak), which can even include abstaining from speech, as illustrated by the Quranic verse about Maryam (19:26) where she says 'innī nadhartu li-l-rahmāni sawman' – 'I have vowed to the Most Merciful a fast,' meaning silence. The passage thus establishes the basic linguistic meaning before moving to the legal definition, though the text is cut off.

Key Terms
الصيام
الإمساك
الصوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fasting only refers to abstaining from food and drink.
Correction: Linguistically, fasting (siyam) means any form of abstention, including from speech, as shown in the Quranic story of Maryam.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the linguistic (lughatan) meaning of 'siyam'?
A) Eating and drinking
B) Abstention (imsak), even from speech
C) Worship
D) Silence only

Key Takeaway
Fasting (siyam) linguistically means abstention, and can even apply to speech, as shown in the Quranic verse about Maryam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fast during Ramadan, do you consider abstaining from idle and sinful speech as part of your ‘siyam’? If not, what would it take for you to treat your tongue with the same discipline as your food and drink?
?
The verse about Maryam shows that she vowed a fast of silence. In a world filled with constant noise, is there a place for such a fast in your life? What specific words or conversations would you need to sacrifice to practice ‘siyam’ of the tongue even for a day?

Defining Qiyam bi al-Nafs: Linguistic Analysis
Al-Sharqawi defines qiyam bi al-nafs and analyzes its grammar and prepositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the attribute of self-subsistence (qiyām bi al-nafs) and the various interpretations of its preposition and the term 'nafs' as applied to Allah.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence means Allah's existence is not dependent on anything else, not even an instrument.

This passage from al-Sharqawi's commentary on al-Jawhara delves into the linguistic nuances of the divine attribute qiyām bi al-nafs (self-subsistence). The author not only defines the attribute but also analyzes the grammar and prepositions used, addressing potential misunderstandings about how we speak of Allah's essence. Understanding these linguistic choices helps prevent theological errors.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the discussion. Note the structure: first, the grammatical parsing; second, the interpretation of 'ba'; third, the meaning of 'nafs'.
2
Identify the three interpretations of 'ba'
The passage presents three possible meanings of the preposition 'ba' in 'bi al-nafs': instrumental, causal, and metaphorical location. Identify each and note the author's preferred interpretation.
The author argues that instrumental is disrespectful, transitive doesn't fit, and causal is best.
3
Analyze the meaning of 'nafs'
The passage explains that 'nafs' means essence (dhāt) here, and lists other possible meanings. Note the author's assertion that it can be applied to Allah without rhetorical accommodation.
The Quranic evidence from Surah al-An'am is cited.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why this linguistic discussion matters. How does the correct interpretation of 'ba' and 'nafs' safeguard tawhid and avoid reducing Allah to a created thing?
This connects to the broader kalam project of affirming Allah's attributes without anthropomorphism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٤. صفة قيامه تعالى بالنفس ودليلها
٢٥ - قِيَامُهُ بِالنَّفْسِ وَحدَانية مُنَزَّهاً أَوْصَافُهُ سَنِيَّة قوله : (قِيَامُهُ بِالنَّفْسِ) معطوف على الوجود بحذف حرف العطف، والتقدير : وواجب قيامه بنفسه، فـ (أل) في النفس عوض عن المضاف إليه، وقول الشارح: (والصفة الرابعة من الصفات السلبية الواجبة له تعالى قيامه بالنفس) حل معنى لا حل إعراب كما تقدّم وقد جعل بعضهم الباء في قوله (بالنفس) باءَ الآلة، وأصله للشكتاني، وفيه إساءة أدب ؛ وقد تخلّص الشيخ يحيى الشاوي من إساءة الأدب بأن فائدة ذلك تظهرُ في المقابل؛ أي لا بغيره فالمعنى: أن الغير ليس آلةٌ في قيامه تعالى، فهو نظير ما سبق في وجوده لذاته لا لعلَّةٍ، ولكن الأولى أن الباء للسببيَّة؛ لأن الآلة واسطة الفعل، ولا تناسب هنا، كما لا يناسب جعلها للتعدية؛ لأن مجرور الباء التي للتعدية يكون مفعولاً به معنى؛ كـ ذَهَبَ اللهُ بِنُورِهِمْ ﴾ [البقرة: ١٧] ، ولا كذلك ما هنا، وجعلها الشيخ الملوي بمعنى (في)، فهي للظرفية المجازية، فالمعنى: قيامه في نفسه ليس باعتبار شيءٍ آخر ؛ كما يقال : هذا العبد في نفسه يساوي كذا)؛ أي: لا باعتبار شيء آخر معه .
والمراد من النفس هنا الذاتُ ؛ فإنها تطلق على الذات كما هنا، وتطلقُ على الدم كما في قولهم : ( ما لا نفسَ له سائلةٌ لا يُنجس الماء)، وعلى الأنفة كما في قولهم : (فلان) ذو نَفْسٍ)، وعلى العقوبة، قيل: منه قوله تعالى: ﴿وَيُعَذِرُكُمُ اللهُ نَفْسَةُ) [آل عمران: ۲۸]؛ أي: عقوبته. والحقُّ أنه يجوز إطلاقُ النَّفْس على ذاته تعالى من غير مشاكلة؛ كما يدلُّ عليه قوله :تعالى: ﴿كَتَبَ رَبُّكُمْ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ الرَّحْمَةُ [الانعام: ٥٤، خلافاً لمن زعم أنها لا تطلقُ عليه تعالى إلا مشاكلةٌ ؛ كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿تَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِي وَلَا أَعْلَمُ مَا فِي نَفْسِكَ المائدة : ١١٦].
(۱) انظر حاشية العلامة الأمير على عبد السلام (ص) (٦٨) .
[4. The attribute of His self-subsistence and its proof
25 - His self-subsistence (qiyāmuhu bi al-nafs) and unity, transcendent are His lofty attributes. His statement: (His self-subsistence) is coordinated with existence by omitting the conjunction, and the estimation is: and obligatory is His self-subsistence. So the definite article in 'al-nafs' substitutes for the possessive. And the commentator's statement: (The fourth attribute among the necessary negative attributes of Him, exalted, is His self-subsistence) is a semantic explanation, not a parsing explanation, as previously. Some have made the 'ba' in His statement (bi al-nafs) the instrumental 'ba', and its origin is from al-Shaktani, and in it there is disrespect. Sheikh Yahya al-Shawi extricated from the disrespect by saying that its benefit appears in the meaning of contrast, i.e., not by another, so the meaning is that the other is not an instrument in His self-subsistence, exalted. It is similar to what preceded in His existence by itself not by a cause. But the better is that the 'ba' is for causation (sababiyya), because the instrument is a intermediary for the act, and it is not appropriate here, just as making it transitive 'ba' is not appropriate, because the object of the transitive 'ba' becomes an indirect object in meaning, like {Allah took away their light} [Al-Baqara: 17], and not so here. And Sheikh al-Mallawi made it to mean 'in', so it is for metaphorical location, so the meaning is: His subsistence in Himself not by consideration of something else, as it is said: This servant in himself is worth such-and-such, i.e., not by consideration of something else with him.
And what is intended by 'al-nafs' here is the essence (al-dhāt). For it is applied to the essence, as here, and it is applied to blood, as in their statement: (Whatever has no flowing blood does not render water impure), and to haughtiness, as in their statement: (So-and-so has a spirit/self), and to punishment, it is said: from it is His statement, exalted: {And Allah warns you of Himself} [Aal-Imran: 28], i.e., His punishment. And the truth is that it is permissible to apply 'al-nafs' to His essence, exalted, without rhetorical accommodation (mushākala), as indicated by His statement, exalted: {Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself} [Al-An'am: 54], contrary to those who claim that it is not applied to Him, exalted, except by rhetorical accommodation, as in His statement, exalted: {You know what is in my soul and I do not know what is in Your soul} [Al-Ma'ida: 116].
(1) See the marginalia of al-Amir on Abd al-Salam (p. 68).
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute of qiyām bi al-nafs (self-subsistence) as one of the necessary negative attributes of Allah. It begins by explaining the grammatical construction: the phrase is coordinated with 'existence' by omitting the conjunction, and the definite article in 'al-nafs' substitutes for the possessive. The commentator clarifies that the meaning is being explained, not the parsing. The discussion then shifts to the preposition 'ba' in 'bi al-nafs'. Some scholars interpreted it as the instrumental 'ba', implying that the self is the instrument, but this is considered disrespectful because Allah is not an instrument. Sheikh Yahya al-Shawi resolved this by saying the benefit appears in contrast: i.e., not by another, so no other is an instrument. However, the preferred interpretation is that 'ba' is for causation (sababiyya), because an instrument mediates an act, which is unsuitable here. The transitive 'ba' is also unsuitable because it would make 'al-nafs' an object, which is not the case. Sheikh al-Mallawi interpreted it as 'in' for metaphorical location, meaning His subsistence in Himself without consideration of anything else. Next, the passage defines 'al-nafs' as essence (dhāt), listing its other meanings (blood, haughtiness, punishment) and citing the Quranic verse where Allah says He prescribed mercy upon His nafs. The author asserts that it is permissible to use nafs for Allah without rhetorical accommodation, contrary to those who claim it is only used by way of mushākala (accommodation), as in the verse where Jesus says 'You know what is in my soul...'. The footnote references a marginalia for further reading.

Key Terms
قيام بالنفس
الباء الآلة
الباء السببية
الظرفية المجازية
النفس
الذات
المشاكلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the preposition 'ba' in 'bi al-nafs' is instrumental, implying that Allah's essence is an instrument for His subsistence.
Correction: The author argues this is disrespectful and not appropriate; the preferred interpretation is causal 'ba', which does not imply any mediation or instrument.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the preferred interpretation of the preposition 'ba' in 'bi al-nafs'?
A) Instrumental 'ba'
B) Transitive 'ba'
C) 'Ba' for causation
D) 'Ba' for metaphorical location

Key Takeaway
The linguistic analysis of qiyām bi al-nafs shows that Allah's self-subsistence is not through any instrument or cause, but is an essential attribute affirmed without accommodation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel your existence depends on something—your job, your health, your relationships—how does knowing that Allah alone is self-subsistent (qiyam bi al-nafs) challenge your heart's attachments? What would it take for you to truly live as if nothing else is necessary for your existence but Him?
?
The passage warns against interpreting 'bi al-nafs' in a way that implies Allah uses an instrument. Are there subtle ways in your own worship or thinking that you treat Allah as needing something from you or from creation to maintain His sovereignty? How can you repent of that?
?
If Allah's essence is described as 'nafs' without accommodation (mushakala), what does that reveal about the intimacy of His self-disclosure? Do you allow yourself to be known by Him in that same intimate way, or do you hide behind pretense?

The Linguistic Derivation of 'Wahdaniyya'
Defines 'wahdaniyya' and discusses its derivation and a dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how kalam scholars analyzed the word 'wahdāniyya' linguistically and the debate over its morphological derivation.

Core Idea
Linguistic analysis of divine attributes reveals theological precision.

This passage examines the word 'wahdāniyya', a key divine attribute. It explains its derivation from 'waḥda' and a scholarly dispute over the function of its suffix. The debate shows how careful analysis of language supports theological clarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note the key terms and the two opposing views on the yā' suffix.
The passage is dense; take time to understand each part.
2
Identify the two positions
Identify the majority view that the yā' is for relationality (nisba) with intensification, and Yaḥyā al-Shāwī's objection that it should be a verbal noun (maṣdar).
The disagreement hinges on whether the suffix indicates a secondary attribute or the attribute itself.
3
Understand the majority response
The majority replied that a thing can be attributed to itself for intensification, preserving the nisba interpretation.
This shows how linguistic forms can emphasize the reality of an attribute.
4
Reflect on the theological import
Consider why this matters: affirming Allah's oneness directly without implying anything separate from His essence.
Kalam scholars use language to protect tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَحْدَانِيَّة) معطوف على (الوجود) بحذف حرف العطف ؛ أي: وواجب له وحدانية، وما ذكره الشارح حل معنى لا حل إعراب كما تقدم، وهي بفتح الواو نسبة إلى الوحدة، فياؤُها للنسب والألف والنون للمبالغة؛ كما في (رقباني) نسبة للرَّقبة، و(شعراني) نسبة للشعر، وقال يحيى الشاوي : (لا يصح كون الياء للنسب؛ إذ المراد ثبوت الوحدة نفسها، لا ثبوت شيء منسوب إليها)، واختار جعلَها للمصدر ؛ كما في الضاربيَّة، وأجاب الأوَّلون بأن الشيء ينسب لنفسه مبالغة
His saying: (Wahdāniyya) is conjoined to (al-wujūd) with the conjunction omitted; that is: and the Necessary has oneness (waḥdāniyya). What the commentator mentioned is an explanation of meaning, not of parsing, as previously. It is with a fatḥa on the wāw, a relational (nisba) to al-waḥda; its yā’ is for relationality and the alif and nūn are for intensification (mubālagha), as in (raqbānī) relational to the neck, and (shaʿrānī) relational to hair. Yaḥyā al-Shāwī said: 'It is not correct that the yā’ is for relationality, because the intended meaning is the affirmation of oneness itself, not the affirmation of something attributed to it.' And he chose to make it for the verbal noun (maṣdar), as in (ḍāribiyya). The first group replied that a thing is attributed to itself for intensification.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage defines the word 'wahdāniyya' and its grammatical and theological significance. The author explains that the term is derived from 'waḥda' (oneness) with the addition of a relational yā and an intensive alif-nūn, similar to words like 'raqbānī' and 'shaʿrānī'. A scholar named Yaḥyā al-Shāwī objected that the relational yā would imply something attributed to oneness, whereas the intended meaning is the oneness itself. He therefore argued that the yā is actually a masdar (verbal noun) suffix, like in 'ḍāribiyya'. The original scholars responded that attributing a thing to itself can be a form of intensification, so the relational yā is still valid. This shows the precision of kalam scholars in linguistic analysis when defining divine attributes.

Key Terms
وَحْدَانِيَّة
وَاجِب
الوَحْدَة
النِّسْبَة
المُبَالَغَة
المَصْدَر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The suffix in 'wahdāniyya' indicates a separate attribute added to oneness.
Correction: The suffix is either for intensification (majority view) or for verbal noun (al-Shāwī's view), both affirming oneness itself.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the alif and nūn in 'wahdāniyya' according to the majority?
A) They indicate the dual number.
B) They are for intensification (mubālagha).
C) They are for the verbal noun (maṣdar).
D) They are for the past tense.

Key Takeaway
The word 'wahdāniyya' directly affirms Allah's oneness, and its intensive form emphasizes that oneness without implying any addition to His essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you declare 'Allah is One,' do you feel the same intensity as the word 'wahdāniyya' conveys through its intensive form, or is your tongue reciting while your heart is divided by worldly attachments? What specific attachment will you let go of today to make your declaration more real?
?
The scholars debated whether the yā' in 'wahdāniyya' implies a relation or the essence itself. In your daily life, do you treat Allah's oneness as an abstract concept 'out there,' or does it shape every thought and action? Will you, right now, ask yourself: In what area of my life do I still act as if someone else has a say?
?
The first group said a thing can be attributed to itself for intensification. You call yourself a 'Muslim' — is that just a label you were born into, or is it an intense reality that defines your very being? The Meaning of al-Ilahiyyat and the Three Unities
Defines al-ilahiyyat and explains unity of essence, attributes, and actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what 'al-ilahiyyat' means in this theological context and the three aspects of divine unity: unity of essence, unity of attributes, and unity of actions.

Core Idea
Divine unity has three dimensions: no equal in essence, no multiple attributes, no effect from others in actions.

This passage opens the discussion of divine matters (al-ilahiyyat) by clarifying what is meant by divine unity. The author distinguishes three distinct aspects of oneness, each addressing a different potential misunderstanding. Understanding these distinctions is essential for grasping the later detailed discussions in the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice how the author defines 'al-ilahiyyat' and immediately introduces the three unities.
2
Identify the three unities
The passage mentions three types of oneness: oneness of essence, oneness of attributes, and oneness of actions. Find each one and note what aspect of God's uniqueness it addresses.
The author refers to earlier and later sections for some of these.
3
Understand the meaning of each unity
For each unity, understand the specific meaning: essence is not composed of parts; attributes are not multiple of the same kind; actions have no effect from any other being.
This prevents misunderstandings about God's nature.
4
Connect to broader theology
Reflect on why each type of unity is necessary for affirming God's absolute oneness. How would violating any of these lead to associating partners with God?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات والمراد منها هنا : وحدة الذات والصفات؛ بمعنى عدم النظير فيهما ؛ وأما وحدة الذاتِ بمعنى عدم التركب من أجزاء، فسبقت في المخالفة للحوادث، ووحدة الصفاتِ بمعنى عدم تعدُّدها من جنس واحدٍ كقدرتين فأكثر وعلمين فأكثر وهكذا، فستاتي في قوله : ( ووحدة أوجب لها ووحدة الأفعال بمعنى أنه لا تأثير لغيره في فعل من الأفعال، فستأتي أيضاً في قوله : فخالق لعبده وما عمل.
Al-ilahiyyat (divine matters): what is meant here is the oneness of essence and attributes, meaning the absence of any equal in both. As for oneness of essence in the sense of not being composed of parts, it preceded in the section on dissimilarity to contingent things. And oneness of attributes in the sense of their not being multiple of the same kind, such as two or more powers, two or more knowledges, and so on—it will come in his saying: 'And oneness necessitated her.' And oneness of actions in the sense that no other has any effect in any action—it will also come in his saying: 'So the Creator of His servant and what he does.'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-ilahiyyat' (divine matters) as used in this work. It specifies that the intended meaning here is the oneness (wahdaniyya) of God's essence and attributes, understood as having no peer or equal. The author distinguishes three aspects of oneness: (1) oneness of essence, which means not being composed of parts—this was already covered in the earlier discussion of dissimilarity to contingent things; (2) oneness of attributes, meaning that there are not multiple attributes of the same kind (e.g., two powers or two knowledges)—this will be addressed later in the line 'and oneness necessitated her'; (3) oneness of actions, meaning that no other being has any effect in any action—this will be addressed later in the line 'so the Creator of His servant and what he does.' The passage thus sets the stage for a detailed discussion of divine unity.

النظير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'oneness of attributes' means all attributes are identical or that attributes merge into one.
Correction: The passage clarifies that oneness of attributes means there are not multiple attributes of the same type (like two powers), not that attributes are one and the same.

Key Takeaway
The passage defines 'al-ilahiyyat' in terms of three aspects of divine unity—essence, attributes, and actions—each essential to affirming God's absolute uniqueness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Ahl al-Sunna Definition of Divine Speech
Al-Ajhuri presents the Ahl al-Sunna definition of God's Speech

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ahl al-Sunna definition of God's Speech as an eternal attribute not composed of letters or sounds, and the negations that distinguish it from created speech.

Core Idea
God's Speech is an eternal attribute, not letters or sounds.

This lesson examines the core definition of Divine Speech according to Ahl al-Sunna. The passage clarifies that God's Speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, transcendent of temporal properties and physical composition. Understanding this definition is foundational for distinguishing the orthodox view from sectarian deviations.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition of Ahl al-Sunna
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own clarity come through.
Notice the concise style.
2
Identify the positive statement and negations
Find the positive statement (eternal attribute) and then each negation (not letter/sound, not temporal, not planning silence, not inability).
3
Understand the two types of silence excluded
The passage negates internal silence (planning but not uttering) and the defect of inability. Reflect on why these are necessary to preserve divine perfection.
4
Reflect on the theological importance
Ask yourself why these negations are essential for a proper understanding of God's Speech. The commentary will help connect to broader doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد اختلف أهل الملل والمذاهب في معنى كلامه تعالى، فقال أهل السنة : صفةٌ أزليةٌ قائمة بذاته تعالى ليست بحرف ولا صوت منزهة عن التقدم والتأخر والإعراب والبناء، ومنزهة عن السكوت النفسي بألا يدبر في نفسه الكلام مع القدرة عليه، ومنزهةً عن الآفة الباطنية بألا يقدر على ذلك والطفولية. ا في حال الخرس وقالت الحشوية وطائفةٌ سموا أنفسهم بالحنابلة كلامه تعالى هو الحروف والأصواتُ المتوالية المترتبةُ ، ويزعمون أنها قديمة، وتغالى بعضُهم حتى زعم قدم هذه الحروف التي نقرؤها والرسوم، بل تجاوز جهلُ بعضهم لغلافِ المصحف (۳) . (۳) قال الإمام السنوسي في شرح الكبرى ( ص ٢٦٧) : (وبالجملة فاعتقاد الحشوية تألف من ضلالات ثلاث : : من تهود وتنصُّر واعتزال ؛ فهم مع اليهود في اعتقادهم الجسم في حق الله، ومع النصارى في اعتقاد حلول الكلام في الأجسام، وأنه لا يفارق مع ذلك الإله، ومع المعتزلة في اعتقاد أن كلام الله تعالى حروف وأصوات، وهو نص مذهب اليهود أيضاً، غير أن المعتزلة لم يقولوا بقيام الحروف والأصوات به سبحانه وتعالى لما تفطنوا لحدوثها، وأدركوا أن قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى محال، وهؤلاء حكموا بذلك لعظيم غباوتهم وجهلهم الضروريات التي تدرك بأوائل العقول، واشترك الجميع في عدم تعقل ما قاله أهل الحق من إثبات كلام ليس بحرف ولا صوت قائم بنفس المتكلم، يعبر عنه بالكلام اللفظي والكتابة والرموز والإشارات).
And the people of religions and sects differed concerning the meaning of His speech, Most High. The Ahl al-Sunna said: It is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, Most High, not a letter nor a sound, transcendent of anteriority and posteriority, declension and construction, transcendent of internal silence such that He does not plan speech in Himself while having the ability to do it, and transcendent of internal defect such that He is unable to do so, and of [the defect of] childhood like in the state of muteness. And the Hashwiyya and a sect who call themselves Hanābila said: His speech, Most High, is the successive ordered letters and sounds, and they claim they are eternal, and some of them exaggerated until they claimed the eternity of these letters that we recite and the scripts, rather the ignorance of some of them exceeded to the cover of the mus'haf. (3) Imam al-Sanusi said in Sharh al-Kubra (p. 267): “In summary, the belief of the Hashwiyya is composed of three misguidances: from Judaism, Christianity, and Mu'tazilism. They are with the Jews in their belief in the body with respect to God, with the Christians in their belief in the indwelling of speech in bodies and that it does not depart from the divine, and with the Mu'tazila in their belief that God's speech is letters and sounds – this is also the explicit doctrine of the Jews, except that the Mu'tazila did not say that letters and sounds subsist in Him, the Exalted, because they realized their temporality and understood that the subsistence of temporal things in His essence, Most High, is impossible. These [Hashwiyya] judged that due to their great stupidity and ignorance of necessary truths perceived by first principles. And all of them share in not comprehending what the People of Truth affirmed: the affirmation of speech that is not a letter nor a sound, subsisting in the essence of the speaker, which is expressed by verbal speech, writing, symbols, and indications.”
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage presents the differing views on the meaning of God's Speech, then proceeds in three main parts.

First, it gives the definition of Ahl al-Sunna: God's Speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence. It is not composed of letters or sounds, and it is transcendent of temporal properties like anteriority/posteriority or grammatical features like declension. It is also transcendent of two types of silence: internal silence (where one plans speech but does not utter it) and the defect of being unable to speak (like muteness or childhood). This establishes that God's Speech is a perfect, eternal attribute.

Second, it presents the view of the Hashwiyya and a group claiming to be Hanābila, who assert that God's Speech consists of letters and sounds that are ordered and successive, and they claim these are eternal. Some among them exaggerated so far as to claim the eternity of the very letters we recite and the physical scripts, and the ignorance of some even extended to the cover of the mushaf (bound copy of the Qur'an). This is a clear innovation.

Third, the commentator includes a lengthy excerpt from Imam al-Sanusi's Sharh al-Kubra, which analyzes the Hashwiyya belief as a combination of three misguidances: Jewish (corporalism), Christian (indwelling of speech in bodies), and Mu'tazilite (speech as letters/sounds). Al-Sanusi points out that the Mu'tazila at least recognized that temporal letters cannot subsist in God, whereas the Hashwiyya failed to grasp this, affirming corporeal attributes in God. The People of Truth (Ahl al-Haqq), by contrast, affirm a speech that is not letters or sounds, subsisting in God, expressed through verbal words, writing, symbols, and indications.

Key Terms
صفة أزلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ahl al-Sunna believe God's Speech is the same as the physical Qur'an.
Correction: No, Ahl al-Sunna define it as an eternal attribute not consisting of letters or sounds; the recited Qur'an is its expression.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Sunna as stated in the passage, God's Speech is:
A) Letters and sounds that are eternal
B) An eternal attribute not composed of letters or sounds
C) Created letters and sounds not subsisting in God
D) Identical to the physical Qur'an

Key Takeaway
God's Speech is an eternal attribute, not letters or sounds, ensuring divine transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you sense that you are uttering letters and sounds, or do you perceive it as the eternal speech of the Lord? What would change if you fully believed the latter?
?
The passage says God's speech is free from 'internal silence'—He does not plan speech without uttering. How does this perfection expose your own procrastination in speaking the truth? When have you remained silent out of fear when you should have spoken?
?
If God's speech is timeless and not composed, then your relationship with the Qur'an is not just with a book but with an attribute of God. Does your heart tremble at this reality? Write one concrete change you will make in your daily interaction with the Qur'an starting today.

Refutation of the Hashwiyya on Divine Speech
Al-Ajhuri presents the Hashwiyya view and refutes it through al-Sanusi's analysis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hashwiyya view that God's Speech is composed of eternal letters and sounds, and the refutation by the People of Truth.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya belief in letter-speech is a composite of Jewish, Christian, and Mu'tazilite errors.

The passage contrasts the Ahl al-Sunna definition with the deviant view of the Hashwiyya, who claim God's Speech consists of successive letters and sounds, asserting their eternity. Imam al-Sanusi's commentary exposes this as a mixture of three misguidances. This lesson clarifies the orthodox rejection and the logical flaws in the Hashwiyya position.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hashwiyya claim about God's Speech
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully to understand their assertion: God's Speech is successive ordered letters and sounds, and they claim these are eternal.
Notice the exaggeration: even scripts and covers are considered eternal.
2
Examine the three misguidances in al-Sanusi's analysis
Identify the three sources of deviation: Judaism, Christianity, and Mu'tazilism. Understand how each contributes to the Hashwiyya error.
3
Understand the difference between Mu'tazila and Hashwiyya
Al-Sanusi notes that the Mu'tazila did not attribute letters/sounds to God because they recognized them as temporal. The Hashwiyya failed to see this due to ignorance.
4
Contrast with the Ahl al-Haqq position
The People of Truth affirm a speech not composed of letters/sounds, subsisting in God, expressed through verbal speech, writing, symbols, and indications.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why defining God's Speech as letters/sounds leads to anthropomorphism and temporality, and why the Ahl al-Sunna definition preserves divine transcendence.

Refutation of Mu'tazila on Divine Speech
Al-Ajhuri presents and refutes the Mu'tazila view on divine speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position on divine speech and why Sunni scholars reject it, including the linguistic evidence for internal speech.

Core Idea
God's speech is not merely temporal letters; it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence.

The Mu'tazila reduce divine speech to created letters and sounds, denying that speech can be an attribute of God. In this passage, al-Ajhuri presents their view and then refutes it using a famous line of poetry that proves speech can exist in the heart without external utterance. This opens the door to understanding God's eternal speech as a true attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila claim
Read the first sentence: 'And the Mu'tazila said: His speech is the temporal letters and sounds...' Understand what they deny (that speech subsists in God) and what they affirm (God creates speech in bodies).
2
Reflect on the refutation
Then read the refutation: 'This is refuted by the fact that internal speech is established linguistically.' Notice the use of al-Akhtal's poetry as evidence.
Internal speech (kalām nafsī) is a key concept.
3
Analyze the evidence
Examine the poetic line: 'Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is only a guide to the heart.' This shows that speech (kalām) can mean inner meaning, not just uttered words.
The poet uses 'speech' for what is in the heart, proving the linguistic validity of internal speech.
4
Connect to the divine attribute
Understand why this refutes the Mu'tazila: if speech can be internal, God's speech can be an eternal attribute without temporal letters.
The refutation is direct and relies on common linguistic usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المعتزلة : كلامه هو الحروف والأصوات الحادثة وهي غير قائمة بذاته، فمعنى كونه متكلّماً عندهم : أنه خالق للكلام في بعض الأجسام) ، لزعمهم الكلام لا يكون إلا بحروف وأصوات، وهو مردودٌ بأن الكلام النفسي ثابت لغةً كما في قول الأخطل : [من الكامل] إِنَّ الكَلامَ لَفِي الفُؤادِ وإِنَّما جُعِلَ النِّسَانُ عَلى الفُؤادِ دَلِيلاً وكلامه تعالى صفةٌ واحدةٌ لا تعددَ فيها، لكن لها أقسام اعتبارية، فمن حيث تعلقه يطلب فعل الصلاة مثلاً أمر ، ومن حيث تعلقه بطلب ترك الزنا مثلاً نهي، ومن حيث تعلقه بأن فرعون فعل كذا مثلاً خبر، ومن حيث تعلقه بأن الطائع له الجنة وعد، ومن حيث تعلقه بأن العاصي يدخل النار وعيد، إلى غير ذلك، وتعلقه بالنسبة لغير الأمر والنهي : تعلق تنجيزي قديمٌ ، وأما بالنسبة للأمر والنهي : فإن لم يشترط فيهما وجود المأمور والمنهي ، فكذلك ، وإن اشترط فيهما ذلك كان التعلق فيهما صلوحيا قبل وجود المأمور والمنهي، وتنجيزيا حادثاً بعد وجودهما . واعلم أن كلام الله يُطلق على الكلام النفسي القديم، بمعنى أنه صفةٌ قائمة بذاته تعالى، وعلى الكلام اللفظي بمعنى أنه خلقه ، وليس لأحدٍ في أصل تركيبه كسب وعلى هذا المعنى يحمل قول السيدة عائشة : ما بين دفتي المصحف كلام الله تعالى.
And the Mu'tazila said: His speech is the temporal letters and sounds, and they do not subsist in His essence. So the meaning of His being a speaker according to them is that He creates speech in some bodies, because they claim that speech is only letters and sounds. This is refuted by the fact that internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is established linguistically, as in the saying of al-Akhtal: “Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is only made a guide to the heart.” And His speech, Most High, is a single attribute with no multiplicity in it, but it has conceptual divisions. In terms of its relation to requesting the performance of prayer, for example, it is a command; in terms of its relation to requesting the abandonment of adultery, for example, it is a prohibition; in terms of its relation to [informing that] Pharaoh did such-and-such, for example, it is an informatory report; in terms of its relation to [informing that] the obedient has Paradise, it is a promise; in terms of its relation to [informing that] the disobedient enters Hell, it is a threat, and so on. And its relation with respect to other than command and prohibition is a determinate (tanjīzī) relation, eternal. As for command and prohibition: if the existence of the commanded and the prohibited is not stipulated in them, then it is likewise [determinate and eternal]. But if that is stipulated, then the relation in them is potential (ṣulūḥī) before the existence of the commanded and the prohibited, and determinate (tanjīzī), temporal after their existence. And know that the speech of God is applied to the eternal internal speech, meaning that it is an attribute subsisting in His essence, Most High, and to the verbal speech, meaning that He creates it, and no one has acquisition in its original composition. And in this sense is understood the saying of Lady Aisha: “What is between the two covers of the mushaf is the speech of God.”
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position on divine speech followed by an elaboration of the Ahl al-Sunna doctrine of a single eternal attribute with multiple conceptual relations. It proceeds as follows:

First, it presents the Mu'tazila view: God's speech consists of temporally created letters and sounds that do not subsist in God's essence; rather, God is called a speaker because He creates speech in some bodies (like the preserved tablet, Gabriel, etc.). This is based on their assumption that speech can only be letters and sounds.

Second, the passage refutes this view by pointing out that internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is linguistically established, citing the famous verse of the poet al-Akhtal: “Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is only a guide to the heart.” This demonstrates that speech can exist without external letters and sounds.

Third, it establishes the correct doctrine: God's speech is a single attribute with no multiplicity in its essence, but it has conceptual divisions (aqsām iʿtibāriyya) based on its relations. For example, when it relates to commanding an action (like prayer), it is called a command (amr); when relating to prohibiting an action (like adultery), it is called a prohibition (nahy); when relating to informing about past events (like Pharaoh's actions), it is called an informatory report (khabar); when relating to reward, it is a promise (waʿd); when relating to punishment, it is a threat (waʿīd), and so on.

Fourth, it explains the nature of the relation (taʿalluq) of this speech to its objects. For relations other than command and prohibition (such as reports), the relation is determinate (tanjīzī) and eternal, because the object is already known. For command and prohibition, there is a nuance: if the existence of the commanded person and the prohibited act is not considered a condition, then the relation is also determinate and eternal (since the command is known in God's foreknowledge). However, if one stipulates that command and prohibition require the actual existence of the commanded and prohibited, then before their existence the relation is potential (ṣulūḥī), and after their existence it becomes determinate and temporal.

Fifth, the commentator concludes with an important note on terminology: the term “speech of God” (kalām Allāh) is applied both to the eternal internal speech (the attribute) and to the created verbal speech (the recited Qur'an). In the latter sense, it means that God creates it, and no one has any acquisition in its original composition. On this basis, the saying of Lady ʿĀʾisha is interpreted: “What is between the two covers of the mushaf is the speech of God,” meaning the created verbal expression of the eternal attribute.

Key Terms
المعتزلة
الكلام النفسي
الكلام اللفظي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine speech must be composed of temporal letters and sounds.
Correction: Speech can be internal (meaning) as established linguistically; God's speech is an eternal attribute, not created.

Checkpoint
What is the main evidence used to refute the Mu'tazila claim?
A) A verse from the Qur'an
B) A line of poetry by al-Akhtal
C) A hadith
D) Rational proof

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila are refuted by the linguistic reality that speech can be internal, establishing that God's speech is an eternal attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's speech were merely created letters, how would that affect your relationship with the Quran as His direct address? Does this lesson challenge your assumption that speech is only audible? Examine whether you have ever considered God's attributes as real and eternal, independent of creation. How does this truth change the weight of every word you utter?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you believe you are encountering an eternal attribute of God or a temporal creation? Let this lesson press you to ask: Is your reverence for the Quran consistent with the belief that it is His eternal speech? What would change in your heart if you truly internalised that?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced speech to letters and sounds. Reflect: Have you ever reduced a deeper reality of Islam to mere outward forms? Where in your life do you need to move beyond the external to grasp the eternal attribute behind it?

What Is Kalam?
intermediate
12 min
God's Speech is One Attribute with Conceptual Divisions
Al-Ajhuri explains that God's speech is a single attribute with conceptual divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's speech is a single eternal attribute but has multiple conceptual modes such as command, prohibition, report, promise, and threat based on its relation to different objects.

Core Idea
God's speech is one, yet it is called command or report depending on what it relates to.

Having refuted the Mu'tazila, al-Ajhuri now explains the correct Sunni doctrine: God's speech is one attribute with no real multiplicity. However, we give it different names like 'command' or 'report' based on its relation to created things. This lesson explores how a single divine speech can be described in many ways.

Guided Path
1
Read the core statement
Read: 'And His speech, Most High, is a single attribute with no multiplicity in it, but it has conceptual divisions.' Understand that the attribute itself is one.
The divisions are only in our conceptual understanding, not in reality.
2
Identify the divisions
Then note the list: when relating to commanding prayer it's called a command; to prohibiting adultery, a prohibition; to informing about Pharaoh, a report; to promising Paradise, a promise; to threatening Hell, a threat.
These are all the same attribute, just different names due to different relations.
3
Understand 'conceptual'
Reflect that these divisions are 'iʿtibāriyya' (conceptual), meaning they exist in our mind, not in the essence of the attribute.
This safeguards tawhid by avoiding multiplicity in God.
4
Connect to daily language
Think of a single law that commands one person and prohibits another; it's the same law but called different things.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال المعتزلة : كلامه هو الحروف والأصوات الحادثة وهي غير قائمة بذاته، فمعنى كونه متكلّماً عندهم : أنه خالق للكلام في بعض الأجسام) ، لزعمهم الكلام لا يكون إلا بحروف وأصوات، وهو مردودٌ بأن الكلام النفسي ثابت لغةً كما في قول الأخطل : [من الكامل] إِنَّ الكَلامَ لَفِي الفُؤادِ وإِنَّما جُعِلَ النِّسَانُ عَلى الفُؤادِ دَلِيلاً وكلامه تعالى صفةٌ واحدةٌ لا تعددَ فيها، لكن لها أقسام اعتبارية، فمن حيث تعلقه يطلب فعل الصلاة مثلاً أمر ، ومن حيث تعلقه بطلب ترك الزنا مثلاً نهي، ومن حيث تعلقه بأن فرعون فعل كذا مثلاً خبر، ومن حيث تعلقه بأن الطائع له الجنة وعد، ومن حيث تعلقه بأن العاصي يدخل النار وعيد، إلى غير ذلك، وتعلقه بالنسبة لغير الأمر والنهي : تعلق تنجيزي قديمٌ ، وأما بالنسبة للأمر والنهي : فإن لم يشترط فيهما وجود المأمور والمنهي ، فكذلك ، وإن اشترط فيهما ذلك كان التعلق فيهما صلوحيا قبل وجود المأمور والمنهي، وتنجيزيا حادثاً بعد وجودهما . واعلم أن كلام الله يُطلق على الكلام النفسي القديم، بمعنى أنه صفةٌ قائمة بذاته تعالى، وعلى الكلام اللفظي بمعنى أنه خلقه ، وليس لأحدٍ في أصل تركيبه كسب وعلى هذا المعنى يحمل قول السيدة عائشة : ما بين دفتي المصحف كلام الله تعالى.
And the Mu'tazila said: His speech is the temporal letters and sounds, and they do not subsist in His essence. So the meaning of His being a speaker according to them is that He creates speech in some bodies, because they claim that speech is only letters and sounds. This is refuted by the fact that internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is established linguistically, as in the saying of al-Akhtal: “Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is only made a guide to the heart.” And His speech, Most High, is a single attribute with no multiplicity in it, but it has conceptual divisions. In terms of its relation to requesting the performance of prayer, for example, it is a command; in terms of its relation to requesting the abandonment of adultery, for example, it is a prohibition; in terms of its relation to [informing that] Pharaoh did such-and-such, for example, it is an informatory report; in terms of its relation to [informing that] the obedient has Paradise, it is a promise; in terms of its relation to [informing that] the disobedient enters Hell, it is a threat, and so on. And its relation with respect to other than command and prohibition is a determinate (tanjīzī) relation, eternal. As for command and prohibition: if the existence of the commanded and the prohibited is not stipulated in them, then it is likewise [determinate and eternal]. But if that is stipulated, then the relation in them is potential (ṣulūḥī) before the existence of the commanded and the prohibited, and determinate (tanjīzī), temporal after their existence. And know that the speech of God is applied to the eternal internal speech, meaning that it is an attribute subsisting in His essence, Most High, and to the verbal speech, meaning that He creates it, and no one has acquisition in its original composition. And in this sense is understood the saying of Lady Aisha: “What is between the two covers of the mushaf is the speech of God.”
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila position on divine speech followed by an elaboration of the Ahl al-Sunna doctrine of a single eternal attribute with multiple conceptual relations. It proceeds as follows:

First, it presents the Mu'tazila view: God's speech consists of temporally created letters and sounds that do not subsist in God's essence; rather, God is called a speaker because He creates speech in some bodies (like the preserved tablet, Gabriel, etc.). This is based on their assumption that speech can only be letters and sounds.

Second, the passage refutes this view by pointing out that internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) is linguistically established, citing the famous verse of the poet al-Akhtal: “Indeed speech is in the heart, and the tongue is only a guide to the heart.” This demonstrates that speech can exist without external letters and sounds.

Third, it establishes the correct doctrine: God's speech is a single attribute with no multiplicity in its essence, but it has conceptual divisions (aqsām iʿtibāriyya) based on its relations. For example, when it relates to commanding an action (like prayer), it is called a command (amr); when relating to prohibiting an action (like adultery), it is called a prohibition (nahy); when relating to informing about past events (like Pharaoh's actions), it is called an informatory report (khabar); when relating to reward, it is a promise (waʿd); when relating to punishment, it is a threat (waʿīd), and so on.

Fourth, it explains the nature of the relation (taʿalluq) of this speech to its objects. For relations other than command and prohibition (such as reports), the relation is determinate (tanjīzī) and eternal, because the object is already known. For command and prohibition, there is a nuance: if the existence of the commanded person and the prohibited act is not considered a condition, then the relation is also determinate and eternal (since the command is known in God's foreknowledge). However, if one stipulates that command and prohibition require the actual existence of the commanded and prohibited, then before their existence the relation is potential (ṣulūḥī), and after their existence it becomes determinate and temporal.

Fifth, the commentator concludes with an important note on terminology: the term “speech of God” (kalām Allāh) is applied both to the eternal internal speech (the attribute) and to the created verbal speech (the recited Qur'an). In the latter sense, it means that God creates it, and no one has any acquisition in its original composition. On this basis, the saying of Lady ʿĀʾisha is interpreted: “What is between the two covers of the mushaf is the speech of God,” meaning the created verbal expression of the eternal attribute.

Key Terms
أمر
نهي
خبر
وعد
وعيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God has multiple distinct speech attributes (e.g., a separate command attribute, a separate prohibition attribute).
Correction: God's speech is a single attribute; the different names are based on relations, not real multiplicity.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for calling God's speech a 'command'?
A) It is a separate attribute
B) It relates to requesting an action
C) It is temporal
D) It is created

Key Takeaway
God's speech is one, but we call it command, prohibition, report, etc., based on its relation to different things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that God's speech is one, yet you call parts of the Qur'an 'commands' and others 'promises'. Does your treatment of the Qur'an reflect this unity, or do you pick and choose which parts to obey and which to ignore? How can you change today to listen to the Qur'an as one single address from your Lord?
?
The same attribute that commands you to pray also threatens the disobedient with Hell. If you truly internalized that these are not separate but one speech, would you still delay your salah? What would it take for you to feel the urgency of every verse as part of one divine message?
?
You may have assumed that God's commands and prohibitions were separate decrees. Now you know they are conceptual divisions of one speech. Does this change how you view God's consistency and justice? Can you name one specific command in the Qur'an that you have been neglecting, and commit to acting on it today?

Types of Relation and the Distinction Between Internal and Verbal Speech
Al-Ajhuri explains the types of relation and distinguishes internal from verbal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of relation (determinate and potential) regarding divine speech, and the distinction between God's eternal internal speech and the created verbal speech (the Qur'an).

Core Idea
God's speech has eternal relation for reports but conditional relation for commands; the Qur'an is created verbal speech expressing the eternal attribute.

After explaining the conceptual divisions of divine speech, al-Ajhuri delves into the nature of its relation to objects. He distinguishes between determinate (tanjīzī) and potential (ṣulūḥī) relations, particularly for commands and prohibitions. He also clarifies the important terminological distinction between eternal internal speech and created verbal speech, using the example of Aisha's famous statement about the mushaf.

Guided Path
1
Read about relation types
Start with: 'And its relation with respect to other than command and prohibition is a determinate relation, eternal.' Understand that for reports, promises, threats, the relation is fixed and eternal.
Tanjiizi means the relation is actual and eternal.
2
Understand the nuance for commands
Then read about commands and prohibitions: if existence is not stipulated, it's also determinate and eternal. But if existence is stipulated, it becomes potential before existence and determinate after.
Suluhi means the relation is potential until the object exists.
3
Grasp the linguistic usage
Read the final part: 'And know that the speech of God is applied to the eternal internal speech... and to the verbal speech...' Distinguish the two meanings.
Internal speech is the attribute; verbal speech is the created text.
4
Apply to Aisha's statement
Interpret her saying 'What is between the two covers of the mushaf is the speech of God' as referring to the created verbal expression of the eternal attribute.
This avoids both literalism and denial.

The Author's Intention and the Concept of Ḥāl
Bājūrī explains author's intent and defines 'ḥāl' as an intermediate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author of Jawharat al-Tawḥīd restricts his discussion to listing names and how the concept of ḥāl (state) relates to enumerating essential attributes.

Core Idea
The concept of ḥāl is a flawed intermediate between existence and non-existence.

The matn of Jawharat al-Tawḥīd begins with the names of Allah derived from attributes. In his commentary, Bājūrī clarifies that the author’s aim is not to expound upon the essential attributes (ṣifāt ma‘nawiyya) but merely to state the names, in order to refute those who deny that some attributes subsist in the Essence. This lesson unpacks that intention and introduces the controversial notion of “state” (ḥāl) upon which the listing of essential attributes depends.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice how Bājūrī contrasts the author's intent with what he did not intend.
2
Identify the author's purpose
What did the author intend? How does this refute deviant sects?
The deviants denied subsistence of attributes like speech and will.
3
Understand the definition of ḥāl
Find the definition of ḥāl and note its controversial nature.
Ḥāl is neither existent nor non-existent but an intermediate.
4
Recognize the two positions
Note that al-Sanūsī used the view of affirmers of states, but the preferred view rejects states.
The investigators say ḥāl is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة أنه أراد مجرد بيان الأسماء المأخوذة مما سبق لبيان وجوب قيام الصفة بالموصوف ردًّا على بعض فرق الضلال، حيث قالوا بعدم قيام بعضها بالموصوف كالكلام والإرادة ولم يرد بيان الصفات المعنوية، ولذا لم يقل: كونه حيًّا؛ لأنَّ عدَّ الصفات المعنوية إنما يتمشى على قول مثبت الأحوال، جمع حال؛ وهي لا موجودة ولا معدومة بل واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، وعليه جرى السنوسي في الصغرى، حيث قال: وكونه قادراً .. إلى آخره والمختار عند المحققين أنه لا حال، وأن الحال محال
An attribute that He intended only a bare statement of the names derived from what preceded to clarify the necessity of the attribute's subsistence in the described, in refutation of some sects of error, for they said that some attributes do not subsist in the described, like speech and will, and He did not intend to explain the essential attributes, so He did not say: 'His being living'; because listing the essential attributes only proceeds according to the view of the affirmers of states (aḥwāl), plural of ḥāl; and it is neither existent nor non-existent but an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent, and upon this al-Sanūsī proceeded in al-Ṣughrā, where he said: 'and His being powerful... etc.' And the chosen view among the investigators is that there is no ḥāl, and that the ḥāl is impossible.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage explains the author's purpose in mentioning the names of God (al-asmā') derived from the preceding discussion. Bajuri states that the author only intended to show the necessity of attributes subsisting in the Essence (al-dhāt) as a refutation of deviant sects who denied that some attributes like speech and will subsist in the Essence. The author did not intend to discuss the essential attributes (ṣifāt ma'nawiyya) such as 'being living', because enumerating essential attributes depends on the position of those who affirm 'states' (aḥwāl). A 'ḥāl' is defined as something neither existent nor non-existent but an intermediate between the two. Al-Sanūsī followed this view in his 'al-Ṣughrā' when he said 'and His being powerful...'. However, the preferred position of the investigators (muḥaqqiqūn) is that there is no such state; rather, the concept of ḥāl is impossible.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية
الحال
الموجودات
المعدومات
الواسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author intended to explain the essential attributes like 'being living'.
Correction: The author only intended to list names derived from attributes to refute deviants, not to expound essential attributes.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of 'ḥāl' according to those who affirm states?
A) It is an existent thing that can be seen
B) It is a non-existent thing with no reality
C) It is an intermediate between existent and non-existent
D) It is an attribute subsisting in the Essence

Key Takeaway
The concept of ḥāl is an intermediate between existence and non-existence, but the preferred Sunni position rejects it as impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you find yourself treating some of God's attributes as if they are 'sort of there but sort of not'—like a ḥāl? How does this lesson's clear rejection of such categories demand that you purify your conviction about God's perfect attributes?
?
The investigators say ḥāl is impossible. Is there any area of your faith where you have accepted a middle ground between belief and disbelief, or between existence and non-existence? Will you commit to the full clarity of affirming only what Allah and His Messenger have affirmed, without inventing intermediate concepts?
?
If you were to explain the concept of ḥāl to someone confused about it, would you speak with the same force as the 'investigators' who call it impossible? What would it cost you to hold that same firmness in your own heart?

Divine Names: Definition and Eternity
Defines 'name' and explains the eternity of God's names with grammatical analysis and ihtibak.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical scholars define the term 'name' (ism) in the context of God's names and how they argue for their eternity using grammatical analysis and the rhetorical figure of ihtibak.

Core Idea
God's names are eternal words indicating His Essence or attributes.

This passage from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid clarifies a key theological concept: what exactly are God's names? The poet states 'His great names are eternal,' and the commentator unpacks the meaning of 'name' and the grammatical structure that proves the names are eternal. By examining the Arabic grammar and the rhetorical device of ihtibak, we will see how scholars affirm the eternity of divine names without confusing them with created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the grammatical terms and the definition of 'ism'.
The Arabic includes Persian 'خدای' meaning God.
2
Understand the definition of 'name'
The commentator defines 'name' as a word that indicates the Essence alone (like Allah) or with an attribute (like al-Alim). Compare this to the common understanding.
This avoids the misconception that name and named are identical.
3
Analyze the grammar and parsing
Two grammatical readings are presented. The first takes the predicate as shared; the second uses ihtibak. Identify the subject, predicate, and the parenthetical clause.
The second reading emphasizes the rhetorical figure of mutual omission.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
The passage concludes by explaining 'the great' as sacred and purified. Note that the eternity and greatness of God's names is a matter of consensus.
This affirms that God's names are not created but eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (أَسْمَاؤُهُ ... إلخ) الأسماء جمع اسم، والمراد به ما دل على الذات بمجردها كـ (الله) و(خدای)؛ أي في اللغة الفارسية أو باعتبار الصفة كالعالم والقادر، ثم إن (أسماؤه) مبتدأ و(العظيمة) وصف كاشف، والخبر (قديمة). وقوله: (كَذَا صِفَاتُ ذَاتِهِ) مبتدأ وخبر، فـ (كذا) خبر مقدم و(صفات ذاته) مبتدأ مؤخر، والجملة معترضة بين المبتدأ وخبره والتشبيه في القدم، وأشار الشارح لإعراب آخر، فجعل خبر قوله: (أسماؤه) محذوفاً دل عليه قوله فيما بعد: (قديمة)، وجعل قوله: (قديمة) الآتي خبراً عن قوله: (صفات ذاته) فيكون المصنف حذف من الأول لدلالة الثاني، كما حذف من الثاني (عظيمة) لدلالة الأول عليه، وحينئذ ففي كلامه من المحسنات البديعية نوع احتباك؛ وهو أن يُحذف من كل نظير ما أثبته في الآخر، وعلى هذا فالتشبيه للتأكيد، والأول هو المتبادر من كلام المصنف. قوله: (العَظِيمَة) أي: الجليلة المقدسة؛ أي: المطهرة عن أن يُسمى بها الغير أو عن أن تُفسر بما لا يليق أو أن تُذكر على غير وجه التعظيم كما قاله السعد، وعِظَمُ أسمائه تعالى مُجمع عليه.
His saying: 'His names... etc.': 'al-asma' is the plural of 'ism', and what is meant by it is that which indicates the Essence by itself like 'Allah' and 'Khuday' (in the Persian language), or by consideration of an attribute like 'al-Alim' and 'al-Qadir'. Then 'His names' is a subject, 'the great' is a disclosing adjective, and the predicate is 'eternal'. His saying: 'likewise the attributes of His Essence' is a subject and predicate; 'likewise' is a forward predicate and 'attributes of His Essence' is a delayed subject, and the sentence is parenthetical between the subject and its predicate, and the comparison is in eternity. The commentator indicated another parsing: he considered the predicate of 'His names' to be omitted, indicated by his subsequent saying 'eternal', and he considered the upcoming 'eternal' to be the predicate of 'attributes of His Essence'; thus the author omitted from the first due to the indication of the second, just as he omitted from the second 'great' due to the indication of the first upon it. In that case, there is in his speech a type of rhetorical figure called 'ihtibak', which is that one omits from each paired phrase that which is affirmed in the other. According to this, the comparison is for emphasis, and the first is the more apparent from the author's speech. His saying: 'the great': i.e., the majestic, the sacred; i.e., purified from being named with them by other than Him, or from being interpreted in a way unbefitting, or from being mentioned without due veneration, as al-Sa'd said. And the greatness of His names, may He be exalted, is agreed upon.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'name' (ism) and explains the eternity of God's names, focusing on the poet's line 'His great names are eternal'. The commentary begins by defining 'name' as a word that either indicates the divine Essence alone (like 'Allah' or 'Khuday' in Persian) or indicates the Essence along with an attribute (like 'al-Alim' meaning 'the Knower'). It then parses the grammatical structure of the line: 'His names' is the subject, 'the great' is an adjective describing them, and the predicate is 'eternal' (though in the poem the predicate is delayed). The line then says 'likewise the attributes of His Essence'—the commentator explains that 'likewise' here refers back to 'eternal', and the phrase is parenthetical. Two possible parses are given: one where the predicate 'eternal' serves for both, and another where there is a rhetorical figure called 'ihtibak' (mutual omission) where each phrase supplies what is omitted from the other. In the latter interpretation, the comparison in the line is for emphasis. The passage concludes with a gloss on 'the great': it means majestic, sacred, and purified from being applied to others, from improper interpretation, or from being mentioned without veneration. The greatness of God's names is a matter of consensus.

الذات
الصفة

احتباك
العظيمة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The name of God is the same as His Essence or is a created thing.
Correction: A name is a word indicating the Essence or attribute; it is eternal, not created, and is sacred.

Checkpoint

B) A word that indicates the Essence either alone or with an attribute

Key Takeaway
God's names are eternal words that indicate His Essence or attributes, and their grammatical construction in the poem emphasizes their eternity through rhetorical figures.

The Dual Usage of 'Quran' and 'Kalam Allah'
Clarifies the dual usage of 'Quran' and 'Kalam Allah' for both mental and verbal speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that both 'Quran' and 'Kalam Allah' can refer to either the eternal attribute of divine speech (al-nafsī) or the recited words (al-lafẓī), and how the common usage differs between the two terms.

Core Idea
'Quran' is mostly verbal; 'Kalam Allah' is mostly mental—but both apply to the other.

This passage addresses a subtle but crucial point of Islamic theology: the dual reference of 'Quran' and 'Kalam Allah'. The commentator explains that the Quran is interpreted as 'His speech' to clarify that it is not merely a recited text but also an eternal divine attribute. Understanding this distinction prevents a misunderstanding about the nature of revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the translation to grasp the literal meaning.
Pay attention to the terms al-nafsī and al-lafẓī.
2
Identify the two key terms
Note that al-nafsī refers to the eternal mental attribute of speech in Allah, while al-lafẓī refers to the verbal expression in created letters and sounds.
3
Contrast the common usages
Observe that 'Quran' is more commonly used for the verbal form, while 'Kalam Allah' is more commonly used for the mental form. Consider why this distinction matters for theology.
This contrast helps prevent misunderstanding that the Quran is only a created text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : أَيْ : كَلامَهُ تفسير للقرآن ؛ فالمراد منه هنا كلامه تعالى، ولما كان الأكثر إطلاق القرآن على اللفظ المقروء، دفع توهم ذلك بتفسيره بكلامه تعالى، فالقرآنُ يطلقُ على كلِّ من النفسي واللفظي، والأكثر إطلاقه على اللفظي، وأمَّا كلام الله فيُطلقُ أيضاً على كلِّ من النفسي واللفظي، والأكثر إطلاقه على النفسي، وتقدم في مبحث الكلام زيادة، فارجع إليها إن شئت.
His saying: i.e., 'His speech' is an interpretation of 'the Quran'; so what is intended by it here is the speech of Allah, may He be exalted. And because the more common usage of 'the Quran' is for the recited word, He removed that misunderstanding by interpreting it as 'His speech, may He be exalted.' So 'the Quran' is applied to both the mental and the verbal, but mostly it is applied to the verbal. As for 'the speech of Allah,' it is also applied to both the mental and the verbal, but mostly it is applied to the mental. And there has preceded in the topic of speech additional material, so refer to it if you wish.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage provides a terminological clarification. The commentator explains the phrase 'His speech' (كلامه) as an interpretation of 'the Quran' in the verse. Since the term 'Quran' is most commonly used for the recited words (اللفظ المقروء), the verse's interpretation as 'His speech' removes the misunderstanding that the Quran is only the recited form. The author then explains that 'the Quran' can refer to both the mental speech (النفسي) — the eternal attribute of Allah — and the verbal speech (اللفظي) — the recited words. However, its more common usage is for the verbal aspect. Similarly, 'the speech of Allah' (كلام الله) is also applied to both, but its more common usage is for the mental aspect. The passage ends by referring back to an earlier discussion on speech for further details. This distinction is crucial for understanding the nature of the Quran: it is both the eternal attribute and the recited words.
